# THE FOURTH STEP

A Novel

# THE FOURTH STEP — Prologue

You are twelve years old.

The hallway is cold. The floorboards are cold under your bare feet and the chill threads through your soles and into your calves like wire pulled through a hem and your legs want to run but your legs are sewn to the floor. The window at the end of the hallway is open. The night is warm but the hallway is cold. The air moves through the window and past you and it smells like salt and eucalyptus and something else underneath, something metallic and sharp like a coin pressed flat against your tongue.

You hear your own heartbeat — a fist knocking from inside your ribs. You hear the house breathing around you — the creak of timber, the rattle of a windowpane somewhere, the particular silence of a building full of sleeping people, the silence that collects in hallways at two in the morning like dust settling on furniture. And then you hear a voice. Low. Female. Not yours. The words are not clear but the tone is. A command. Sharp. The kind of tone that makes your shoulders climb toward your ears and your breath freeze halfway down your throat like a swallowed stone.

Two figures at the end of the hallway.

The light is not on and the light is not off. A grey half-light from the open window that makes shapes without making details. One figure is tall. One is small. They are close to the window. They are close together. The tall one's back is to you. The small one is facing the glass.

The tall figure's arm moves.

Not fast. Not a swing. A push. Deliberate and controlled. The small figure stumbles backward. Arms reach out — not toward the tall figure but toward the window frame. Fingers splay. Nails catch wood. A sound, small and high, like a gasp cut in half. Like breath choosing a direction and choosing wrong.

Then the window is empty.

Not falling. Not jumping. Gone. Through the open window and into the dark beyond it. The air where the small figure stood is suddenly, completely empty. The window frame is bare wood and moonlight. The fingers are not there anymore.

The air changes. The salt and eucalyptus and metallic smell is gone and there is only the hollow a room makes when someone has just left it, and the hollow has a sound, and the sound is the absence of sound, and it is the loudest thing you have ever not heard.

You are at the window. You don't remember walking. You are standing at the window and you are looking down. The grass below is dark and wet. Something is on the grass. Something small. Something that was a figure and is now a shape and was a shape and is now a stillness that doesn't move and doesn't move and the not-moving is louder than any scream could be.

The tall figure is beside you. You didn't hear her approach. Her hand is on your shoulder. Warm. Firm. The warmth of a palm that has just done something terrible and has not yet stopped being warm. Her voice is close to your ear and she says words and the words are calm.

The window is empty. The grass is dark and wet and the salt air carries the silence away from the house and the silence will sit inside this girl for twenty-five years, locked in the place where words should be, and it will not loosen its grip until she stands in this hallway again, older, and the grip finally fails.

---

# THE FOURTH STEP — Chapter 1: Neve

## The Drive

I left Lily at the kitchen table with her homework. She looked up when I put my bag by the door. "Do you have to go this weekend?" Twelve years old and she already knows the tone that means the answer is yes, don't ask again. She gets it from me. I get it from my mother.

"Back Sunday night, I think," I said.

Liam was at the counter making sandwiches for her lunch. He looked at me over her head and I saw the question he wasn't asking. He stopped asking about my childhood years ago. He knows there's a room in me he can't enter and he has agreed, without ever saying so, not to knock. He married me anyway. I don't know if that's kindness or resignation. Probably both.

"She'll be fine," Liam said. He meant Lily. He always means Lily when he says "she'll be fine." Fine. The word follows me through my life like a dog that won't go home.

I kissed Lily's forehead. She's the same height I was at her age. The same brown hair, the same watchful eyes that take in more than they let on. Looking at her is like looking into a mirror that reflects backward instead of forward. I held her a moment too long and she pulled away with the embarrassed shrug of a twelve-year-old who doesn't want to be babied.

"Love you," I said.

"Love you too, Mum. Drive safe."

I drove south. Out of Melbourne, past the sprawling edge of the city where the houses thin and the paddocks begin, onto the freeway that cuts down the Mornington Peninsula toward the sea. The GPS says an hour and eleven minutes. I know the way without it.

I should turn it off. I don't. The voice — calm, female, Australian — tells me to take the next exit, and I follow her instructions even though I've taken this turn a hundred times before I was thirteen. My hands are on the wheel at ten and two, the way my mother taught me. My mother taught me everything. How to hold a knife. How to fold a shirt. How to answer a question without answering it.

The reunion was Saskia's idea. At least, the email came from Saskia's address — a weekend at the house in Sorrento, the five of us, after twenty-five years. I didn't want to go. I told Liam it was a bad idea and he said "Then don't go," which is the logical response, the healthy response, the response I would give a client. Don't do the thing that frightens you unless there's a reason. I didn't have a reason. I couldn't name one. But the email arrived and my hands shook when I read it and I said yes before I'd decided, which means some part of me decided before I did.

I've spent fifteen years sitting across from people in carefully arranged rooms, helping them face the things they're afraid of. Families torn by divorce. Children who won't speak. Parents who sit on opposite ends of the sofa and look at me instead of each other. I'm good at it. I'm very good at helping other people map their patterns. My own patterns I keep in a locked room at the back of my mind, behind a door I have never once opened. I know it's there. I built it myself. That's the thing about denial — it requires tremendous skill to maintain. My clients think they're bad at avoidance. They're amateurs. I'm a professional.

My phone buzzes on the passenger seat. Mum. I answer on the second ring.

"How far are you?"

"About forty minutes."

"You don't have to do this, sweetheart."

"I know."

"Sometimes it's better to leave things where they are."

In the background I hear the murmur of the hospice — a television playing something nobody is watching, someone's call bell drilling through the corridor, the particular acoustic of a building designed for people who are checking out one by one. Mum has been there three weeks. Pancreatic cancer. Stage four. She told me herself, sitting in her kitchen with a cup of tea, as calm as if she were reading the weather. "It's treatable but not curable," she said. Only my mother could deliver her own death sentence in the passive voice.

"So — you're comfortable?" I ask.

"Don't worry about me. Worry about you."

"I'm not worried about me, Mum. I'm fine."

A pause. The hospice sounds are very far away and very close at the same time. "You were always the brave one, Neve. But brave isn't the same as ready."

I don't know what ready would feel like. I don't know what I'm going back to. A house. Four women I haven't spoken to in twenty-five years. A weekend that was — that was a long time ago. I was a child. I don't remember much about that holiday. I was twelve. I slept through most of it, apparently. I was always falling asleep in cars, in the afternoons, on the couch. My mother says I slept through the whole holiday.

"Neve? Are you still there?"

"I'm here, Mum."

"You'll call me when you get there?"

"Yes. I think so."

"And if it gets to be too much —"

"Mum. I'm fine."

I say it the way I always say it. The way she taught me to say it. Fine. Such a useful word. It means nothing and it means everything and it means stop asking. She hears it the way I mean it — stop asking — and she stops. The phone goes quiet. The GPS talks. The road opens and the sea appears on my right, grey and flat under a winter sky.

The last time I drove this road I was twelve and the sea was blue and my mother was driving and I was in the back seat with my best — I was in the back seat. I don't remember who was in the back seat with me. I don't remember the drive at all, actually. I was probably asleep. I was always asleep. My mother says I slept through the whole holiday. She says it the way she says everything — with certainty, with authority, as if she is reading from a record that cannot be disputed. And because I don't remember otherwise, I believe her. I have always believed her.

Something lifts in my chest. Not a memory. Not quite. A sensation — salt air and creaking floorboards and light, golden light, the kind of light that makes everything look like it's glowing from the inside. A holiday. A summer holiday. I was happy. I think I was happy. The sensation is warm and full and then it's gone, replaced by something else, something tight behind my sternum that I can't name.

Anticipation. That's what it is. The body can't tell it from anxiety. They produce the same physiological response: elevated heart rate, shallow breathing, muscle tension. I learned this in my first year of training. I've taught it to a hundred clients. The mechanism doesn't change just because it's happening to me.

The GPS says eleven minutes. My hands are shaking on the wheel. I notice this the way I notice things in my clients — from a distance, clinically, as if the hands belong to someone else. Essential tremor. Caffeine. Low blood sugar. I haven't eaten since breakfast. That's all this is. I know what hypoglycemia looks like. I know what anxiety looks like. This isn't anxiety. I'm not anxious. I'm fine.

I can smell the sea now — salt and something else, something underneath, something I can't name that makes my throat close like a hand around a pipe. My chest tightens. I tell myself it's the heating. The car is too warm. I turn the heating off.

The GPS says seven minutes. The road narrows. The houses begin — holiday homes, weekenders, painted in the soft coastal palette of blue and white and grey. I know this road. I don't remember learning it but I know it the way I know the lyrics to a song I can't remember hearing. Left at the war memorial. Right past the newsagent. Straight through the roundabout. The GPS confirms what my hands already know.

I see the house before the GPS tells me to turn.

Different colour — it was white, now it's a blue-grey. The porch is wider. The garden gate is new. The front garden has been landscaped — native grasses, stone paths, the orderly minimalism of a property styled for rental. But the roofline is the same. The angle of the eaves. The position of the upstairs windows. The shape of it against the sky is the same and my grip tightens on the wheel and the car is too close and I'm not ready and —

I pull into the driveway and stop the car.

I sit for a long time.

The house is just a house. It's been renovated. Someone has lived here, changed it, painted it, made it theirs. It's a holiday rental now. We're renting it for the weekend — Saskia arranged it, or said she did. It belongs to strangers. It's just walls and a roof and windows and a floor.

A band of pressure wraps around my ribs like a blood-pressure cuff inflating past the point of comfort. My lungs can't expand. I can't catch my breath.

I sit in the driver's seat and I breathe the way I teach my clients to breathe. Four in, seven hold, eight out. Four in, seven hold, eight out. The breath goes in and the breath goes out and the house stays where it is. You are safe. You are in control. The past is not happening now. The past is a story we tell ourselves, and the story can be rewritten. I believe this. I have built my career on this belief. The house is just a house.

My hands are still shaking. I press them flat against the wheel and hold them there until the trembling stops.

Five minutes. I count them on the dashboard clock. Five minutes of breathing and not looking at the upstairs windows and telling myself the things I tell people in my office every day. The mind cannot distinguish between a real threat and a perceived threat. The amygdala responds to both identically. What you're feeling is not a memory. It's a physiological response to a stimulus. Name it and it loses power.

It doesn't lose power.

My phone buzzes. A text from Mum: "You don't have to do this, sweetheart."

I text back: "I know. I'm already here."

I don't get out of the car. I sit and I look at the house and I wait for something — a feeling, a memory, a reason to turn around. Nothing comes. The house is just a house. It's painted a different colour. The porch is wider. The garden gate is new. The roofline is the same but that doesn't mean anything. Houses have rooflines. All houses have rooflines.

I get out of the car.

My legs are shaking — a fine tremor in the quadriceps that I can feel but refuse to look at, as if not seeing it might make it not real. I lock the door and stand in the driveway and the sea air hits me and I smell — I smell something I can't — salt and wood and underneath it a sweetness, a warmth, like sun on old timber. The house is just a house. It's just a house.

I lift my bag from the back seat and the weight of it grounds me, gives my hands something to hold that isn't the steering wheel, isn't the door handle, isn't my own wrist. I walk toward the front door. My knees want to buckle and I keep walking because my knees do not get a vote. I have helped hundreds of people walk toward the things they're afraid of. I know how this works. One foot. Then the other. The fear is information, not instruction. You don't have to obey it. You just have to keep moving.

The path to the front door is concrete now. It was brick. I know this without knowing how I know it. The sound of brick dust under shoes — small, shifting, scraping — I can hear it. I can hear it in my feet. The path is concrete but my body feels brick and my eyes see concrete and my feet say brick and I don't know which one to trust.

The front step is the same. One step up, wide, made of bluestone. I step onto it and the width is right and the height is right and my foot knows the placement the way a hand knows the shape of a childhood banister.

The front door is new. I knock.

Saskia opens the door.

She looks exactly the same — taller, sharper, older, but the same. Same dark eyes that miss nothing. Same short hair, same precise posture, same way of standing with her weight distributed evenly as if she might need to move quickly. She's wearing a pressed shirt at a weekend house. Of course she is.

"Neve." She says my name like she's verifying it. Like it's evidence she's cataloguing.

"Saskia."

We don't hug. We were never huggers, either of us. She steps aside and I see the hallway behind her and my breath stops.

The hallway is different — wider, lighter, renovated. Grey walls where there were — where there were — I don't know what colour the walls were. I don't remember. The hallway is different. Everything is different. New paint, new furniture, new everything. But the floorboards.

I step inside. The floorboards creak under my feet.

The same pitch. The same groan. Low and resonant, the way old timber speaks when weight finds its soft spots — a syllable the house has been rehearsing for twenty-five years, waiting for someone to walk across it again. The sound rises through my shoes and into my arches and up through my shins and into my chest and something in my chest recognizes it. My body remembers. My body knows this sound the way my feet knew brick, the way my foot knew the bluestone step. The floorboards creak with the exact note they creaked with twenty-five years ago and I know this because my body tells me and my body is not confused.

"You okay?" Saskia is watching me. Her eyes miss nothing. They never did.

"Fine," I say. "Just tired from the drive."

The word sits between us like it always does. Fine. The wall I build out of four letters. The answer that isn't an answer. The door I close between what I feel and what I say. Fine. Stop asking. Stop looking. It's fine.

I'm in the house. I'm standing in the hallway and the air is different here — warmer, thicker, carrying something underneath the smell of new paint. Something old. Salt and wood and the particular mustiness of a house that's been holding its breath between guests. Or maybe that's not what it is. Maybe there's something else underneath, something my body knows and my mind doesn't, something that was here before the paint and the carpet and the new furniture and will be here after all of it is gone. Something that has been waiting.

The hallway opens to the left where the living room used to be. Ahead, the staircase rises. I can feel it without looking at it. I can feel its presence the way you feel someone standing behind you — a pressure, a weight, a displacement of air that says something is there.

Saskia says something about the kitchen and I nod and I follow her voice and I keep my eyes on the floor and on the walls and on the back of her pressed shirt and I do not look at the staircase.

I don't look at the stairs. I don't look at the stairs. I don't look at the stairs.

---

# THE FOURTH STEP — Chapter 2: Saskia

## First Arrival

I arrived at four-seventeen. The rental agreement said check-in was four, but I don't like to be late. I don't like to be early, either. I like to be precise — the way a scalpel is precise, or a verdict, or the distance between what someone says and what their body does.

The key was in a lockbox. Four-digit code. I entered it on the first attempt because I memorized it when the confirmation email arrived eleven days ago. The lockbox was metal, powder-coated black, mounted at forty-five degrees on the doorframe. Not the original lockbox. Not the original doorframe, either. The doorframe is new — pine, dressed, painted white. The original was hardwood. I know this because I have photographs.

The door opens into a hallway. The hallway is five meters long. I measured it this afternoon with a tape measure I brought from Sydney. The original hallway, according to the 1999 floor plan I obtained from the Mornington Peninsula Shire records office, was five meters long. The renovation did not change the dimensions. Renovations rarely change load-bearing walls, and this hallway runs between two load-bearing walls. I noted this. I noted the width: one meter thirty-eight. The ceiling height: two meters sixty-four. The original ceiling was the same height. The floorboards are new — blackbutt, modern cut, machine-finished, laid in a straight pattern. The originals were Baltic pine, laid in a herringbone pattern. I know this because I have a photograph from a 1997 real estate listing that shows the hallway floor clearly.

I set my bag in the front bedroom. Ground floor. I chose it deliberately. The room has one window, facing east, with a clear sightline to the driveway and the front gate. The door opens into the hallway. From the bed, I can see both the window and the door. This is not a preference. This is a requirement. I have always slept this way.

I unpack with efficiency. Toiletry bag on the sink. Clothes in the drawer. Laptop on the small desk by the window. Then I remove the document sleeve from the false bottom of my suitcase. It's a rigid A4 sleeve, archival quality, acid-free. Inside: the police incident report, two pages, photocopied from the original at the Sorrento police station in 2006 when I was nineteen and my father's colleague — Sergeant Bill Tran, now retired — owed my father a favor. The autopsy summary, one page, obtained through a freedom-of-information request that took fourteen months. A handwritten timeline I compiled in 2004 and have updated twenty-three times since. A photograph of Kiera Doyle, aged twelve, taken at a school sports day. I found it in the archives of the Sorrento Primary School newsletter, Volume 12, Issue 4, Term 4, 1999. She is running. Her hair is behind her. She is laughing.

I don't look at the photograph. I put the sleeve in the desk drawer and lock it. The key goes into my trouser pocket. I haven't decided if I'll use the contents this weekend. I haven't decided what this weekend is. A fact-finding exercise. An opportunity. Twenty-five years of waiting for these women to be in the same room as this house and now they're coming and I don't know what I'll do when they arrive.

Yes, I do. I'll watch. That's what I do. I watch and I note and I cross-reference. I've spent my career reading people for deception. Every twitch, every hesitation, every word chosen and word avoided. I know what a lie looks like the way a mechanic knows the sound of a failing engine — not because I was taught, but because I have listened to enough of them to recognise the frequency.

I walk through the house. Methodically. Room by room.

The living room is at the end of the hallway. The renovation opened it up — the original had a dividing wall separating it from what was a dining area. Now it's one space. New kitchen along the north wall. Stone benchtop. The benchtop is granite, not marble. Two stools, not three. The stove is gas, five burner, Smeg. The original kitchen was along the east wall. It had an electric stove, four burner, Westinghouse. I know this because I remember it. I remember the layout of this kitchen when I was twelve and the window above the sink looked out onto the back garden and there was a calendar on the wall with a picture of a lighthouse and the date was December and the square for the fourteenth was empty.

The calendar is gone. The window above the sink is the same — same dimensions, same position, same aspect — but the glass is new. Double-glazed. The frame is new. I note all of this.

There are two exits from the living room. The front hallway and a back door leading to a deck. The deck is new. The back door is new — sliding glass, aluminum frame. The original was a wooden door with a window panel, painted green. I remember the green. It was chipping.

The back garden is visible from the sliding door. Stone wall at the boundary. The stone wall is the same — local limestone, mortared, approximately one meter forty high. I can see it clearly. The lawn is new turf, neatly kept. A path of flagstones leads from the deck to a gate in the wall. The gate is the same. White wooden gate. I remember the gate.

I turn from the sliding door and walk back through the hallway. I don't go upstairs yet. I'm methodical. Ground floor first.

The ground floor has four rooms: the front bedroom I've claimed, a second bedroom at the rear, a bathroom, and the open-plan living space. The bathroom is new — it was a closet under the stairs in the original layout. The renovation kept the stairs in place — same wall, same rise — but replaced the treads and bannister. The stairs are against the west wall, where they've always been.

This matters. The stairs have been renovated in place and the upper hallway is in the same place. The upper hallway connects two bedrooms and a bathroom. The window at the end of the upper hallway is — I don't go upstairs yet. Ground floor. Methodical.

I make coffee in the new kitchen. The kettle is electric. It takes two minutes and fifteen seconds to boil. I stand at the counter and wait. The house is quiet. It's a Friday afternoon in Sorrento in July and the holiday houses on this street are mostly empty and the only sound is the wind off the sea and the tick of the kettle and the creak of timber as the house adjusts to the temperature drop.

I take my coffee to the front bedroom. I sit on the bed with my back to the wall and my eyes on the window and the door. I drink the coffee. It's acceptable. Not good. The beans are pre-ground, supermarket grade, probably six weeks old. I note this because I note things. I note the color of the paint in this room — Dulux Natural White, or a near equivalent. I note the light fixture — single pendant, brass, with a linen shade. I note the absence of art on the walls. Rental property. No personality. No trace of the people who owned it before.

Except.

The floorboards in the hallway creak. Not the new blackbutt — the blackbutt is too well-laid, too flat, too dead. The creak comes from underneath. From the original subfloor. The house was built in 1971, renovated in 2008, renovated again in 2019. They replaced the surfaces but they didn't replace the bones. The bones are still here.

At five-twenty-two, a car pulls into the driveway. Blue. Mid-size sedan. Victorian plates. I'm standing at my bedroom window, curtain held back two centimeters. The car stops. The engine turns off. The driver doesn't get out.

I watch.

The driver is Neve Cassidy. I haven't seen her in twenty-five years but I recognize her immediately. Brown hair, tied back. Steady posture. The posture is wrong for someone who's nervous — too rigid, too held. She's performing calm. I've seen this performance in court a hundred times. The witness who has rehearsed composure so thoroughly that the mask itself becomes evidence of its opposite.

She sits in the car for five minutes. I time it. Five minutes and four seconds. She looks at the house. She doesn't get out. Her hands are on the wheel at ten and two and she is looking at the house and she is not getting out of the car.

She looks at her phone. I can see her right hand lift from the wheel, thumb moving across the screen. Texting. She puts the phone down. She sits for another two minutes. Then she opens the car door.

She stands in the driveway. The wind hits her. She takes a step toward the house. She stops. She starts again. She walks to the front door and knocks.

I open it.

She looks exactly the same. Taller than I remember — I was tall at twelve, but she's grown into her height. Same steady posture. Same watchful eyes. She's wearing a jumper and jeans and her hair is tied back and she looks like a woman arriving at a weekend house, nothing more, nothing unusual, nothing to see.

But she won't look at the stairs. She hasn't looked at them once. She came through the door and the stairs are directly to her right — west wall, impossible to miss — and she has not turned her head.

"Neve," I say.

"Saskia."

I step aside. She walks in. Her shoulders are rigid. She's breathing through her mouth. The floorboards creak under her feet — the subfloor, the original subfloor, the one that was here twenty-five years ago — and I see her flinch. A small flinch. Suppressed. Almost invisible. I see it.

"Five minutes in the driveway. You couldn't decide whether to come in?" I ask.

"Fine," she says. "Just tired from the drive."

I show her the house. I don't need to — she can see it herself — but I show her because I want to watch her move through it. The living room. The kitchen. She remarks on the changes. "It's completely different," she says. She says it twice. She says it the way people say things they're trying to believe.

She doesn't go upstairs. She doesn't even look at the stairs. When we pass the hallway, she angles her body away from the west wall, a quarter turn, just enough that the stairs are in her peripheral vision rather than her direct sightline. She doesn't know she's doing it. I know she's doing it.

I make more coffee. We sit in the living room and make conversation. She asks about my work. I tell her criminal defense, Sydney. She tells me she's a family therapist, Melbourne. We talk about the weather. The traffic. The drive. The coffee. We do not talk about why we are here. We do not talk about the house. We do not talk about the last time we were in a house that looked like this one.

At six-fifteen, she goes to her room. The downstairs bedroom. She doesn't say she chose it because it's downstairs. She says she likes the window. The window faces east. The morning light, she says. She likes the morning light.

I sit in the living room after she's gone. The house is quiet again. The wind is up. The sea is close — I can hear it when the wind is right, a low rhythmic sound, like breathing. December didn't sound like this. December was hot and still and the air was heavy with salt and eucalyptus and the sounds were cicadas and children and the clang of a boat bell somewhere in the harbor.

I was fourteen the second time I came to the Sorrento police station. The first time was the night Kiera died — all of us, the five girls, taken in a police van, sitting in the station with plastic cups of water and someone's mother's cardigan around our shoulders, the wool scratchy and smelling of lavender and someone else's washing powder. I don't remember much about that night. I remember the plastic cup sweating in my hand. I remember the fluorescent light buzzing like a trapped insect. I remember a policewoman's hand on my shoulder, gentle, professional, the way you touch a child you don't know how to comfort because comfort requires understanding and no one understood what had just happened.

But the second time I came alone. Two years later. Fourteen. I'd taken the bus from Rosebud, forty minutes, and I walked into the station and I asked to speak to someone about Kiera Doyle. The desk sergeant was a heavy man with a grey mustache and he looked at me the way adults look at children who are asking about things they shouldn't know about.

"It was an accident, love," he said. "Let it go."

"I'd like to see the incident report. Fourteenth of December, 1999. Kiera Doyle."

"There's no report for you to see. It was an accident. You were there. You know what happened."

I did know what happened. I knew that what they told us happened didn't match what I remembered. I knew that Kiera didn't fall. I knew that the way they described it — "she leaned out the window," "she was looking at the stars," "it was a warm night and the window was open" — was a story. Stories have a shape. Real events don't fit together that neatly.

I didn't let it go. I have never let it go.

I stand. I walk to the hallway. I climb the stairs.

The stairs are new. West wall. Fourteen steps, turned at the midpoint. The treads are the same blackbutt as the hallway floor. The bannister is steel and timber. New materials, but the original substructure remains beneath. The original stairs were narrower, steeper, with a wooden bannister worn smooth by hands. The fourth step creaked. I remember the fourth step. I remember stepping over it in the dark because I didn't want to wake anyone.

The upstairs hallway is five meters long. I don't need the tape measure. It's the same length. Same width. Same ceiling height. The walls are grey now — a pale, modern grey, Dulux, I'd guess Lonely Hour or similar. In 1999 they were cream. The floor is the same blackbutt. In 1999 it was carpet — blue, low pile, synthetic. The renovation stripped it back to the subfloor and laid the new boards on top. Underneath, the original boards are still there. The bones.

The hallway has two doors on the left, one on the right, and a window at the end. The window is what I came to see.

I stand in front of it.

The window is different. Smaller. Fixed-pane — no opening mechanism, no latch, no way to open it. The frame is white aluminum, modern, double-glazed. In 1999 the window was larger, timber-framed, with a top-hung casement that opened outward. It was open that night. December. Warm night. Salt air moving through the house.

I examine the frame. I run my fingers along the edge where the aluminum meets the wall. The new frame is recessed into the original opening, but the original opening is still there — behind the new frame, behind the plaster, the original timber lintel and sill are in place. The renovation covered the window, didn't replace it. They fixed the pane and reduced the size and sealed it shut and called it done.

I stand in the hallway. I look at the window. The new window. The smaller, sealed, different window.

The window is different. But the frame is the same. And the frame is what matters — the frame holds the shape of what happened here, the way a scar holds the shape of the wound that made it.

---

# THE FOURTH STEP — Chapter 3: Imogen

## Okay

I packed three bags, which is okay, it's a whole weekend, you need options, and anyway one of them is just food. The cooler bag is the big one, the insulated one from Costco that Greg says is "a bit much for a weekend" but Greg is not here and Greg does not understand that you cannot arrive at a weekend away with other women and not have food. That's not how it works. You bring food. You bring options. So I have: a container of my banana bread (the one with the chocolate chips, which is Sophie's favorite, though I'm not sure why I made Sophie's favorite when Sophie isn't coming), a box of Tim Tams, a bag of honey-soy almonds, and a wheel of Brie that I'll need to refrigerate within the hour. The Esky has an ice pack and it's July, not December, and the Brie should be okay, it should be okay, everything is okay.

The GPS says I'm eight minutes away. I turn off the podcast — something about decluttering — because I want to arrive calm and happy and normal. Normal. What a word. I'm a normal woman driving to a normal weekend with old friends in a holiday house and that is a completely normal thing to do.

The road opens up and there's the sea on my left, grey and flat, and the air changes even through the closed windows. Salt. You can always taste the salt in Sorrento — it coats your lips, it finds the gaps between your teeth, it gets into the fabric of everything. Sophie loves the beach. Sophie is ten and she's safe at home with Greg and Ethan and she is not here and she is okay. Sophie is okay. Everyone is okay.

The GPS says turn right. I turn right. The street is familiar in the way that places from childhood are familiar — not a memory exactly, more like a feeling your body has before your mind catches up. The houses are holiday houses, close together, weatherboard and brick, the kind of street that's busy in January and empty in July. A few cars in driveways. A dog barking somewhere. The quiet of a coastal town in winter.

And then I see the house.

It's blue-grey. It was white. The porch is wider. The garden gate is new. There's a blue sedan in the driveway — Neve's car — and I pull in next to it and turn off the engine and sit for a moment.

"Right," I say out loud, to nobody. "Here we go."

I get out. The wind is cold — July cold, the kind that finds the gaps in your cardigan and slides through them like a rumour through a schoolyard. I open the boot and take out my three bags and the cooler bag, which is heavier than I remember. I should have brought the wheeled one. Never mind.

I walk to the front door. The path is new — concrete, modern, clean lines. The original path was brick, old brick, with moss between the joints. I remember — no. I don't need to remember the path. The path is a path. It's a new path to a new door on a renovated house and that's all it is.

I knock. The door opens.

Saskia.

She looks exactly the same. Tall, sharp, short dark hair, those eyes that see everything and give nothing back. She's wearing a pressed shirt at a weekend away in a holiday house in July. A pressed shirt. Of course she is. I have a sudden flash of her at twelve, the same sharp eyes, the same precise posture, standing at the edge of every group, watching.

"Imogen." She says my name the way she says everything — like she's filing it.

"Saskia!" I say, and I mean it to be warm and it is warm, I am warm, I am a warm person. "Oh my goodness, look at you! You look exactly the same! How are you? How's Sydney? How's work? It's been too long, hasn't it? Way too long."

I'm talking too much. I know I'm talking too much. I can hear myself doing it — the words tumbling out, one after another, filling the space between us. But the alternative is silence, and silence in this hallway would be — would be not okay. I don't want silence in this hallway.

I step inside. The hallway is different. Wider. Lighter. The floor is new — some kind of blonde wood, modern, flat. The walls are white. The ceiling is white. It's bright and clean and renovated and it is completely different from the hallway I remember, which was darker and narrower and had blue carpet that was scratchy under bare feet and a coatrack by the door with too many jackets on it and a mirror that made everyone look shorter than they were.

"Look at this place!" I say. "Completely different! You wouldn't even recognize it, would you?"

I set the cooler bag down in the hallway. I keep the tote on my shoulder. I look around, and I mean it — I look at the walls and the floor and the ceiling and the light fixture and I say "completely different" and I want that to be true so much that I can feel it in my chest, a kind of pressure, like the pressure at the bottom of a swimming pool.

"Where's Neve?" I ask.

"Her room. Downstairs."

"Of course! I'll take whatever's left. I don't mind. Whatever's available is okay." I pick up the cooler bag. "Should I put this in the kitchen? I brought food — banana bread, almonds, Tim Tams, some Brie that needs refrigerating. It's nice to have options."

Saskia watches me. She's always watched me. Even at twelve, those dark eyes tracking everything.

I follow her to the kitchen. It's new — stone benchtop, white cabinets, modern fixtures. A lovely, modern, renovated kitchen and there is nothing about it that reminds me of the old kitchen at all. Nothing. The old kitchen had yellow tiles and a fridge that hummed and a calendar on the wall with a picture of a lighthouse.

There's a window above the sink. It's the same window. Same position, same size. The glass is new but the window is in the same place.

I set the cooler bag on the benchtop and start unpacking. The Brie goes in the fridge. The Tim Tams on the counter. The almonds next to them, lined up straight, the bag facing forward. My hands are busy and that's good because when my hands are busy my mind is busy and when my mind is busy it's not — it's not somewhere else.

"I'm making tea, okay? Tea for everyone? I don't know, I just need to — tea." I fill the kettle. Neve appears in the doorway — brown hair tied back, steady posture, watchful eyes.

"Imogen." She smiles. It's a real smile and a careful smile, both at once. "You look wonderful."

"So do you! Both of you! It's like no time's passed, isn't it? Except it has. Twenty-five years. I don't know where it all went." I laugh. The laugh is real. The laugh is too high. "Speaking of time passing — Sophie just got the lead in her school play! She's playing Dorothy. She's so brave. She gets up on that stage and she's fearless. She's ten and she's fearless."

I'm doing it again. Talking too much. Talking about Sophie because Sophie is safe and Sophie is alive and Sophie is ten and Sophie is not twelve. Sophie is two years younger than Kiera was. Two years. That's nothing. That's a heartbeat.

"The banana bread's still warm," I say. "Sophie helped make it — she cracked the eggs. She's getting so good." I pause. "She's so sensitive. I love that about her, but I worry about sensitive girls. The world isn't always kind to sensitive girls."

The kettle boils. I pour three cups. I stand in this renovated house with these two women I haven't seen in twenty-five years and I hold my tea and I think: this is okay. This is normal.

"I should call Greg," I say. "He's got Sophie and Ethan tonight. Sophie has dance class on Friday evenings — jazz, she's been doing it since she was six — and Greg always forgets to pack her dance bag. I texted him three times on the drive down. I know that's excessive. I just don't want her to be uncomfortable."

I look around the kitchen. "It really is completely different," I say. "Like someone took everything out and put a fresh tablecloth over the whole thing. You'd never know."

I mean it. I want it to be true. The surfaces are different — the benchtop, the cabinets, the floor. It's lighter, airier, modern. It's nothing like the kitchen I remember.

Except the window.

The window above the sink is in the same place. I can see the back garden through it. The garden.

I turn away from the window. I pick up my tea and say, "So! Should I make dinner? I brought pasta. I thought it would be nice to cook together, the way we — well, we didn't cook, did we? We ordered pizza and ate it on the floor in the living room and —"

The memory comes back, fast and warm. The living room. Five sleeping bags in a row. Pizza boxes open on the carpet. Kiera telling a ghost story with a torch under her chin. Rowan screaming. All of us screaming. Laughing so hard we couldn't breathe. Kiera was the best storyteller. She could make the room disappear and all that was left was her voice and the dark and the story.

We played Truth or Dare. Kiera's idea — it was always Kiera's idea. She dared Saskia to eat a spoonful of Vegemite, straight, and Saskia did it without flinching, and Kiera laughed, and Kiera's laugh was — Kiera laughs with her whole body. Throws her head back. Hair everywhere. Like the joke is the most wonderful thing that has ever happened to her.

Laughs. Present tense. I said laughs.

I correct myself, inside, where no one can hear. Laughed. Kiera laughed with her whole body.

We ate pizza and we played games and it was a good night. One of the best. That's what I remember — the laughing and the ghost stories and the sleeping bags and the warm, safe feeling of being surrounded by friends. That's all that happened. A good night. A normal sleepover.

And then — and then it was morning and we were all okay.

That's where the memory stops. That's where I always let it stop. The good parts. Kiera laughing. The torch under her chin. The pizza. The good night. That's all I need to remember. The rest is — it was an accident. She fell. The window was open. It was summer. She leaned out. That's what everyone said happened. That's what happened.

"Imogen?" Neve is looking at me.

"Sorry! I don't know where I went just then. Miles away. Thinking about Sophie's dance class, which — I know that sounds stupid, she's okay, she's okay." I laugh. Too high again. "I'm always thinking about Sophie."

I busy myself with the tea things. I rearrange the mugs. I wipe a spot of water from the benchtop. I straighten the tea towel. My hands need to move. When my hands move, the rest of me stays in place.

"Rowan's not here yet?" I ask. "And who else is coming — Cate, was it? I don't remember a Cate."

Saskia says Cate is coming in the morning. Rowan by seven. I nod and drink my tea and feel the pressure in my chest, the underwater feeling, something pressing against the inside of my skin.

I don't go upstairs. The thought doesn't occur to me. When Saskia offers to show me the upstairs, I say "Oh, I'll explore later, I'm sure it's just as lovely as down here," and I turn back to the counter and start slicing the banana bread. I don't think about why I said no. I don't think about the fact that the upstairs is somewhere I haven't been in twenty-five years. The upstairs is upstairs. I'll see it later. Or I won't. What matters is the banana bread, which I am slicing into even pieces, because running out of things is not okay.

I arrange the slices on a plate and carry them to the living room. Saskia and Neve are on the couch. I sit in the armchair — the one facing the hallway, not the window — and tuck my feet under me and smile.

"It's so good to see you both," I say. "This is nice. A weekend away with old friends. Completely normal."

The room is quiet for a moment. Wind outside. The creak of the house.

"Completely normal," I say again, softer, like I'm trying to convince the room.

I pick up a slice of banana bread. I don't eat it. I hold it in both hands the way Sophie holds her toast, like it's something precious, and I think: Sophie is safe at home and Kiera is — Kiera fell. It was an accident. The window was open. She leaned out. She fell.

It was an accident.

I take a bite of the banana bread. I chew. I swallow. I smile.

Later, after Rowan arrives — thin, angular, long dark hair, a silver ring on her right hand I don't recognize — and after we've eaten dinner and drunk wine and made conversation that circles around everything important like planets orbiting a sun they can't look at directly, I find myself in the kitchen alone. The others have gone to bed, or are pretending to. I'm putting the leftover Brie in the fridge and rinsing the wine glasses and wiping down the benchtop because I can't leave a kitchen messy, I physically cannot do it, my body won't let me, the same way my body won't let me look at the upstairs hallway or think about December or say Kiera's name in past tense.

I stand at the sink. I run the water. I look out the window.

The back garden is dark. Winter dark. The moon is behind clouds. But I can see the shape of it — the stone wall, the lawn, the flagstone path, the white gate. The stone wall is limestone, mortared, about chest-height on an adult. The flagstones are uneven — I remember them being uneven. The white gate is the same.

I look at the garden and the garden looks back at me and for a moment, just a moment, it's not winter, it's summer, and the grass is green and the gate is open and there are ambulance lights on the stone wall, red and blue, red and blue, washing over the limestone, and someone is screaming and —

"The garden's the same," I say.

The words come out before I can stop them. I hear my own voice in the empty kitchen and it sounds wrong. It sounds like a confession.

I turn away from the window. Quickly. I dry my hands on the tea towel. I turn off the light. I go to bed.

I do not look at the window again.

---

# THE FOURTH STEP — Chapter 4: Rowan

## Arrival

The bus from Melbourne pulls in at seven. I am the last one off. It exhales and pulls away and I am standing on the street with my camera bag over my left shoulder and my small backpack over my right and the evening light is turning the rooftops amber — the color of honey held up to a window, the color of a memory developing in a darkroom. The light is already changing. Light always is.

I see the house.

It is not white anymore. It is the color of storm clouds — blue-grey, soft, weathered. The porch is wider than I remember. Or perhaps the porch was always this wide and I was smaller. The roofline is the same. The roofline is exactly the same and something in my chest shifts when I see it, a physical sensation, like a book falling off a shelf in another room.

I lift the camera before I decide to lift it. My hands know. They always know. I frame the house — roofline against amber sky, the wide porch, the front door that was green and is now black — and I take the shot. The shutter clicks. The sound of a moment being removed from time and held still.

I take another. The window on the second floor. Smaller. Replaced. But the wall around it is the same. The texture of the render, the way the evening light catches the corner of the sill. The shutter clicks. The light is going. I should go inside.

I walk up the path. The garden gate is new — wrought iron, painted black. The path is concrete now — smooth, modern, clean lines. It was brick once, uneven, moss growing between the cracks. My body adjusts for the dip near the third stone without being told — muscle memory for a surface that isn't there anymore.

I stop at the front door. My hand is raised to knock and I am looking at my own knuckles — thin, pale, the silver ring on my right hand catching the last of the light — and I think: I have been here before. Not the thought. The sensation. Like a tuning fork struck twenty-five years ago, still vibrating at a frequency just below hearing.

I knock.

Footsteps inside. The door opens and Saskia is standing there — tall, sharp, short dark hair, eyes the kind of dark that absorbs light and gives nothing back. Pressed white shirt at a weekend house. Sleeves rolled to exactly the same point on each forearm.

"Rowan."

"Saskia."

She steps aside. I step in.

The hallway opens in front of me and the light is different — interior light, warm, yellow, a pendant lamp that wasn't there before — but the space is the same. The width. The ceiling height. The way sound behaves, slightly held, as if the walls are cupping noise like water. My gaze finds the staircase. It goes up. Turns at a right angle. The bannister is new but the geometry is identical. Ten steps to the turn. Then four more. Then the hallway at the top.

I do not go up.

"Only one light on. Where is everyone?" I ask.

"Living room." Saskia gestures left. Her eyes move to my camera bag, then my face, then back. She is cataloguing. She has always catalogued — filing people the way I file light, by angle and temperature and exposure.

I walk past her into the living room. Warm. New furniture — leather sofas, a low wooden table, a rug the color of dried wheat. Imogen stands by the window with a glass of wine. Neve sits on the edge of the sofa with her hands wrapped around a mug. They both look up and for a moment nobody moves.

The stillness of a photograph. The four of us in the frame. Imogen by the window, light behind her, soft and rounded, hair lighter than it was. Neve on the sofa, shoulders slightly raised, her eyes — brown, steady, watchful — finding me and holding. Saskia behind me in the doorway, her shadow falling across the floorboards.

I see surfaces. I see light. The way the lamp catches the edge of Imogen's jaw and turns it gold. The way Neve's fingers press into the ceramic and the whites of her knuckles show. The way Saskia stands at an angle that lets her see every exit. I see all of this as light and form and texture. I do not see what they are feeling. I never do.

"Rowan." Imogen sets her wine down and wraps her arms around me. She smells like vanilla and fabric softener. Her cardigan is cream-colored, a small stain on the left cuff. She touches people gently, as if the world is fragile and she is trying not to break it.

"You look wonderful," she says, pulling back. Her eyes move over my face. "A bit thin. Are you eating?"

"Enough."

"You were always thin. Remember how thin you —" She stops. She has remembered something. Her smile flickers. She picks up her wine.

Neve stands. She doesn't hug me. She puts her hand on my arm and squeezes, brief and firm. "It's good to see you, Rowan." Her voice is warm and even and the warmth is real and the evenness is effort. I can see the effort. The muscular tension around her eyes. The way she is holding herself the way you hold a pose for a photograph — still, deliberate, waiting for the shutter.

"You too, Neve."

I sit down. I choose the chair with its back to the wall. From here I can see the living room, the doorway to the hall, and the staircase through it — the first five steps rising into shadow. The rest turn and disappear. I face them. I always face them. I do not know why.

Imogen pours me wine without asking. Red. It catches the light — garnet, deep, almost black at the edges. I hold it up. I look at it the way I look at everything.

"We're all here," Imogen says. Her smile is wide and warm and lives in her whole face — the smile of someone who has decided everything is fine. "Well, almost. Cate's coming tomorrow morning. Isn't that exciting?"

"Cate," I say.

"Cate Morrow. Don't you remember? A year or two behind us. Moved to London." Imogen settles into the sofa and tucks her feet under her. "I don't remember her at all, honestly. But Saskia says —"

"I don't remember her either," Saskia says from the doorway. She is leaning against the frame, arms crossed. Not joining. Observing.

"I barely remember any of that time," Neve says. She says it lightly. Conversationally. Her fingers tighten on her mug.

I don't say anything. I remember all of it. Not as a story. Not as a sequence of events with a beginning and middle and end. I remember it as sensation. The cold of the floorboards. The quality of the moonlight — how it was blue, not white, blue as milk in a glass. The sound of — 

The sound of —

Imogen is talking. Something about her daughter Sophie and the school play and how Greg was going to record it on his phone. Her hands move as she speaks. They never stop. She gestures, she traces the rim of her glass, she smooths the fabric of her cardigan over her knee. Her hands are small and pink-nailed and they perform a constant, restless choreography, as if she is knitting the air itself into something she can hold.

The conversation moves the way conversations move when people haven't been in the same room in twenty-five years. Carefully. In circles. Neve asks about Melbourne. Saskia asks about Sydney. Imogen asks about everywhere — Reykjavik, Lisbon, wherever I was last — and I say the names and they sound like postcards from someone else's life.

"Rowan." Imogen leans forward. Her eyes have found something. "That ring. That's a beautiful ring. Where did you get it?"

My hand moves to the silver ring on my right hand. The metal is cool. The band is thin. The stone is small — a moonstone, pale and opaque, the color of condensed fog. It has sat against my skin for twenty-five years, so long the finger beneath it is thinner than the others.

"It was given to me," I say. "The stone holds light."

Imogen waits. Eyebrows raised, head tilted, the expectant architecture of someone holding space for an answer.

I take a sip of wine. The waiting dissolves.

"That's lovely," Imogen says. She settles back, her fingers intertwining in her lap, knuckles whitening.

The evening fills with small talk. The wine goes down. The light outside the windows shifts from amber to grey to the blue of early night. Neve returns from the kitchen with a tray of food Imogen must have brought — cheese, crackers, grapes arranged in a precise spiral on a blue plate. Everything in its place. Everything ordered and placed just so.

I eat a cracker. I drink wine. I watch the room the way I watch everything — as a frame. The four of us arranged in our separate positions, each one a point of light in the composition. Saskia at the edge, angular, her sharp jawline catching the lamp. Imogen in the center, warm and rounded, her hands never stopping. Neve beside her, contained, her stillness louder than movement.

And me. Back to the wall. Facing the door. Facing the staircase I can see through the open doorway, the first five steps rising into shadow, the new bannister pale and smooth, the wall beside it painted a clean grey.

The hallway was cold. The hallway was cold and the light was — the light was blue, December-moonlight blue, not this warm yellow lamp-light but blue, thin, the color of — and there was a sound, somewhere between a gasp and a thud, a sound that entered through the soles of my feet and climbed my spine and —

"Rowan? Do you want more wine?"

Imogen is holding the bottle. The wine inside it is dark and still. The surface of it is flat. I look at the surface of the wine and I am back in the room, the living room, the warm yellow light, the clean grey paint, the present.

"No," I say. "Thank you."

Neve is watching me. Her gaze is steady. Saskia is watching me too — I can feel her attention the way you feel a camera lens, a concentrated point of observation.

The evening turns. Imogen says "completely different" and Neve says "mm" and Saskia says nothing and I look at the staircase through the doorway — the five visible steps rising into shadow, patient, unchanged in their geometry.

I should not have come here. I should have stayed in whatever city I was in last — was it Lisbon? Reykjavik? The cities blur. The light in each is distinct — Lisbon gold, Reykjavik grey-blue, Melbourne flat white — but the cities themselves blur into one continuous motion. I live in a continuous present. The past exists only as sensation. The future exists only as light I have not yet photographed.

But this house. This house is not past or present. This house is a photograph taken twenty-five years ago, sitting in the developer all this time, slowly becoming visible. And I am standing in it.

The conversation has stopped. I have been silent too long. Three women waiting for me to say something and I don't know what because I cannot read what is behind their faces — only the surfaces, the tension in Imogen's smile, the stillness of Neve's hands.

"I should get my things from the hall," I say.

"Your room is the one at the end of the upstairs corridor," Saskia says.

Upstairs.

I don't go upstairs. Not yet. I will. Later. When the house is quiet and the others are asleep and the light is — I will go upstairs later.

"I'll bring my bag up later," I say. "The light down here is enough."

The conversation resumes. Imogen talks about Sophie again. Neve nods, her eyes somewhere internal. Saskia pours herself water — she has switched from wine, controlled as always, every movement deliberate, like a photograph that knows it is being looked at.

I lean back in my chair. The wall is solid against my spine. The staircase through the doorway — beyond the turn, more steps, then the hallway, then the window, smaller now, fixed-pane, and the light was blue in December, blue as milk, blue as — 

From somewhere in the house a board creaks. The sound is specific — a pitch, a weight. Weight pressing timber that has been pressed in the same spot a thousand times. The sound is not new. The sound is twenty-five years old and it is happening now.

I am looking at the staircase.

"The fourth step still creaks," I say.

The room changes. I don't see it change but I feel it — a shift in pressure, the way a room feels when everyone stops breathing at once. I am looking at the staircase. The fourth step is just visible, pale wood in shadow.

Three women not speaking. Not moving. I turn and they are all looking at me. Imogen's wine glass frozen halfway to her mouth. Neve's hands still on her mug. Saskia's eyes wide.

The stillness holds. I look at their faces — the surfaces, the light — and I see surprise and something else I cannot name because I do not read the psychology of faces. I read the light on them. I do not understand why they are looking at me this way.

"Sorry?" Imogen says. Her voice is high. Too high. "What did you say?"

"The fourth step," I say. "It creaks. I heard it just now."

Saskia's eyes narrow. Not with suspicion — with something sharper. With attention.

"How would you know that?" Neve asks. Her voice is careful. Measured. The way you speak to someone who has said something that doesn't make sense.

I look at her. I look at the staircase. I look back at her.

I don't understand the question. I know the fourth step creaks the way I know the hallway upstairs is cold even though I have not been up there yet. The way I know light and texture without being told.

"I just know," I say.

The silence holds for another moment. Then Imogen laughs — a short, bright sound, too bright, a laugh that is trying very hard to be casual. "Well, old houses, right? They all creak. Every old house has a creaky step."

Neve is not laughing. Saskia is not laughing. They are looking at me and then at each other and the looking is the kind that happens when the frame has moved and no one knows what the new picture is yet.

I pick up my wine. The room returns to its arrangement — Imogen talking, Neve nodding, Saskia watching. But underneath, there is something new in the air. Something I cannot photograph. Something I cannot see.

I sit with my back to the wall and I face the staircase and I do not think about what any of it means because meaning is not something I do. I do light. I do surface. I do sensation. The rest — the story, the sequence, the before and after — that is someone else's territory.

The staircase waits in the doorway. And somewhere in the back of my mind, a hallway is cold and the light is blue and a sound I cannot name is still happening, has been happening for twenty-five years, a note that will not stop vibrating.

I do not go upstairs. Not yet.

---

# THE FOURTH STEP — Chapter 5: Neve

## The First Evening

Imogen makes pasta. She makes it the way she does everything — thoroughly, busily, with her hands in constant motion and a running commentary that fills the kitchen the way music fills a dentist's waiting room. Background sound. Comfort noise. The hiss of garlic hitting oil. The rolling bubble of the water. The soft tap of the wooden spoon against the pot rim. She moves around this kitchen as if she's known it for years, reaching for the colander without looking, finding the olive oil on the first try, opening the drawer with her hip while both hands are full. She has been in this kitchen for less than two hours.

"The renovation's lovely," she says. "Whoever did it had great taste. It's completely different, isn't it? Completely different."

She says it again. She has been saying it since she arrived. Completely different. As if repetition will make it true, or as if saying it aloud prevents anyone from disagreeing. The kitchen is different — new benchtops, new cabinets, modern fixtures where there were old ones. But the dimensions are the same. The window above the sink is in the same position. The stove is in the same place relative to the door. The bones are the same. I don't say this.

Saskia sits at the counter with a glass of water. She switched from wine an hour ago. Controlled as always. One glass with dinner, then water. She watches Imogen cook the way she watches everything — with precision and with purpose. Even at twelve she sat at the edge of the group, dark eyes tracking, cataloguing, filing. Now she's a criminal defense barrister and she watches people the way a radiographer watches an X-ray: looking for what shouldn't be there, the shadow on the lung, the fracture under the skin.

Rowan is in the armchair in the living room, camera bag at her feet, wine glass untouched on the table. She's looking at the wall. Not at the wall — at the light on the wall. The way the pendant lamp throws a gradient from warm yellow to deep shadow along the grey paint. Her eyes track the light the way most people track conversation. She's somewhere else — somewhere visual, somewhere sensory, somewhere that doesn't require words. She hasn't moved from that chair since she sat down after arriving.

The four of us arranged in separate positions. A configuration. A seating chart none of us are filling in.

Imogen calls us to the table. She's set four places — cloth napkins, wine glasses, a small vase with a sprig of rosemary she found in the garden. The pasta is penne with tomato and basil and it smells the way pasta smells — garlic and warmth and home. The table looks like a magazine spread. It looks like four women having a normal dinner in a normal holiday house on a normal winter evening.

"Lovely," Saskia says. A single word. She takes her seat with the precision of someone choosing a position in a courtroom. Back straight. Chair at a specific distance from the table. Full view of the room.

We sit. Four women around a table. The same number as before. One on each side, the surface between us like a negotiation table, like a peace treaty being signed by parties who haven't yet agreed on the terms of the war.

"Greg," Saskia says. She's not asking. She's opening a line of inquiry. "Tell us about Greg."

Imogen brightens — which shouldn't be possible because she's already at maximum brightness, but somehow she finds another gear. "Greg's wonderful. He's an accountant — I know, boring, right? But he's not boring, he's actually very funny once you get to know him. He does impressions. His Paul Hollywood is uncanny. Sophie tells him to stop but she's laughing when she says it."

"Sophie's your daughter?" Saskia asks. She knows. She's asking for the record.

"Ten. She just got the lead in the school play — Dorothy, in the Wizard of Oz. She's so brave. Gets up on that stage and she's fearless." Imogen's hands move as she speaks. She gestures, she traces the rim of her glass, she smooths the fabric of her cardigan over her knee. "And Ethan's seven. Obsessed with Minecraft. I don't understand it but I try. Greg says I hover. I don't hover. I'm attentive. There's a difference."

She doesn't stop. She doesn't breathe between sentences. The words are a wall she's building between herself and the silence. Every sentence is a brick. Every detail about Greg and Sophie and Ethan and the school play and Minecraft is a brick, and the wall gets higher and higher and the silence stays on the other side where it belongs. I watch her hands. They haven't stopped since she arrived. Keep talking, keep busy, keep moving, and the thing you're avoiding stays in the corner where you put it. It's effective in the short term. It's exhausting in the long term. I've seen it in a hundred clients.

I see it in Imogen.

Saskia eats precisely. Small bites. Measured chews. She holds her fork the European way, tines down. She drinks water between bites. She answers Imogen's questions about Sydney with brief, controlled responses. The firm. The cases. The harbour view from her apartment. She gives nothing away. She has always given nothing away. Even at twelve she was a locked room the rest of us stopped trying to open.

"And you, Neve?" Imogen turns to me. "Liam and — what's your daughter's name?"

"Lily."

"Lily! That's beautiful. How old is she?"

"Twelve."

Something shifts in my chest. A physical displacement. Twelve. Lily is twelve. I say the number flatly, the way you'd read it from a form, the way you'd state a statistic that has no personal meaning. Twelve. The same age I was when — the same age.

"Twelve! What a wonderful age. Sophie's ten but she acts twelve, she's so mature. Does Lily dance? Sophie's obsessed. Jazz and contemporary both. She's performing next month in the —"

"Imogen." Saskia says. Flat. A verbal full stop.

Imogen laughs. "Sorry! Sorry. I'm nervous. Can you tell I'm nervous? I'm not nervous. I'm just — it's been so long. Twenty-five years. That's a whole person. You could fit a whole person into twenty-five years."

She means a whole life. Kiera would be thirty-seven now. Kiera's life would fit into twenty-five years. She doesn't say it. The number sits on the table between us anyway, invisible and heavy.

I poured more wine. The red catches the overhead light — garnet, deep, almost black at the edges. I'm on my second glass and the edges are softening. Not the important edges. The ones I need to stay sharp — the ones that keep me upright and functional and fine. The other edges. The ones that noticed the floorboards creak and the smell underneath the new paint and the fact that the kitchen window is in the same position it was twenty-five years ago. Those edges can afford to soften. They're doing their work too well.

The smell. There it is again. Underneath the garlic and the basil and the detergent Imogen uses on the benchtop — underneath all of it, something else. Old wood. Salt air. The particular warmth of a house near the sea in summer, when the timber holds the day's heat and releases it slowly through the night. It's winter now. It's July. The house should smell like paint and cleaning products and nothing else. But underneath, underneath the renovation, underneath the new surfaces, the house smells the way it smelled. The way it smelled when I was twelve. The way it smelled when the air was warm and the windows were open and the salt came through the screens on the breeze and I was happy, I think, I was happy, I was —

The sensation dissolves. I take a breath. I pick up my fork. I eat.

I don't say anything about the smell.

"And what about you, Rowan?" Imogen directs the conversation like traffic, keeping it moving, preventing any one of us from sitting too long in the same place. "What's life like? Where are you living now?"

Rowan looks up from the light on the wall. "I was in Lisbon. Before that Reykjavik. Before that — I don't remember."

"You don't remember where you lived?"

"The order doesn't matter. The light matters. The light is different everywhere. Lisbon is gold. Reykjavik is blue-grey. Flat. Like steel."

"You're a photographer?" This from Saskia. Her voice carries a specific frequency — the one she uses when she's gathering information rather than making conversation.

"Freelance. I sell enough to live. I don't need much."

"You were always taking photos," Imogen says. "Even at twelve. Remember? You had that little disposable camera and you used the whole roll in one afternoon. Kiera was so —"

She stops. She doesn't finish the sentence.

The room holds. Four forks paused. Four breaths held.

And then Imogen does what Imogen does. She picks up the thread and ties it into something bright and safe and moves on. "You were always so creative, Rowan. Even then. We all were, in our way. Saskia was the logical one, Neve was the caretaker, I was the worrier. Isn't that funny? We had types. Like characters in a story."

"We weren't characters," Saskia says. "We were children."

"Same thing," Imogen says. "Children are characters. They haven't learned to be anything else yet."

The conversation turns to Cate. Saskia's phone buzzed during the main course and she glanced at the screen and said, "Cate's arriving tomorrow morning."

"Cate Morrow," Imogen says. "I've been trying and trying to place her. Was she in our year?"

"A year or two behind us," Saskia says.

"I don't remember her at all." Imogen shakes her head. "I feel terrible. Did we know her well?"

"Not well."

"I don't — I think I should remember her. I don't," I say. The name is unfamiliar. Cate Morrow. It sounds like a name from a different story, a different group, a different summer. I try to place her face among the faces from that time and nothing comes. The faces from that December have dissolved, most of them, except for the ones that haven't. The ones I could still see if I closed my eyes, which I don't.

"I'm sure she'll remind us who she was," Imogen says. She's stacking the empty plates, carrying them to the sink, running the water. "More the merrier, right? It'll be nice to have a new face. Freshen things up."

Imogen brings out the banana bread. She slices it with the serrated knife from the block — the right knife, the specific knife for the task, because Imogen would never use the wrong knife when the right one is available. She arranges the pieces on the plate and sets it in the center of the table and the gesture is so perfectly domestic, so precisely Imogen, that for a moment — one single, held breath of a moment — we could be any four women at any weekend house, eating dessert, drinking wine, being normal.

And then Imogen says it.

"Remember when Kiera used to make us laugh so hard we couldn't breathe?"

The name lands on the table like a held breath released.

One.

Two.

Three seconds.

No one speaks. No one moves. The ripples spread outward in every direction. The banana bread sits on the plate. The wine sits in the glasses. The house creaks — a low sound, timber settling, the sound houses make in winter when the temperature drops and the bones contract.

Three seconds is an eternity in a therapist's office. Three seconds is an eternity at a dinner table where four women are pretending to be something they are not. Three seconds is long enough for the silence to acquire weight, to become a fifth presence at the table, to fill the room with the thing that no one is saying.

No one mentions how she died. No one says the word "window." No one says "she fell" or "it was an accident" or "she leaned out" or any of the other phrases that have been waiting in the air since we arrived. No one says anything at all.

Then Imogen picks up her wine. "She was so funny. God, she was funny. Remember her impressions? She could do anyone. Teachers. Parents. That woman at the corner shop who always gave us extra lollies. She had this way of tilting her head and narrowing her eyes and suddenly she was someone else entirely. I still think about her sometimes."

"We all do," Saskia says.

Her voice is level. Her face is level. Her eyes are not level. Underneath the surface something is burning and I see it because I am trained to see it and I file it and I don't examine what it means because examining what it means would mean examining other things and those other things are behind a locked door I built myself.

Rowan hasn't moved. Her hand is on the ring — the silver ring, the moonstone, the one a friend gave her. Her eyes are on the wall. The light on the wall. She hasn't looked at any of us during the silence. She has looked at the light.

"We should do this more often," Imogen says. She means: let's be normal. Let's be four women who had a normal childhood and grew up and had normal lives and are having a normal dinner in a normal house. Let's not talk about the thing we're not talking about. Let's be fine.

I help clear the table. I wash and Imogen dries and the warm water runs over my hands and I focus on the physical sensation — the temperature, the pressure, the slip of detergent between my fingers. A grounding technique. Be in the body. Name five things you can see, four you can touch, three you can hear. The water. The plate. Imogen humming something tuneless. The window above the sink, which I do not look at. The back garden beyond the window, which I do not look at.

I am fine. This is fine. We are four women washing dishes.

Saskia says goodnight. She takes her water glass and her laptop and disappears into the front bedroom. The door closes. A lock turns. Of course she locks her door. Saskia would lock her door in a safe house.

Rowan drifts. She picks up her camera bag and stands in the hallway for a long moment, looking at the staircase. The first five steps visible through the doorway, rising into shadow. Then she climbs. The fourth step creaks — that low, resonant groan, that particular pitch — and she pauses and then continues. Her footsteps move along the upstairs hallway. A door opens. Closes. Silence.

I am alone with Imogen.

"Long day," she says. She wipes the benchtop for the third time. The cloth moves in small, precise circles. "We should sleep. Big day tomorrow. Cate's coming. I'll make pancakes. Do you think Cate likes pancakes?"

"She'll like what you make. I think. You always — people always like what you make."

"You're sweet." She stops wiping. Looks at me. For a moment the performance drops and what's underneath is tired and strained and much older than thirty-seven. "It's good to see you, Neve. I mean that."

"It's good to see you. I think it is."

She hugs me. Vanilla and garlic and dish soap. Her arms tighten — too long, too hard — and then she lets go and walks to the stairs and climbs them and the fourth step creaks under her weight and she disappears into the dark at the top.

The house settles around me. I stand in the hallway and I breathe and I think: I should go upstairs. I should see the upstairs. I am a grown woman and this is a renovated house and there is nothing up there that can hurt me.

I turn left. I walk to the downstairs bedroom. I close the door.

I don't think about why.

The room is small and white. A bed, a nightstand, a lamp, a window facing east with heavy curtains drawn. The carpet is new — grey, low pile, soft under my feet. The walls are Dulux Natural White, or near enough. Rental property. No personality. No trace of the people who lived here before. A blank room. A safe room. A room on the ground floor, at the back of the house, as far from the stairs as the floor plan allows.

I sit on the bed and I call Liam.

"Hey. How's it going?"

"Fine. The house is — fine. Renovated. Different."

"That's a lot of fines."

"I'm tired. Long drive."

"Is it weird? Seeing them after all this time?"

"I think so. Maybe. They're the same people but — I don't know. The surface is different."

"How's the house?"

"Different. You wouldn't recognize it."

I believe this when I say it. The house is different. It has been painted and renovated and filled with new furniture and new light fixtures and new carpet. It is different. It is completely different.

"Is Lily asleep?"

"Not yet. Reading. She says to tell you the main character in her book reminds her of you."

"Of me?"

"Observant. Watches everyone. Doesn't miss anything."

Something contracts in my chest. "I'll call tomorrow. Give her a kiss for me."

"Will do. Get some sleep, Neve."

"Goodnight."

I hang up. I hold the phone in both hands and stare at the wall and think: I should have brought a book. Something to fill the space between now and sleep. Something to fill the space between my ears where the smell of old wood and salt air is still lingering, underneath the paint, underneath the renovation, underneath everything new and clean and different.

I brush my teeth. I change into pyjamas. I get into bed. The sheets are stiff — rental property sheets, washed in industrial detergent. They smell like nothing. I'm grateful for nothing.

I turn off the lamp.

The dark is complete. No streetlights on this road — a coastal street in winter, holiday houses shuttered, no traffic. The only light is the faint glow of the moon through the gap in the curtains, a pale grey line that falls across the ceiling like a scratch.

I lie still and I listen to the house.

I've explained hypnagogic auditory sensitivity to enough clients to recognize it. The mind in the dark amplifies sound. The auditory cortex increases its gain in the absence of visual input. Every creak and tick and groan is physics — timber expanding and contracting with temperature, pipes cooling, the house adjusting to the cold the way a body adjusts to a change in air pressure. Normal. Neurological. Nothing more than that.

Floorboards creak in the hallway. Once. Twice. The sound of the house settling its weight. The renovation replaced the surface boards but not the subfloor — I noticed this when I came in. The new blackbutt on top, the old bones underneath. The bones remember.

I close my eyes.

The smell is here too. In the dark, without the visual override of white walls and grey carpet and modern fixtures, the smell is stronger. Old timber. Salt. The particular warmth of a house that held a summer night twenty-five years ago and has never quite let it go. The smell of that December. The smell of that — I was twelve. I was happy. I was happy and then I was asleep and then it was morning and everything was fine.

My mother says I slept through the whole holiday.

I breathe. Four in, seven hold, eight out. The breath goes in and the breath goes out and the house settles and the smell fades and I am almost asleep when the sound comes.

From upstairs.

The fourth step.

That specific pitch. That resonant, weighted groan. The sound of a particular piece of timber being pressed by a particular amount of weight in a particular spot. The sound I've heard twice tonight — Rowan climbing, Imogen climbing — except Rowan and Imogen are in their rooms. Saskia is in the downstairs front bedroom with her door locked. The stairs should be empty.

I hold my breath. It doesn't happen again. I tell myself I imagined it.

---

# THE FOURTH STEP — Chapter 6: Saskia

## Night Watch

The house is quiet by ten forty-three.
