I know this because I am sitting at the kitchen table with my laptop open and the clock on the screen reads 22:43 and the last sound from upstairs was a door closing forty minutes ago and since then nothing but wind and timber and the particular silence of a house full of people pretending to sleep — the silence that isn't silence at all but the collective held breath of five women lying in five beds listening to the same sounds and telling themselves different stories about what they mean.

I didn't come here to sleep. I came here to work.

The laptop screen is the only light in the kitchen. I've closed the blinds. The battery is at eighty-one percent. The file is labeled "KDOYLE_INCIDENT" and it is password-protected and it lives on a thumb drive in my toiletry bag and I have read it four hundred times. I read it again.

The report is two pages. Two pages for a girl's life. "The deceased, Kiera Anne Doyle, aged 12 years, was found at approximately 2120 hours on 14 December lying on the rear lawn of the premises, having apparently fallen from a second-floor window." Apparently. The word is doing a lot of work in that sentence. It's the kind of word police use when they haven't verified something but don't want to say they haven't verified it. Apparently. Reportedly. Allegedly. I use these words in court. I know what they cost.

"Minor bruising noted on the upper back and shoulders, bilaterally. Consistent with impact injuries from fall."

I was nineteen when I first understood that sentence. I was in the law library at Sydney Uni, third floor, the section on forensic pathology that no undergraduate ever visits. I'd been there for six hours, cross-referencing bruising patterns. Bilateral bruising on the upper back and shoulders. Two sides. Left and right. Symmetrical. The report says "consistent with impact injuries from fall" and that's technically possible — a body hitting the ground can produce bruising on the back. But bilateral bruising on the shoulders specifically? The shoulders are not primary impact points in a fall from height. The back, the pelvis, the head — these are primary. The shoulders are secondary at best. And bilateral means both sides, evenly distributed, which suggests force applied from behind, not force applied by the ground. Two hands. Gripping. Pressing into flesh. Ten fingers leaving marks that the attending officer noted and dismissed in the same breath.

I've spent twenty-four years trying to get someone to take that detail seriously.

I typed a timeline in 2004. I was fourteen. Two years after the night in question. I typed it on my mother's desktop computer, in her study, while she was at work. I printed it and put it in a manila folder and hid the folder under my mattress. I have updated that timeline twenty-three times since. The current version is on the screen in front of me.

The timeline begins at 1800 hours, 14 December. Arrival at the Sorrento house. Five girls: Neve Cassidy, Saskia Brennan, Imogen Hart, Rowan Thorne, Kiera Doyle. One adult: Elaine Cassidy, mother of Neve. Owner of the property.

1830. Pizza delivery. Ordered by Elaine Cassidy. Five girls ate in the living room. Elaine in the kitchen on the phone. I noted this at the time because I thought it was strange — an adult ordering pizza for children and then not eating with them. She said she had a headache. She said she was going to lie down.

1900–2100. Sleepover activities. Truth or Dare. Ghost stories. Music. The details here are fragmentary. My own memory provides some. Imogen's — if she were honest — would provide others. Rowan's would provide images but not sequences. Neve's would provide nothing. Neve has always claimed she doesn't remember the latter part of the evening. She was asleep, she says. She fell asleep on the couch. Her mother confirmed this.

2100. The approximate time the girls settled into sleeping bags. I remember this because I was the last one awake. I always was. Even at twelve I lay in the dark and listened. I heard — I heard the house. The creak of timber. The tick of pipes. The wind through a window that was open somewhere upstairs. Salt and eucalyptus in the air.

2120. Approximate time of the fall. The report says Kiera was found at 2120. The fall would have occurred minutes before. The report does not specify who found her or in what order the girls came upstairs. It notes that Elaine Cassidy "was alerted by a noise and descended from her bedroom." Her bedroom was on the ground floor, according to her statement. She said she had been in bed with a migraine.

But I was the first one up the stairs. I know this because I remember the staircase. I remember the fourth step creaking under my weight. I remember the hallway at the top — the carpet blue and thin, the walls cream, the window at the end open and warm air coming through. I remember standing in that hallway and looking toward the window and seeing —

I close the laptop.

I don't need to read my own timeline to know what I saw. I've carried it for twenty-four years. Two shapes at the end of the hallway. One tall. One small. The tall one moved. The small one — the small one was gone. And Neve was already standing in the hallway. Under the window. Looking at me. Her face wasn't surprised. It was already terrified. Like she'd been terrified for longer than the moment. Like the terror was established. Like it had been there before I arrived.

I told the police this. I was twelve and I told the police that Neve was already upstairs and the policewoman wrote something down and no one ever followed up. No one ever asked me again.

I open the laptop.

There are files on each of the women. I compiled them over the past two years, after the reunion email arrived. Professional discipline. You don't walk into a situation without knowing the players.

Neve Cassidy. Family therapist. Private practice, Melbourne. Married to Liam Cassidy, 40, high school teacher. One daughter, Lily, 12. Lives in Northcote, nineteen minutes from her mother's current address (Elaine Cassidy, hospice care, Caulfield). Daily phone contact with mother, based on my observation of her phone activity today. Neve has never discussed her childhood before age thirteen in any public forum. She has never given an interview. She has never written about trauma. She chose a career that requires her to help others access their memories while maintaining a perfect void in her own. This is either irony or architecture. I note both possibilities.

Imogen Hart. Primary school teacher. Year 6 — eleven- and twelve-year-olds. Perth. Married to Gregory Hart, 39, accountant. Two children: Sophie, 10, and Ethan, 7. Prescribed medication for anxiety — I don't know the specific drug, but I've observed the pill bottle in her toiletry bag. The label is turned inward. She doesn't want anyone to see it. She teaches twelve-year-olds. She has built her life around the supervision of children the age Kiera was when she died. This is not coincidence. This is compensation behavior. The minimizer constructs a world where twelve-year-olds are safe because she is the one keeping them safe.

Rowan Thorne. Freelance photographer. No fixed address. The last two years: Lisbon, Reykjavik, Melbourne, Perth, back to Melbourne. No permanent home. No partner. No children. Wears a silver ring on her right hand — moonstone, simple setting, child-sized. Not her ring. She didn't buy it. Someone gave it to her. A friend, she said. Rowan's narration is sensory, not narrative. She remembers what things looked like and felt like and sounded like but she cannot tell you what happened. This is not evasion. This is the way trauma stores itself when the hippocampus fails to consolidate the experience into a story. She has images, not events. She has light, not sequence.

And Cate Morrow. Arriving tomorrow. A year or two behind us. Moved to London. Lost touch. No one remembers her. I've tried to verify her. The digital footprint is thin — too thin for someone who supposedly lived in London for twenty years. A LinkedIn profile created three months ago. A Facebook account with twelve friends and no posts before January. No Instagram. No Twitter. No professional history that can be independently confirmed.

I've noted this. I've underlined it. I've put a question mark next to her name.

I close the file. I open the autopsy summary.

One page. Photocopied, grainy, the toner slightly uneven across the right margin. I obtained it through a freedom-of-information request that took fourteen months. The request was denied twice and granted on the third application, after I provided a legal justification that was technically sound and personally motivated and that no one at the coroner's office had the energy to dispute.

"Minor bruising on the upper back and shoulders, bilaterally. No fractures. Cause of death: blunt force trauma to the head consistent with fall from height. Manner of death: accidental."

Accidental. There it is again. The word that closes doors. The word that stops investigations. The word that turns a dead twelve-year-old into a statistic instead of a case.

I've shown this page to three forensic pathologists. Two said the bruising was "not inconsistent with a fall." One — Dr. Amira Hassan, University of Sydney, retired — said: "This is grip bruising. Five-point contact, bilateral. Someone held this child from behind. The question isn't whether she fell. The question is what happened before she fell."

I keep Dr. Hassan's email in the file. I've read it a hundred times. It's the only professional confirmation I've ever received and it lives in a folder on a thumb drive in my toiletry bag because the world does not want to hear that a twelve-year-old girl was pushed out of a window in a holiday house in Sorrento twenty-five years ago. The world wants accidents. The world wants closure. The world wants two pages of report and the word "apparently" and the word "accidental" and everyone moving on.

I have never moved on.

I take out the photograph. Kiera at the school sports day. Running. Hair behind her. Laughing. She is twelve and alive and the photograph is the only proof I have that she existed in motion, in joy, in the specific way that twelve-year-old girls exist when they haven't yet learned that the world can hurt them. She ran with her whole body. She laughed with every inch of herself. She was the most alive person in any room she entered.

I put the photograph back. I put the autopsy summary back. I lock the file. I close the laptop.

The kitchen is dark now. The only light is the glow of the laptop's sleep indicator, a slow white pulse, like a heartbeat. I sit in the dark and I think about the funeral.

It was December. A church in Sorrento — St. John's, weatherboard, white, the kind of church you see in small coastal towns and forget immediately. The pews were full. Kiera's parents in the front row — I remember her mother's face, not her father's. Her mother's face was the face of someone whose world has ended. Not crying. Beyond crying. A dry, hollow devastation — a face like a house after a fire, the walls still standing but everything inside reduced to ash and the ash still warm.

Aisling was there. Kiera's younger sister. She was eight. She sat between her parents and she didn't understand what was happening and she kept looking around the church with these wide, confused eyes and no one explained anything to her because no one could. How do you explain to an eight-year-old that her sister fell out of a window? You don't. You say it was an accident. You say it was quick. You say she didn't suffer. You say the things adults say when the truth is too large for the room.

And Elaine Cassidy. Front row. Arm around Neve. Composed.

That's the word I've carried for twenty-four years. Composed. Not grieving. Not devastated. Not dry-hollowed like Kiera's mother. Composed. The way you compose yourself before a meeting. The way you compose your face when you need it to say nothing. She sat in that pew with her arm around her daughter and her face was smooth and her eyes were dry and her posture was perfect and she looked like a woman attending a function she was required to attend. A principal at a school assembly. A duty. An obligation.

She doesn't look sad, I thought. I was fourteen and I was standing at the back of the church with my mother and I looked at Elaine Cassidy and I thought: She doesn't look sad. She looks tired.

Tired the way you look tired after a long night. Tired the way you look tired after you've been awake dealing with something you didn't expect to deal with. Tired the way you look tired when the adrenaline has worn off and the performance is over and the only thing left is exhaustion.

I was fourteen and I noticed this and I filed it and I have never unfilled it.

I stand. I pour a glass of water from the tap. I don't drink it. I stand at the sink and hold the glass and look at the dark window. The window above the sink. The same position as the original. The glass is new — double-glazed, aluminum frame — but the window is in the same place and the garden beyond it is the same garden. Stone wall. Flagstone path. White gate. The grass is winter-dormant, pale in the moonlight, flat, still. In December it was green and Kiera's body was on it and the ambulance lights turned the stone wall red and blue and red and blue.

I put the glass down. I turn from the window.

And I hear it.

Footsteps. Slow. Deliberate. From the upstairs hallway. The sound of weight on floorboards — not the stairs, the hallway. The movement of someone pacing, or walking, or standing and shifting from one foot to the other.

I go to the foot of the stairs.

The staircase rises in front of me. Fourteen steps, turned at the midpoint. The bottom half is visible — pale wood, steel bannister, the wall beside it painted grey. The upper half turns and disappears into darkness. The hallway at the top is not visible from where I stand.

I look up.

Dark.

The footsteps stop.

The house settles. A board groans somewhere to the left — the living room, perhaps, or the wall between the kitchen and the hall. The wind moves around the eaves. The sea, faint, rhythmic, far away.

I wait. Thirty seconds. Sixty. Nothing.

I could go up. I could climb these stairs and walk that hallway and find out who is awake and what they are doing. I could confront them in the dark, in the cold, in the hallway where Kiera fell. I could ask the questions I've been waiting twenty-four years to ask.

I don't go up. Not because I'm afraid. Because tonight is not the night. Tonight is observation. Tonight is data. Tonight is the gathering of information that will be used later, precisely, in a room where the lights are on and the questions are direct and the answers cannot be avoided.

I turn from the stairs. I go back to the kitchen. I sit at the table. I open the laptop. I add a note to the file.

22:47. Footsteps in upstairs hallway. Origin unclear. Duration: approximately twelve seconds. Stopped when I reached the foot of the stairs.

I save the file. I close the laptop.

The kitchen is dark. The house is dark. The wind blows and the sea breathes and somewhere above me, in the hallway where a twelve-year-old girl went through a window, someone is awake.

I sit in the dark and I think about Kiera. Not the report. Not the bruising. Not the timeline. Kiera. Running at the sports day. Laughing with her whole body. Twelve years old and the most alive person I've ever known.

Someone else is awake in this house. Someone else is thinking about that night.

---

# THE FOURTH STEP — Chapter 7: Imogen

## The Dream

I can't sleep.

I took something to help me sleep at half ten — just a herbal thing, something from the health food shop, Greg says it's placebo but it usually works — but I took it at half ten and it's now midnight and I'm still awake. Which is okay. It's okay. Sometimes it takes a while. It means I'm in a strange bed in a strange room, except it's not a strange room, is it? It's not strange at all.

I'm lying on my back with my eyes open and the room is dark and the ceiling is the same height.

I know because I'm measuring it with my eyes. I can't stop measuring it. The ceiling in our bedroom at home is higher. This ceiling is lower. This ceiling is the exact height of the ceiling in the room I slept in when I was twelve. The room with the yellow lamp and the floral bedspread and the window that faced east, toward the morning. My body knows the distance between my face and this plaster the way my hands know the weight of a mixing bowl.

I close my eyes.

Greg. I should think about Greg. Greg is at home with Sophie and Ethan and he texted at nine to say goodnight and Sophie had already brushed her teeth and Ethan wanted a second story. Greg is okay. Greg handles everything when I'm away, he's good at it, and I should stop worrying about whether Sophie's dance bag was packed because Sophie is ten, not two, and she can pack her own dance bag. Sophie being independent. Sophie being capable and brave and wonderful.

Sophie is ten and she is wonderful and she is safe and she is asleep in her bed at home and she is not here and she is not twelve.

I open my eyes.

The window faces east. I can see the shape of it against the dim grey of whatever light is getting through the curtains — not much, it's July, it's winter, the mornings are dark. But the window is in the same position. Same wall, same size, same orientation. The glass is new — double-glazed, modern, the kind of window you put in a renovated holiday rental to keep the heating bill down. But the rectangle of it is the same. The way the dim reaches the wall is the same.

Under the paint — there's new paint, you can smell it, a thin chemical sharpness that catches the back of your throat when you breathe — but under the paint there's something else. Old wood. Salt air. The particular smell of a house that's close to the sea, that has been close to the sea for a long time, that has absorbed salt into its bones. It's the same smell. Twenty-five years of paint and renovation and new carpet and it's the same smell and my body knows it and my mind doesn't want to.

Lesson plans. I should think about lesson plans. Next term is busy — the Gold Rush unit, the diorama project, the Sovereign Hill excursion — Sophie got the lead in the school play. She's playing Dorothy. She's so brave. She gets up on that stage and she's fearless and I don't know where she gets it because I was never brave, I was the quiet one, the one who sat at the back and colored neatly. Kiera was the brave one.

Kiera is brave. Brave and funny and — she fell. It was an accident. She leaned out the window and fell. That's what happened. Everyone said so. The police, my mum, the counselor at school. It was an accident. The window was open because it was summer and December and she leaned out too far. That happens. It's terrible and it's sad but it was an accident and accidents happen.

I should take another dose. I'm allowed to — the bottle says a half dose is okay if the first doesn't work within an hour. But I don't want to be foggy in the morning. I want to be bright and warm and normal, the way I was today. The banana bread was good. The dinner was nice. The wine — I shouldn't have had three glasses. The wine is why I can't sleep, interacting with the pill, that's all. A perfectly normal explanation for not sleeping in a holiday house in a room that —

The room I slept in when I was twelve had a single bed against the right wall. The bedspread was white with yellow flowers — daisies, I think, or maybe sunflowers. There was a bedside table with a lamp shaped like a mushroom. I chose that room because it was the smallest and the furthest from the stairs and because the window faced east and the morning light would wake me and I wanted to be awake early because —

Because why?

I don't remember why. I was twelve. I wanted to be awake early because I was twelve and it was a sleepover and sleepovers are exciting and I wanted to make the most of every minute. That's why. That's a perfectly normal reason.

But I'm lying here now and the ceiling is the same height and the window faces the same direction and the air smells the same under the new paint and I'm thinking about something I don't want to think about, which is the living room.

Not this living room. The old one. The one that was here before the renovation. The one with the cream walls and the blue carpet and the brown couch that was too soft and swallowed you when you sat down and the coffee table with the ring stain where someone had put a mug down without a coaster and the TV in the corner, a big boxy thing with a CRT screen and a remote that only worked if you pointed it exactly right.

I was standing in that living room.

I was standing in that living room at — I don't know what time. Late. After the sleeping bags. After the ghost stories. After we turned out the lights and the room went dark and someone said "goodnight" and someone else giggled and then the giggling stopped and the breathing became slow and even and one by one they fell asleep. But I didn't fall asleep. I never fell asleep at sleepovers. I was always the last one awake, lying in my sleeping bag, staring at the ceiling, listening to the others breathe. So I got up. I went to the kitchen to make hot chocolate because I couldn't sleep and hot chocolate helps, it always helps, warm milk and sugar and the ritual of it — filling the mug, putting it in the microwave, watching it turn, listening to the hum.

I was standing in the living room with the mug in both hands. It was the red mug — the house mug, the one that was already in the cupboard when we got there, the chunky one with the chip on the rim. The hot chocolate was too hot. I was blowing on it. The steam was rising and the room was dark except for the light from the kitchen behind me and the TV was — was the TV on? I think the TV was on. There was a movie. There was something on the screen, a woman's face, a close-up, and the volume was low, almost muted, the way you keep a TV in a house where people are sleeping.

And then I heard something.

From upstairs. A voice. A woman's voice. Sharp. Brief. The way you speak when you don't want to be heard but something has startled you and the words come out before you can catch them. One syllable, maybe two. A command or a warning or a name. I don't remember the words. I remember the sound of them — the edge, the sharpness, the way they cut through the quiet of the house like a knife through the dark.

Then the sound.

The sound that came after.

Not a voice. Not a word. A sound that was — it was between sounds. Between a gasp and a thud. Between something falling and something landing. Between a cry and a silence. A sound that started and stopped and started the way sounds start when the body making them doesn't get to finish and the air finishes instead.

The mug.

The mug slipped. Or I dropped it. Or it leapt out of my hands — I don't know how to describe it because I wasn't there anymore, I was somewhere else, the sound had taken me out of my body and the mug was on the floor and the hot chocolate was everywhere, all over the carpet, all over my bare feet, and the mug was in pieces, the red mug, the handle in one corner and the rim in another and the pieces were still settling, still skittering across the carpet, tiny ceramic fragments finding their resting places, and I was standing there with empty hands and hot feet and the sound was still inside me, not in my ears but in my chest, a vibration that wouldn't stop.

And then — 

I don't remember what happened after that.

I don't. I honestly don't. Someone came downstairs — one of the girls, or the adults, or the ambulance, or — I don't know. I don't remember. The next thing I remember is morning. Daylight. My mother's hand on my shoulder. "Time to go home, sweetheart." And the car, and the drive, and my mother's voice on the phone in the kitchen that night, low and serious, and me in my bed in my own room with the ceiling at the right height and the window facing the right direction and everything okay, everything okay, everything back to normal.

Because it was the TV.

It was the TV. That's what the voice was. The TV was on and there was a movie and the woman in the movie said something sharp and that's what I heard. The movie. I don't remember what movie. An action movie, maybe. Or a drama. Something with a woman's voice. The volume was low but I heard it because the house was quiet and everything sounds louder in a quiet house. That's what I heard. The TV. That's all it was.

And the sound after — the mug. The mug fell because I dropped it. I was tired and the mug was hot and I dropped it and it shattered and that's the sound I heard, my own mug on the carpet, not anything else. Of course that's what it was. I was standing in a dark living room at midnight holding a mug I was too tired to hold and I dropped it. That's the most normal thing in the world. People drop things when they're tired. Mugs shatter. Hot chocolate spills. These are just things that happen. Little accidents. That's all it was. An accident.

The word sits in my chest. An accident. It was an accident.

I sit up in bed. My heart drums against my ribs. I can feel it in my throat, in my wrists, behind my eyes. The room is dark. The ceiling is the same height. The window faces east. The air smells like old wood and salt and something underneath both that I can't name and don't want to.

I need water. I always bring a glass to bed at home — Greg teases me about it, "You and your water glass," and I say "Hydration is important, Gregory," and he laughs and that's our life, that's our normal life.

I get out of bed. The carpet is new — soft, deep pile. The carpet that was here when I was twelve was blue and flat and scratchy. I know this because my feet remember.

I open the bedroom door. The hallway is dark and quiet. The floorboards creak under my feet — the same pitch, the same groan, the house speaking in its sleep. The kitchen is to the left. The stairs are straight ahead. I turn left toward the kitchen. I find the light switch. The fluorescent tube above the benchtop flickers twice and holds, throwing the kitchen into that flat white light that makes everything look like a hospital.

I fill a glass from the tap. The water is cold. I drink half of it and the cold is grounding, real. I set the glass on the benchtop. I should go back to bed. I should turn off the light and think about lesson plans and Sophie's dance bag and Greg's sandwiches and all the normal things waiting for me at home in Perth where the ceilings are high and the air smells like our house, not this house, not this salt-and-wood smell that won't let me go.

But instead of turning left toward my room, I walk to the foot of the stairs.

I don't decide to do this. My feet carry me to the bottom of the staircase, and I stand there and I look up. The staircase rises into darkness. The new bannister is pale, catching a sliver of light from somewhere. The steps are in shadow. The fourth step — Rowan said it still creaks. The stairs are new, they shouldn't creak, but I heard something when Neve came downstairs for tea. That particular groan. That particular weight.

The hallway at the top of the stairs is invisible from here. Dark. The kind of dark that looks solid, with edges and a surface you could touch. The air at the top is colder, stiller, the air in a place where nothing moves.

I stand at the bottom of the stairs and I look up into the dark and I think about the voice I heard twenty-five years ago. Sharp. Brief. A woman's voice. From upstairs. Not a TV. Not a movie. Not a dream.

And I think, for the first time in twenty-five years, a thought I've never let myself think: *What if it wasn't an accident?*

I push the thought down so hard my hands shake and my jaw aches from clenching and the glass of water on the kitchen benchtop is waiting for me and I should not be standing at the bottom of these stairs thinking thoughts that are not okay and not okay and not anything I have permission to think.

I go back to bed. I drink the rest of the water. I lie down. I close my eyes. The ceiling is the same height. The window faces east. The air smells like salt and old wood and underneath it all, underneath the paint and the carpet and the years, something that was here before any of us and will be here after all of us are gone.

I don't sleep.

---

# THE FOURTH STEP — Chapter 8: Rowan

## Night Vision

The hallway is cold.

I don't remember coming upstairs. I was asleep — or something like sleep, the grey space between sleeping and waking where the body moves without the mind's permission, where the legs carry you and the hands open doors and the feet find the floor the way feet find the ground in the dark, by texture, by temperature, by the particular cold of timber that has been cold for a long time. I was asleep and now I am standing in the upstairs hallway and my feet are bare and the floorboards are cold against my soles and the cold is the same cold.

Exactly the same cold.

Twenty-five years of new paint and new carpet and renovation and insulation and double-glazed windows and the cold is the same. It rises through the floorboards and into the arches of my feet and up through my shins and settles in my knees like water pooling in the lowest point of a house. It is not a temperature. It is a presence. The cold lives here. The cold has always lived here, in this hallway, in this specific length of space between the top of the stairs and the window at the end, and it knows me and I know it and we are standing face to face in the dark.

My eyes adjust. The hallway is not black — it is never fully black, not in a house near the sea. There is moonlight. It comes through the window at the end of the hall, through the gap between the curtains that someone — the cleaning service, the last guests, the rental manager — didn't quite close. The light is grey and thin and falls on the carpet in a narrow blade, dividing the hallway into two equal halves. Left of the blade: dark. Right of the blade: less dark. The blade itself: silver, sharp, the color of the ring on my right hand.

I am standing in exactly the right place.

I know this because the space is measured in my body. The hallway is twelve steps long. I know this without counting. The ceiling is eight feet high. The width is four feet — wide enough for two people to pass, narrow enough to touch both walls if you extend your arms. These numbers are not memories. They are facts stored in my muscles, in my bones, in the way my body holds the architecture of rooms it has not entered in twenty-five years.

The window is smaller now. I can see it at the end of the hallway — the rectangle of grey light, the aluminum frame, the fixed pane. You can't open it. The window that is here now is not the window that was here then. The window that was here then was tall and wide and it opened outward on a brass hinge that creaked in the wind. December wind, warm, carrying salt and eucalyptus. The window was open that night. The night was warm and the window was open and the air moved through the house like breathing.

This window does not breathe. It is sealed. Aluminum and double glass and rubber seals and the air stays where it is. But the frame — behind the new frame, where the aluminum meets the wall — the original frame is still there. The bones of the house. You can renovate surfaces but you cannot renovate bones.

The moonlight through the new window is winter light. July light. It is blue-grey and thin and it doesn't reach the walls. December light was different. December light was gold. December light filled this hallway the way water fills a glass — from the bottom up, pooling against the carpet, rising until even the ceiling was gold, until the air itself seemed to glow, warm and dense and alive with the color of summer. I remember the light. I remember it the way I remember temperature and texture — as a physical fact, not an emotion. The light was gold. The air was warm. The window was open. These are facts. They do not require interpretation.

My camera is in my hands. I don't remember picking it up. It is there, weight familiar against my palms, the strap around my neck, the cold metal of the body against my fingers. My hands know what to do even when my mind doesn't. My hands have been doing this for twenty-five years — framing, focusing, pressing the shutter, taking moments out of time and holding them still. My hands lifted the camera. My hands brought it upstairs. My hands are already composing the shot.

I lift the camera to my eye. The viewfinder frames the hallway: the window at the end, the grey blade of moonlight on the carpet, the walls on either side, the ceiling above. The composition is symmetrical. The window is the vanishing point. The hallway is a corridor of shadow leading to a rectangle of grey. I press the shutter.

The flash fires.

The hallway erupts in white light — harsh, clinical, a flat white that strips the space of shadow and depth and renders everything in equal brightness. For one fraction of a second I see it all: the grey carpet, the white walls, the aluminum window frame, the fire extinguisher in its case on the left wall, the smoke detector on the ceiling, two doors on the left, one on the right, leading to bedrooms. For one fraction of a second the hallway is not a hallway but a photograph — a surface, a two-dimensional plane, a moment extracted from time.

Then the flash dies. The white collapses. The shadows return. The grey blade of moonlight falls on the carpet and the hallway is dark again and my eyes are full of afterimages, bright purple shapes swimming in the black.

I look at the screen on the back of the camera. The image glows: the hallway, frozen, lit by flash. Flat and bright and detailed. The window at the end. The walls on either side. The carpet. The doors. Everything visible. Nothing hidden.

I zoom in on the window. The aluminum frame is white and clean and modern. The glass is dark — it reflects the flash back at the camera, a bright white square in the center of the frame. But around the edges, where the aluminum meets the wall, where the new frame is screwed into the old structure, I can see the original wood. Dark, stained, old. The timber that was here before the aluminum, before the renovation, before everything changed.

And on the wood — something. Lines, maybe. Dark marks against the old timber. I zoom closer. The screen fills with grain and pixel and the image breaks apart. Too low resolution. The details won't resolve. Something is there, scratched into the old timber, but the camera can't reach it. I would need to see it properly — on a larger screen, with more light.

I lower the camera.

The hallway is dark. The cold is in my feet. The moonlight is grey and thin. I am standing in exactly the right place and I have been standing here for twenty-five years.

You are standing in the hallway. You are twelve. The carpet is blue and scratchy under your bare feet and the air is warm — December warm, summer warm, the kind of warm that makes you want to lie on the floor and feel the house breathing around you. The window at the end of the hallway is open. The brass hinge is creaking. The air comes through in slow pulses, salt and eucalyptus and something else, something metallic, something you don't have a word for because you are twelve and your vocabulary has not yet grown large enough for the taste of fear.

You hear a sound from below. From the stairs. No — from above. From the hallway. You are already in the hallway. You were always in the hallway. The sequence is wrong but the sensations are right: the warmth, the light, the creak of the hinge, the taste of metal on your tongue.

You look up.

The hallway is twelve steps long. At the end, in front of the window, there are two shapes. One is tall. Tall the way an adult is tall, tall the way your mother is tall, tall the way the world is tall when you are twelve and the ceiling is eight feet high and everything above you is larger than you. The tall shape is between you and the window. Its back is to you. It is blocking the light.

The other shape is small. Small the way you are small. Small the way Kiera is small — was small, is small, has always been small. The small shape is closer to the window. The moonlight touches its edges. The small shape is not moving.

The tall shape moves.

The arm of the tall shape lifts and extends and presses against the small shape and the small shape moves — backward, toward the window, toward the open window, toward the dark and the warm air and the brass hinge that is creaking — and the small shape reaches out. A hand. Small fingers splayed wide. Nails catching the wooden frame. Five parallel lines of pressure against old paint. The hand grips. The fingers curl. The knuckles whiten.

The tall shape moves again.

And the small shape — the small shape goes through the window.

Not fell. Not jumped. Not slipped or stumbled or lost her balance. Went. The way light goes through glass. The way sound goes through walls. The way a thing goes from being here to being not-here without any of the expected steps between. One moment the small shape is at the window, gripping the frame, fingers white, and the next moment the window is empty. The frame is empty. The hand is gone. The air where the shape was is just air. The space where the fingers gripped is just wood with scratches — five parallel lines, downward, the marks of someone holding on.

As if the air took her. As if the night reached in and pulled her through. As if the window was not a window but a door that opened in one direction only and the small shape went through it and did not come back.

I am back in the hallway. The cold is in my feet. The moonlight is grey. The window is sealed. I am thirty-seven and I am standing in the same spot and the cold is the same.

I go back to my room. My laptop is on the bed where I left it. I sit on the edge of the mattress and I open it and the screen fills my face with blue-white light. I insert the memory card. I open the photograph. The hallway, frozen. The flash, flat and bright. The window at the end. The walls on either side.

I zoom in.

Past the aluminum frame. Past the rubber seal. Past the new paint and the new plaster and the careful renovation that was supposed to erase everything that was here before. To the wood. The old wood. The bones of the house.

The scratches are there.

I zoom closer. Five parallel lines. The spacing is even — the width of a child's hand. The depth is consistent — the depth of fingernails pressing into soft timber. The direction is downward, top right to bottom left, the angle of a hand gripping from below, a body being pulled away from the frame, fingers holding on and losing their grip, nail by nail, the wood taking the last of the person's weight and then letting go.

I zoom until the image is nothing but grain and pixel and five lines in the wood. I zoom until the scratches are the only thing on the screen. I look at them. They are real. They are not a memory. They are not a sensation. They are not light or temperature or the ghost of a feeling that might or might not have happened. They are physical. They are present. They are here, in this house, under the new frame, waiting.

Someone held on. Someone's fingernails pressed into this wood with enough force to leave marks. Someone gripped this frame the way you grip something when letting go means falling. And someone lost their grip. The scratches go down. The motion is downward. The fingers slid. The hand opened. The body went through the window.

Went. The word again. The only word I have for what I saw. Not fell. Not jumped. Went. As if the English language does not contain a verb for what happened in this hallway twenty-five years ago, as if the thing that occurred is outside the range of words I know, as if I have been standing at the edge of a category that doesn't exist, looking at something my brain can see but cannot name.

I close the laptop.

The room is dark. The house is quiet. Somewhere below me, three women are sleeping or not sleeping in their beds, each one carrying her own version of this hallway, her own fragments of this cold, her own scratches in the wood that she may or may not have looked at, may or may not have let herself see.

I open the laptop again. I zoom in on the photograph. The scratches are real. Five parallel lines. Downward motion. Still here after twenty-five years of paint and renovation and denial.

Someone held on. Someone lost their grip.

---

# THE FOURTH STEP — Chapter 9: Neve

## Morning

I wake at five forty-three. The clock on the bedside table says so — white numerals on a black face, the kind of clock that doesn't apologize for the hour. The room is grey. Not the grey of morning but the grey of not-quite-morning, the pre-dawn stillness where the world hasn't decided what colour to be yet. I lie still and listen to the house.

Houses have rhythms. The heating clicking on and off. Pipes expanding and contracting. Timber adjusting to temperature. I know this because I have spent fifteen years sitting in rooms listening to the sounds people don't know they're making. The leg that won't stop bouncing under the table. The pen clicking during a difficult question. The silence that says more than the words. The house is quiet but not silent — it's making sounds, settling sounds, breathing sounds, the way all houses breathe. And underneath those sounds, underneath the heating and the pipes and the timber, there is something else. A frequency. A hum. A pressure in the air that sits on my chest like a hand.

I should assess this objectively. I'm a therapist. I know that trauma sensitizes the nervous system to threat cues, that returning to a trauma site produces physiological arousal without conscious recall. Basic conditioning. The body holds what the mind refuses to carry. I've explained this to a hundred clients. Understanding the mechanism does not stop it from working.

I get out of bed. The floor is cold. My feet find the rug and then the floorboards and then the rug again and the texture change registers somewhere in my brain as data, not memory. I dress in the dark — trackpants, jumper, thick socks. My hands know where the bag is, where the clothes are, where the door is. My hands are very capable. My hands do most of my living.

The hallway is empty. The house is empty, or the others are still asleep. The kitchen is at the end of the hallway, through the living room, and I walk toward it with my eyes on the floor and my shoulders level and my breathing even. I do not look at the stairs. I do not look at the stairs. I do not look at the stairs.

The kitchen is new. Stone benchtop, modern appliances, the anonymous competence of a property styled for transients. I find the coffee — a capsule machine — and press the button. The machine whirs and produces a thin stream of brown liquid into a white mug. Thick, white, handle wide enough for two fingers, unchipped, unremarkable. I wrap both hands around it. The heat grounds me. My hands are shaking. The tremor is bilateral, fine, present at rest. Essential tremor. Caffeine withdrawal. Low blood sugar. That's all.

I take the coffee to the back door. The sliding door is new — aluminum frame, double-glazed — but the view through it is not. The garden.

I step outside.

The morning is cold. July on the Mornington Peninsula — the kind of cold that doesn't bite so much as seep, finding the spaces between fabric and skin, settling there like a guest who has no intention of leaving. The air is salt and damp grass and something underneath, something the garden has been breathing for twenty-five years. The deck is new — treated pine, weathered to grey. The deck was not here before. Before, there was a wooden step down from the green door, and the path was —

The path is the same.

The flagstone path leads from the deck to the white gate in the stone wall. The stones are the same — local limestone, irregular shapes, fitted together with mortar that has been repointed but not replaced. The path curves slightly to the left before straightening toward the gate. The curve is the same. I can feel it in my feet before I see it. The grass is new turf — green, even, professionally maintained. But the shape of the lawn is the same. The rectangle. The edges. The position of the stone wall at the boundary, limestone, one meter forty high. The height of my chin when I was twelve.

The garden is the same. Renovated, replanted, but the bones are the same. The wall. The path. The proportions of enclosure. I could close my eyes and walk from the deck to the gate and my feet would know every stone, every shift in level, every groove in the mortar.

I walk to the middle of the lawn and I turn and I look back at the house.

The house is blue-grey. The porch is wider. But the shape of it — the roofline, the eaves, the position of the windows on the upper storey — the shape is the same. And from where I am standing, I can see the upstairs window.

The window at the end of the upstairs hallway.

It's smaller now. Fixed-pane. Sealed shut. White aluminum frame reflecting the grey morning light. But the position is the same — centre of the upper wall, above the back door, looking down on this garden, this lawn, this path, this stone wall. Looking down on me now the way it looked down on everything that happened here before.

Pressure builds behind my eyes. Not pain. Pressure. The sensation of something pushing from inside my skull outward, as if my optic nerves are being compressed by a fluid that has nowhere to go. I look at the window and the pressure increases. I look at the window and my hands tighten on the mug and my breath goes shallow and there is something — something behind the pressure, something my brain is trying to show me, something pressing against the inside of my forehead like a repressed memory forcing its way through the wall I built to contain it, the shape almost visible, the outline almost forming, the —

I look away.

I look at the stone wall. I look at the gate. I look at the grass beneath my feet. The pressure recedes. Not gone — never gone, just pulled back, like a wave retreating from sand that it will return to. But enough. Enough to breathe. Enough to think. Enough to tell myself what I always tell myself: the past is not happening now. The window is just a window. The house is just a house. I am a thirty-seven-year-old woman standing in a garden at six in the morning and nothing is wrong and everything is fine.

I drink the coffee. It's lukewarm. I drink it anyway. The routine of it — lift, sip, lower. A rhythm. A wall between myself and the window I am not looking at.

I stand in the garden for — I don't know how long. Time moves differently here, in discrete blocks without transitions, and when I look at my watch it is seven forty-five and the coffee is cold and I have been standing in the centre of the lawn looking at everything except the upstairs window for over an hour.

Voices inside. Imogen, I think — her warm, carrying voice, filling silence the way grout fills cracks. The smell of toast. The domestic sounds of a house with people in it. I should go in. I should be fine.

I stay in the garden. The garden is safer than the house. Flat and open and the sky is above me and the walls are at a distance and there is no staircase and no hallway and no window except the one I am not looking at.

At nine-fifteen, a car pulls into the driveway. I hear it from the garden — tyres on the drive, engine cutting, a door opening and closing. Footsteps on the front path. Then the front door opens and a voice says "Hello? Is anyone here?" and the voice is warm and bright and confident in the particular way of someone who has practiced being welcome.

I go inside.

She's standing in the hallway. Brown hair, shoulder length. Average height. Pleasant face — not striking, not forgettable, the kind of face designed to blend. She's wearing jeans and a navy jumper and flat shoes and she has a small overnight bag over one shoulder and she is smiling. The smile is the first thing I notice. It is a good smile — full, genuine-seeming, crinkling the corners of her eyes. It is the smile of someone who is happy to be here, who is delighted to see us, who has been looking forward to this reunion with the uncomplicated pleasure of an old friend returning.

"Hi," she says. "I'm Cate. Cate Morrow. Saskia invited me — I think? I was a couple of years behind you all at school. You probably don't remember me."

She extends her hand. I take it. Her grip is warm and firm and exactly the right duration — not too brief, not too long, not too tight, not too loose. A calibrated handshake. A handshake designed to convey friendliness and trustworthiness and exactly nothing else.

"Neve," I say.

"Neve Cassidy. Of course." Her smile widens. "It's so good to finally put a face to the name. Saskia's told me quite a bit — do you all still see each other? It's been so long."

I look at her the way I look at clients in the first session — watching for the seams. The smile is perfect. That's what bothers me. It's perfect in the way things are perfect when they have been practiced — no hesitation, no micro-adjustments, no moment of genuine uncertainty before the expression settles. Real smiles are messy. They arrive too early or too late, they're asymmetrical, they engage the wrong muscles. This smile engages all the right muscles in all the right order with all the right timing. This smile is a performance.

I know this because I do the same thing. Every day. In my office, with my clients, with my family, with Liam, with Lily, with my mother. I perform calm. I perform competence. I perform fine. I know what a performance looks like because I give one every day of my life.

"Welcome," I say. "I think everyone's through here — the kitchen. Come through."

I lead her through the hallway. She remarks on the house — "Oh, this is lovely, is it a rental?" — and I answer — "Yes, Saskia found it" — and we make the small talk people make when they are filling the space between themselves and the thing they are not discussing.

Imogen greets her with immediate warmth. Rowan is upstairs — I heard her footsteps above me earlier. Saskia is at the kitchen table with her laptop, watching Cate the way I watch Cate — from behind a professional mask, cataloguing details, looking for the thing that doesn't fit.

Cate sits at the table. Imogen pours her coffee. Cate wraps her hands around the mug and smiles and says "This takes me right back — doesn't it feel like coming home? Being here, all of us together?" and I watch her settle into the chair with the ease of someone who has studied how ease looks.

She was a couple of years behind us. I try to place her. I close my eyes for a moment and I reach back — back to Sorrento Primary, back to the late nineties, back to the playground and the classrooms and the faces of children I haven't thought about in twenty-five years. The faces come in fragments: a gap-toothed grin, a ponytail, a scraped knee, a school hat pulled low. But Cate's face doesn't surface. There's no slot for her in the filing cabinet of my memory, no folder with her name, no image that corresponds to "a couple of years behind."

This doesn't mean anything. I was twelve. The two-year gap between us would have been enormous — the difference between the senior playground and the junior playground, between being twelve and being nine. I wouldn't have noticed a girl two years younger. She would have been background. Set dressing. A smaller child in a larger world. Of course I don't remember her.

Of course.

We sit in the kitchen and Cate asks questions. "So what does everyone do now?" and "Are you all married? Kids?" and "I moved to London when I was fifteen, so I completely lost touch." She asks the questions the way a talk-show host asks — with the appearance of genuine interest, with the timing of someone who knows when to pause and when to follow up, with the precision of a person who has learned that the best way to be liked is to be curious about others.

My therapist brain is running. Cate's questions are well-constructed. Open-ended. They invite disclosure without demanding it. They're the questions a therapist asks. The questions a journalist asks. The questions of someone trained to draw information without people noticing.

Or I'm projecting. Cate is a woman I don't remember from school and I am suspicious of her because I am suspicious of everything in this house, including myself. That's the more rational explanation. That's the one I should go with.

Imogen is talking about her children — Sophie, ten, and Ethan, seven. Her face when she says their names is the realest thing in the room. Cate listens with her head tilted and her smile in place and her eyes on Imogen's face and I watch her watching Imogen and I think: you are very good at this. Whatever this is.

Saskia says very little. Saskia is watching too. I wonder what she sees.

The coffee in my mug is cold. I've been holding it since the garden. My hands are wrapped around it the way my hands wrap around everything — tightly, knuckles whitening, tendons standing out, the grip of someone holding on to something that might be taken away.

I look up and Cate is watching me. Not the room. Me. Her smile is in place but her eyes are different — focused, assessing, a fraction too intense for the casualness of the moment. She looks away when she sees me notice. The break is clean, professional, the way a card player lays down a hand when they realize they've been read.

I excuse myself. I go to the back garden again.

The morning has warmed slightly. The sky is pale grey rather than dark grey. The stone wall is the same. The path is the same. The lawn is the same. The house is the same shape against the same sky. The window is —

I look at the window.

The pressure returns immediately — behind my eyes, in my temples, at the base of my skull. Something my brain is trying to push through, something that has been building since I drove down the highway and my hands began to shake.

I look at the window and I am not in the garden.

I am twelve.

I am standing in this garden and the grass is warm under my bare feet and the air is not July-cold but December-warm, thick with salt and eucalyptus and the sweet green smell of cut grass and summer. The light is not grey but gold. The stone wall is the same — limestone, warm in the sun, white where the mortar catches the light. The path is the same. The gate is the same.

And in the driveway — where the rental cars are now, where the brick has been replaced by concrete — there is an ambulance.

It is white with orange stripes. The lights are on — red and blue, red and blue, painting the stone wall in pulses of colour that turn the limestone first crimson then cobalt then crimson again. The lights are silent. No siren. The siren stopped a long time ago, or it was never on, or I didn't hear it because I was —

I was —

The stone wall is red. The stone wall is blue. The lights pulse and I am twelve and I am standing in this exact spot and the grass is warm and there is an ambulance in the driveway and my hands are empty and I am looking at the wall and the wall is the colour of emergency and no one is moving and the lights keep going, red and blue, red and blue, and someone is —

The mug cracks.

The sound is sharp — a clean, high crack, the sound of ceramic reaching its limit, the sound of something holding too much pressure for too long finally letting go. I look down. The mug has split along a hairline fracture that I didn't see, that was probably there all morning, that my grip has been widening with every passing minute in this terrible garden. My hands are still wrapped around it. My grip is too tight. The pressure of my fingers has broken the ceramic. The coffee — cold, leftover, a thin brown pool at the bottom of the mug — is seeping through the crack and running over my fingers and dripping onto the grass.

I let go. The mug falls in two pieces. The handle separates. The shards land on the lawn, white against green, and the last of the coffee darkens the grass between my feet.

I kneel to pick up the pieces. The ceramic is sharp. I feel it before I register it — a thin, clean line of pain across the pad of my index finger. I lift my hand. A bead of blood is forming, round and dark, swelling from the cut. I watch it grow. I watch the surface tension hold the blood in a perfect sphere, trembling with each beat of my pulse, dark red, darker than it should be against the pale morning light.

I pick up the broken mug pieces. I watch the blood pool on my fingertip and think, without knowing why: *The last time I was in this garden, someone was dying.*

---

# THE FOURTH STEP — Chapter 10: Saskia

## Observation

I am awake at six. I don't set an alarm. I don't need one. My body wakes at six the way a court convenes at ten — precisely, without enthusiasm, because the schedule requires it.

The house is quiet. I lie in bed for four minutes and listen. No footsteps. No voices. The heating clicked on at five-thirty — I noted the time. The wind has died since last night. The sea is audible, faint, rhythmic, approximately once every four seconds. Normal wave interval for this section of the coast in winter.

I dress. Dark trousers, white shirt, grey cardigan. I don't own weekend clothes. I don't see the purpose of signalling relaxation through fabric.

The kitchen is empty. I make coffee — the capsule machine produces an acceptable approximation of espresso, though the crema is thin and the temperature is three degrees below optimal. I note this without irritation. Facts are not complaints. Facts are data.

I sit at the kitchen table with my laptop. The screen displays my notes from last night: a timeline of the evening, observations of each woman's behaviour, a list of questions I intend to ask. I have fourteen questions. I've ranked them by priority. The first three are about the night Kiera died. The last eleven are about what each woman has done with the twenty-five years since.

Neve is the first downstairs. Eight-seventeen. She's wearing trackpants and a jumper and thick socks and she's carrying a white mug from the cupboard. She looks like she hasn't slept. The skin under her eyes is discoloured — bilateral, purplish, consistent with venous pooling from poor sleep. Her shoulders are held at an angle that suggests chronic tension in the trapezius. She fills the mug from the capsule machine, wraps both hands around it, and walks to the back door without making eye contact.

"Morning," I say.

"Morning." She doesn't turn. "I'm going to sit in the garden for a bit."

She slides the door open and steps outside. I watch her walk to the centre of the lawn and stop. She stands there, holding her mug, looking at the house. Her face is turned up toward the upper storey. Toward the window at the end of the upstairs hallway. She's looking at it the way you look at something you can't stop looking at — not with curiosity, but with the fixed, involuntary attention of someone watching a wound they can't stop touching.

Then she looks away. Quickly. Deliberately. The way you look away from something that hurts.

Neve performs calm. She performs it the way a witness performs credibility — with effort, with control, with the minute attention to detail that reveals the performance itself. Her hands shake. Her breathing is shallow. She won't go upstairs. She hasn't been upstairs once since she arrived. She angles her body away from the staircase when she passes it. She took the ground-floor bedroom and justified it with "I like the morning light," which is a reason that explains the choice without explaining it.

Her avoidance is specific. It's not the house she avoids — she moves through the ground floor with ease. It's not the past she avoids — she references it cautiously, testing the edges. It's the upstairs. Specifically the upstairs hallway. Specifically the window. She knows something is up there the way a body knows a wound — not with the mind, but with the tissue.

Imogen appears at eight-forty-two. She's wearing a floral dressing gown and she's already talking.

"Good morning! Did anyone sleep? I slept terribly, the mattress is so firm, isn't it? Not that I'm complaining — it's a lovely house, such a lovely house. I'll make breakfast. Does everyone want breakfast? I make a very good eggs Benedict if there's hollandaise — there probably isn't hollandaise, is there? Rental properties never have hollandaise. I'll check."

She moves through the kitchen with efficiency, opening cupboards, assessing supplies, her hands in constant motion. The behavior is consistent with what the literature calls undoing: ritualized action aimed at symbolically reversing an event.

Imogen performs cheer. She performs it the way Neve performs calm — thoroughly, with the exhausting commitment of someone who has been rehearsing for twenty-five years. But there's a gap. Imogen doesn't look at the back of the house. Her gaze slides across the sliding door, the deck, the garden, and she turns away. Every time.

She'll face the front. She'll face the side. She'll face the kitchen and the hallway and the living room. But the back of the house, where the view includes the window above the door — no. She keeps her back to it. She positions herself at the counter with her shoulders to the sliding door, and she moves through the space like someone navigating a room with a missing floor — carefully, around the gap, never looking down.

I note these observations in my file. Neve: avoids upstairs, somatic distress when viewing upper window from garden, performative calm, shaking hands, restricted breathing. Imogen: avoids back-of-house sightlines, performative cheer, constant manual activity, possible undoing behavior.

Rowan is not downstairs.

I notice this the way I notice everything — as a data point. Four women in the house. Three visible. One absent. Rowan's shoes — canvas sneakers, mud-caked, size eight — are still by the front door. She's here. She's not in the kitchen.

I wait until Imogen is absorbed in breakfast and Neve is in the garden. Then I go upstairs.

The stairs are new. Fourteen steps, west wall. I don't count them. I counted them yesterday. The fourth step doesn't creak — the renovation replaced the stair structure entirely. But the upstairs hallway has the same dimensions. Five meters. One meter thirty-eight wide. Two meters sixty-four high. The grey carpet is the same grey carpet. The window at the end is the same sealed, fixed-pane, aluminum-framed window.

Rowan is standing in front of it.

She's holding her camera to her eye. Not a phone — a proper camera, black body, large lens, the tool of someone who sees the world in frames and light values. She's photographing the window frame — not the glass, not the view, but the edge where the aluminum meets the wall, the junction of new and old.

She doesn't hear me. Or she does and doesn't acknowledge it. With Rowan, it's impossible to tell. She exists in a different register — sensory, not social. The camera mediates her relationship with the world. She sees it through a lens, literally and figuratively.

"Rowan."

She lowers the camera. Turns. Her face is the same face it was at twelve — angular, pale, framed by dark hair that's longer now but still Kiera's style. The silver ring is on her right hand. Kiera's ring. She took it at the funeral. I saw her. I didn't say anything. I've never said anything.

"Saskia."

"What are you photographing?"

She looks at the camera, then at the window, then back at me. The sequence takes three seconds. She's processing the question through the lens of her own experience — translating my words into her sensory vocabulary before answering.

"The frame," she says. "I found something last night."

She sits on the floor — the hallway carpet, cross-legged, the camera in her lap. She scrolls through the images on the screen. Then she turns the camera toward me.

The photograph is well-composed. Flash-lit, flat, clinical — the kind of light that eliminates shadow and reveals surface. The image shows the window frame: the white aluminum edge, the rubber seal, and behind it, visible in the gap between new frame and old wall, the original timber. Dark, rough, old.

And on the timber: scratches.

Five parallel lines. Evenly spaced. Running downward, top right to bottom left. The width of a child's hand. The depth of fingernails in soft wood.

My pulse surges. I feel it — the sudden acceleration, the surge of adrenaline, the fight-or-flight response activated by a piece of visual data. My heart rate increases from approximately sixty-five beats per minute to approximately ninety. My breathing remains even. My face remains still. My expression does not change.

I have spent twenty-four years waiting for evidence. Twenty-four years of police reports and autopsy summaries and freedom-of-information requests and dead-end conversations with retired officers who told me to let it go. Twenty-four years of knowing that Kiera was pushed and being unable to prove it. And here, in a photograph on a camera held by a woman sitting on a carpet in a renovated hallway, is the proof. Fingernail scratches. Grip marks. Someone held on. Someone was pulled away.

I do not reach for the camera. I do not lean forward. I do not change my posture. I lower myself to the carpet, cross-legged, facing her, maintaining eye contact. My pulse is at ninety beats per minute. My face is an unreadable document.

"When did you take this?" I ask.

"Last night. Two, maybe three in the morning. I came upstairs. I couldn't sleep. I photographed the hallway and I saw them."

"Show me the original image."

She scrolls. Shows me. The full hallway, flash-lit. The window at the end. The walls. The carpet. And in the corner of the frame, where the aluminum meets the wall, the scratches. Undeniable. Physical. Present.

"Have you shown anyone else?"

"No."

I reach into my pocket and take out my phone. I open the camera app. "Hold it up again. I want to photograph it myself."

She holds the camera screen toward me. I photograph the screen — her photograph of the scratches. Then I stand and walk to the window frame. I crouch. I angle my phone into the gap between the aluminum and the wall. The light is poor. I use the flash. Three photographs from different angles. The images are adequate. Not high resolution — phone camera, LED flash, suboptimal conditions — but legible. The scratches are visible. They can be verified against Rowan's higher-quality images.

I stand. I pocket the phone.

"Rowan. Fourteen December, 1999. What do you remember?"

She's sitting on the carpet with her camera in her lap and her knees drawn up and she looks twelve. The way she looked at twelve. The way all of us looked at twelve — small, young, unprepared for what the world was about to do.

"I remember the cold," she says.

Her voice is different here. Not the dreamy, sensory voice she uses in company. Quieter. More precise. As if the hallway itself demands a different register.

"I remember the light. Gold light. December light. It filled the hallway like water."

She looks at the window.

"I remember two shapes. At the end of the hallway. One was small. One was tall. The tall one moved. The small one —"

She stops.

"The small one what?" I ask.

"The small one went through the window."

The word she uses is went. Not fell. Not jumped. Went. The same word she used at twelve, when the police asked her what she saw and she said "she went through the window" and they wrote it down as "she fell from the window" because went was not a word that fit their narrative. I read the transcript. I know what they changed.

"Went," I say.

"Went. Like — like the window was a door. Like she went through it on purpose. But she didn't. She didn't go. She was — the tall one moved and then she went. The tall one moved first."

"The tall one moved," I repeat. I'm recording this. In my mind, with the precision of a court transcript, word for word. "Rowan. Was the tall one an adult or a child?"

She closes her eyes. I watch her face. The processing is visible — her eyelids flickering, her jaw tightening, her fingers curling against the camera body. She's in the hallway. Not this hallway. The other one. The one from twenty-five years ago.

"Tall," she says. "Tall like an adult. I couldn't see — it was dark. The shapes were dark. But the tall one was tall the way a grown-up is tall. The small one was small the way we were small."

"Did you see a face?"

"No. Just shapes. Just the shapes and the movement and then —"

"Then?"

"Then the window was empty."

I have enough. Scratches. Testimony. A witness who saw two figures and one of them was adult-sized and the adult moved and the child went through the window. Twenty-four years of waiting and now I have this.

I stand. I put my phone in my pocket. Three photographs of the frame, one of Rowan's camera screen. Evidence. Admissible? No. A coronial investigation doesn't require the same evidentiary threshold as a criminal trial. Relevant? Yes.

"I need to go downstairs," I say. "Don't show those photographs to anyone else. Not yet. Can you do that?"

Rowan nods. She lifts the camera back to her eye. She photographs the window again. She photographs the light. She photographs the space where the shapes stood. She is doing what she has always done — recording, preserving, holding moments still. The camera is her memory. The camera holds what her brain cannot sequence.

I go downstairs.

The kitchen is occupied. Imogen is at the stove — eggs now, scrambling, the spatula moving in quick circles. And at the table, with a cup of coffee and a smile, is a woman I don't recognize.

She's brown-haired, average height, pleasant-faced. She's wearing jeans and a navy jumper. She's sitting in the chair where Neve sat last night, angled toward the room, with her back to the wall and her eyes on the doorway. She positioned herself the way I position myself. I note this.

"Saskia," Imogen says from the stove. "This is Cate. Cate Morrow. She arrived this morning — she was a couple of years behind us at school."

"Cate." I sit across from her. I don't smile. I don't extend my hand. I look at her face the way I look at a witness who hasn't been prepped.

"Saskia. It's wonderful to finally meet you." Her smile is full and warm and precisely timed. "Saskia Brennan, right? The barrister? I've heard such things about you."

"Have you."

"I feel like I know you all already. From — well, from reputation, I suppose. It's a small community, Sorrento, even from London. You hear things."

"What kind of things?"

"Oh, just — how close you all were. The five of you. It was like a little gang, wasn't it? Everyone knew the five girls from Sorrento Primary."

"There were six of us," I say.

Her smile doesn't falter. A micro-pause — three-tenths of a second, possibly less — and then the correction lands and she nods and says "Of course. I meant — yes. The group. I was too young to be part of it, of course. I was in Year Three when you were in Year Six. But I remember watching you. You all seemed so — grown up. So confident."

The correction was smooth. The pause was not. Three-tenths of a second is within normal conversational range, but she moved past "six of us" without asking who the sixth was. A person who genuinely didn't know the group wouldn't have known there were six. A person who did know wouldn't have said five. The error reveals familiarity. The speed of the correction reveals practice.

She asks questions. Not about the present. About the house, the group, the history. Subtle questions, wrapped in nostalgia, delivered with a warmth that makes them feel like conversation rather than interrogation. She asks when the renovation happened. She asks who owned the house after the — after the holidays. She asks whether anyone kept in touch with the Doyles.

The Doyles.

Kiera's family.

She asks about the Doyles the way you ask about a distant relative — with appropriate concern, with the right amount of curiosity, with the follow-up question ready before the first answer is finished. She asks where they went after — after Sorrento. She asks if anyone knows what Kiera's parents do now. She asks if anyone has seen the grave.

These are not the questions of someone who was two years behind us at school. These are the questions of someone investigating. Someone building a picture. Someone who needs information and has learned how to ask for it without asking.

I watch her. I answer minimally — yes, no, I don't know. I let Imogen fill the gaps. Imogen talks about the area, the changes, the new cafes, and Cate listens and asks follow-ups and every follow-up is a thread being pulled, gently, persistently, to see how far it unravels.

She asks about the holiday house specifically. "Was it a family friend's place? The one you all stayed at?" And Imogen says "It belonged to Neve's family, didn't it?" and Cate says "Oh, of course" and looks at me and I see it — a flash, a micro-expression, the contraction of the muscles around the eyes that Ekman would classify as grief or pain, gone in under half a second, replaced by interest, replaced by the listening face, replaced by nothing.

She knew whose house it was. She didn't need Imogen to tell her. The "oh, of course" was cover. The micro-expression was real.

I stand. "I need some air."

"Of course," Cate says. "We should talk more later — just the two of us, I mean. If that's all right?"

I walk to the hallway. I stop at the doorway. I turn. Cate is watching me. She was watching me leave — her head was turned, her eyes were following me, and when I turned, she looked away. The break was smooth. Practiced. The kind of look-away that comes from knowing you're being observed.

I watch from the doorway and think: *Who are you?*

---

# THE FOURTH STEP — Chapter 11: Imogen

## Breakfast

I wake at six, which is normal, which is okay. Back home I wake at five forty-five because Sophie needs to be at school by eight and there are lunches to make and uniforms to find and Ethan's asthma pump to check and breakfast to cook, and the mornings are my favourite part of the day because the mornings are when everything is under control, when the house hums with toast and running water and Sophie's voice asking for the pink cup, not the blue one, Mum, the pink one, and I love the pink cup and the blue cup and all the cups because the cups mean Sophie is here and Sophie is alive and Sophie is ten and Sophie is okay.

I don't have the pink cup here. I don't have Sophie here. I have a holiday house kitchen that has been renovated within an inch of its life and a stocked pantry — someone arranged this, the rental company, a welcome basket with muesli and instant coffee and a bottle of milk in the fridge — and four other women asleep in their rooms, or not asleep, and a morning ahead of me that needs to be normal.

I need to cook.

I open the pantry. I open the fridge. I take stock. Eggs — a dozen. Butter. Milk. Bread. There's a punnet of cherry tomatoes, some spinach, a block of cheddar. In the pantry: flour, sugar, a tin of baking powder, a jar of honey. There's enough here. There's enough for a proper breakfast, a real breakfast, the kind of breakfast that says this is a normal morning in a normal house with normal people and everything is okay.

I start with the eggs. I crack six into a bowl — no, eight. There are five of us now, with Cate. Eight eggs. I whisk them. The whisking is good — the motion, the rhythm, the sound of the fork against the bowl. My hands are busy. When my hands are busy the rest of me is okay. I add a splash of milk. A pinch of salt. A grind of pepper. I whisk more. The eggs become pale yellow foam, uniform, smooth. That's good. That's how eggs should be.

I put butter in the pan. It melts. The sound it makes — the sizzle, the foam, the way butter sounds when it hits hot metal — is the sound of a kitchen being a kitchen. I pour in the eggs and I stir, low and slow, the way I do at home, the way I've done every morning for fifteen years, pulling the curds from the edge, letting the liquid run back, folding, folding.

I toast bread. I slice the tomatoes in half and put them in a bowl. I lay the table — five places, five plates, five knives, five forks. I adjust the spacing between the plates. I adjust it again. I go back to the eggs and stir. I go back to the table and adjust the forks. I straighten the napkins. I reorganise the cherry tomatoes into a neat circle. I wipe a smudge from the water glass. My hands are moving. They have not stopped since I entered the kitchen. They will not stop.

"There you are."

I turn. Cate is standing in the doorway.

She's dressed — jeans, a cream jumper, flat shoes. Her hair is pulled back. Her face is — she looks the way everyone looks first thing in the morning, which is okay, which is normal, except her eyes. Her eyes are awake. Fully awake. The way Saskia's eyes are awake. Like they've been awake for hours, watching.

"Good morning!" I say, and my voice is bright and warm and I mean it. "I'm making breakfast. Scrambled eggs, toast, tomatoes. Sit down, sit down. Coffee?"

"I'd love some." She crosses to the counter and pulls out a stool. She sits. She rests her chin on her hand. She looks at me the way Sophie looks at me when I'm cooking — attentive, interested, like what I'm doing matters.

I pour her coffee. I hand it to her. I go back to the eggs.

"Can I help?"

"No, no, I've got it. I like cooking. Greg says I use cooking as a — well, he says I channel my energy into it. He's probably right. I made three different kinds of pancakes for Sophie's birthday last year. Three. Chocolate chip, blueberry, and plain. She only ate the chocolate chip ones, but the point is I made them. Options. Options are important."

I'm talking too much. I know I'm talking too much. The words are coming the way the eggs are coming — steady, continuous, not stopping. I stir. I talk. I talk. I stir.

"So this is the house," Cate says. She wraps her hands around the coffee cup. "It's not how I pictured it. Was it always this — open? The layout, I mean?"

"You should have seen it before. Well — you probably don't remember. You were a couple of years behind us, right?"

"Right. I don't remember much."

"It was different. The kitchen had yellow tiles. Can you imagine? Yellow tiles. And there was a calendar on the wall with a picture of a lighthouse. Very holiday-house. Very—"

I stop. The spatula is in my hand. The eggs are nearly done, soft and glossy, the way they should be. I shouldn't remember the calendar. I shouldn't remember the yellow tiles. Those details are from twenty-five years ago and I haven't thought about them in — I haven't thought about them.

"It was cosy," I say, recovering. "Old-fashioned, you know? Like your nan's house. Everything a bit worn but in a good way, like — okay, I don't know, it was just warm. That's the word. Warm."

"I don't, really," Cate says. "I was so young. Tell me about the old days. What was it like? You all being here together?"
