She's looking at me with those awake eyes, chin on her hand, and she is asking me a simple question about old times and I should be able to answer it because I've been thinking about the old times since I got in the car yesterday, rehearsing them, organising them, keeping the good ones at the front and the other ones somewhere else, somewhere I don't have to look.

"It was lovely," I say. "It really was. We were twelve — twelve and silly and — we played Truth or Dare, do you remember that game? Everyone played it. Kiera was the one who always suggested it. She was — she was the one with all the ideas. The games, the ghost stories, the — she was the one who made everything fun."

The eggs need stirring. I stir them. My hand moves in circles, folding, folding.

"We had pizza," I say. "We ordered pizza and ate it on the floor in the living room, on our sleeping bags, five in a row, all lined up. There were five of us. Five sleeping bags. Five girls. Me, Neve, Saskia, Rowan, and Kiera."

I say the names in order, the way I've always said them, a list, a roster, five names that go together, a set, a complete set, except the set is not complete anymore, has not been complete for twenty-five years.

"We stayed up late," I say. "We told ghost stories. Kiera told the best ghost stories. She'd hold a torch under her chin and — she was the best. She was the best of us."

Cate is watching me. Her hands are around her coffee cup. The steam rises between us.

"What was she like?"

The question is simple. Four words. What was she like. A question anyone might ask about someone they've heard about, someone from the past, someone they never met. A normal question at a normal breakfast in a normal house.

I stop stirring.

The spatula is in the air. My hand is frozen mid-motion, the spatula held above the eggs, and the eggs are starting to stick to the bottom of the pan because I'm not stirring them, but I can't move, not yet, because she's asked me about Kiera and I have an answer. I've always had an answer. I've been practising this answer for twenty-five years, in the shower and in the car and in the dark when Sophie is asleep and the house is quiet and the question rises from somewhere I don't like to go and I answer it. I answer it the way I'm about to answer it now, every word in the right order, every word in the right place, rehearsed, because some things need to be rehearsed, because some things need to be said correctly, because if you say a thing enough times the right way with the right words in the right order it becomes true.

"She was the best of us," I say. "She was brave and funny and she — she fell."

A breath. The eggs are sticking. The butter is browning. The kitchen smells like toast and coffee and something hot, something I don't have a word for.

"It was an accident. She leaned out the window and fell. It was summer and the window was open and it was just — just an accident."

The words come out the way they always come out. Flat. Level. The same cadence, the same rhythm, the same arrangement of syllables I've been arranging since I was twelve years old. It was an accident. She leaned out. The window was open. Summer. Just an accident. The words have worn a groove in my mind the way water wears a groove in stone — smooth and deep and effortless, the path of least resistance, the place the words go when they leave my mouth because there is nowhere else for them to go.

I put the spatula down. I turn off the heat. I plate the eggs — five plates, five portions, sliding the soft yellow curds onto the toast, arranging the cherry tomatoes in neat circles, a sprig of spinach on each plate because presentation matters, because when food looks right it is right, because when things are arranged correctly they are correct.

I am not looking at Cate.

But I glance at her — just a glance, a flicker, the way you glance at something in your peripheral vision — and I see it.

Her face.

For one second — less than a second, a fraction, a flash — her face is not the face of a woman asking a casual question at breakfast. It is something else. Something raw. Something that looks like a wall collapsing. Like a mask cracking. Like someone who has been holding something for a very long time and the grip has failed and the thing they were holding is falling. Her eyes, for one fraction of a second, are not awake but destroyed. Her mouth is open, not speaking, not breathing, just open, as if the air has been knocked out of her.

Then it's gone.

Her face smooths. Her hands tighten around her cup. She looks at me and she says, "God. I'm so sorry. We were so young — all of us. I can't imagine what that was like for you."

Her voice is steady. Completely steady. The voice of someone who heard a sad story and is responding appropriately. Measured. Level. The way you'd say it to a stranger. The way you'd say it about someone you never knew.

"Yeah," I say. "It was — I mean, yes. Obviously. It was terrible. We were twelve, you know? You don't — you don't expect that at twelve."

I don't register what I just saw on Cate's face because by the time I turn back to the counter it has already been replaced by something else — a neutral expression, a polite nod, the face of a woman who is okay. Everything is okay. The eggs are plated. The toast is done. The table is set. Five places. Five plates. Five forks.

"I'll get the others," I say, and I walk out of the kitchen and down the hallway and I call up the stairs, "Breakfast! Eggs are ready!" and my voice is bright and warm and okay.

They come. Neve first, hair tied back, the careful posture, the watchful eyes. Saskia next, already dressed, always dressed, the pressed shirt, the precise movements. Rowan last, thin and angular and moving the way she moves everything — like light, like she's not entirely in contact with the ground.

We sit. Five women at a table. Five plates of eggs and toast and tomatoes. Five cups of coffee. A normal breakfast in a normal house on a normal morning and everything is okay and I am okay and we are all okay.

I eat. I lift my fork and I put eggs in my mouth and I chew and I swallow and I say, "Does everyone like them? I can make more if we need more, there are plenty of eggs," and I smile and I eat and I talk and I fill the silence with words the way I fill the kitchen with cooking, the way I fill my hands with motion, the way I fill every space I enter with warmth and noise and the appearance of everything being okay.

Cate eats quietly. Neve picks at her food. Saskia eats with mechanical efficiency. Rowan holds her fork in her long fingers and looks at her plate as if she's photographing it, as if the eggs and the toast are a composition to be studied.

I'm talking about Sophie. I don't mean to be talking about Sophie but I am. "She's just started Year Five and her teacher says she's very advanced in reading, which doesn't surprise me because she's always got a book in her hand, always, and I think that's wonderful, don't you? A child who reads? Greg says I worry too much but I don't think you can worry too much about children, I think worrying is — it's what mothers do, it's —"

I stop talking because I'm talking too much. Because the silence I'm trying to fill is not the kind of silence that can be filled with words. It's the kind of silence that sits underneath the words, underneath the cooking and the chatter and the plates and the forks, underneath everything, patient and permanent, and no amount of noise will move it.

I eat my eggs. The eggs are good. Soft and creamy, the way I like them. I am aware of Cate across the table, eating steadily, her face composed, her hands calm around her fork. I am aware of Neve beside me, staring at her plate as if she's trying to read something written on it. I am aware of Saskia at the end of the table, watching everyone, always watching, her dark eyes moving from face to face the way a camera moves across a room.

I am aware of Rowan, who has stopped eating and is looking toward the hallway, toward the stairs, her fork suspended in the air, her gaze fixed on something the rest of us can't see.

"Rowan? Are you okay?" I ask.

She doesn't answer. She sets her fork down. She stands.

"I need to — excuse me," she says, and she leaves the table and I hear her footsteps on the stairs — one, two, three, four, and the fourth step groans — and then she is gone, upstairs, and the table has four women at it now, and the silence is back, and it's bigger than before.

I pick up my fork. I take another bite. I chew. I swallow. I say, "She's probably not a breakfast person. Not everyone is. Greg isn't. I make him eggs every morning and he has toast instead. Men are funny like that."

I'm talking. I'm filling. I'm okay.

I look down at my hand.

My knuckles are white.

I've been gripping my fork so hard the tendons in the back of my hand are raised, sharp lines under the skin, and my fingers are locked around the handle, the metal pressing into my palm, and I didn't know I was doing this. I didn't feel it. I don't remember gripping.

I let go.

Four red marks on my palm. Four indentations where the metal pressed into the flesh. Four marks in a line, evenly spaced, the width of fork tines pressed hard enough to leave a pattern in the skin.

I don't remember gripping.

---

# THE FOURTH STEP — Chapter 12: Rowan

## The House Speaks

The morning light is different from the night light. It comes through the windows not as a blade but as a wash — soft, diffuse, the colour of skim milk, spreading across surfaces without direction or intent. It illuminates everything equally. It hides nothing. Morning light is the least forgiving light there is.

I take my camera out of its bag at seven. The house is awake — I can hear Imogen in the kitchen, the percussion of cooking, the rhythm of someone who needs her hands to move. I can hear voices, low. The murmur of conversation I cannot make out and do not try to. I stand in my doorway and I listen to the house breathing — floorboards settling, pipes in the walls, the whisper of air through vents. Every house has a respiratory system. This one breathes shallow and cold.

I start in the hallway. Not the upstairs hallway — the downstairs one. I work from the bottom up, the way light does.

The entrance hall. Grey walls, blonde wood floor, white ceiling, pendant lamp. I frame it: the length of the hall, the front door at one end, the living room doorway at the other. I press the shutter. The moment is captured. The hallway is now a photograph — a flat rectangle of light and shadow, fixed, unmoving, true in the way photographs are true: precisely and nothing more.

I move to the living room. Leather sofas. Wooden table. Rug the colour of dried wheat. I photograph the rug — the texture of it, the way the fibres catch the morning light and throw it back in small bright points, each one a sun in miniature. I photograph the window, the view of the front garden, the glass reflecting the room back on itself, so that in the photograph you can see both the garden and the room, layered, transparent, the inside and the outside occupying the same flat space.

I move to the kitchen. The smell of eggs and butter and toast. Imogen is at the stove, spatula in hand, her face pink from the heat. Cate is sitting at the counter, hands around a coffee cup, watching Imogen the way you watch a performance. I lift the camera. Imogen turns.

"Oh! Rowan! Breakfast is almost ready — ten minutes! Are you hungry? Of course you're hungry, I'm sorry, that's a silly question. Sit, sit — okay, just — I'll make you a plate."

I lower the camera. Some spaces are not mine to take.

I go back to the hallway and I photograph the staircase from below — the rise of the steps, the turn, the bannister going up and curving out of sight. The bannister is new — pale wood, smooth, modern. But where the bannister meets the wall, where the newel post is bolted to the plaster, there is a scratch. Not a construction scratch. Not a wear mark. A scratch — thin, deliberate, the kind of mark a ring makes when a hand slides along wood.

My ring. Kiera's ring. On my right hand. Silver.

I photograph the scratch. I zoom in on the screen. The mark is consistent with metal on wood — pressure applied laterally, a single pass, the depth of a band being dragged across the surface. It could be from the renovation. It could be from a mover, a tool, a belt buckle. It could be from a hand gripping the bannister in the dark, a silver ring catching the wood as the body moved fast, fast toward the top of the stairs, fast toward the hallway, fast toward the window at the end.

I photograph it and move on.

The upstairs hallway. I climb the stairs — one, two, three, four. The fourth step is silent beneath me — new timber, renovation-tight — but the absence of the groan is a sound in itself, a frequency my body remembers, entering my chest and vibrating. I keep going. Five, six, seven, eight, nine, ten. Turn. Eleven, twelve, thirteen, fourteen. The hallway opens in front of me, grey and still and cold, the sealed window at the end a rectangle of morning grey, the blade of light on the carpet thinner now, less dramatic, morning light being less dramatic than moonlight.

I photograph the hallway. The empty hallway. The window at the end, the walls on either side, the doors to the bedrooms, the smoke detector, the fire extinguisher. I photograph the window — the aluminium frame, the fixed pane, the reflection of the hallway inside the glass, so that the window shows me the hallway I am standing in, flattened and reversed, a photograph within a photograph.

I photograph the wall beside the window. I photograph the place where the aluminium frame meets the original wood. The scratches are still there — five parallel lines, downward, the marks I found last night. In morning light they are less dramatic. Less like evidence. More like what they are: lines in wood. Old lines. Patient lines. Lines that have been waiting for someone to see them, the way light waits for a lens.

I move to the rooms.

The first door on the left. A bedroom, small, a single bed, a window facing the street. I photograph the window. I photograph the ceiling — there is a discolouration near the corner, a faint brown stain, the ghost of an old leak, water damage from a roof that has since been repaired. The stain is the shape of a continent. I photograph it. The house remembers what the renovation tried to forget.

The door on the right. A bathroom. White tile, white fixtures, a mirror above the sink. I photograph the mirror — my own face in it, framed by the camera, the camera reflected in the mirror, an infinite regress of seeing. My face is thin and angular and the morning light is not kind to it. My eyes are dark. My hair is dark. The silver ring on my right hand catches the flash and throws it back.

The second door on the left. The room at the back of the house. I open it.

It is not a bedroom anymore. It has been converted into a study — a desk under the window, a bookshelf, a desk lamp, an upright chair. The window faces the back garden. The walls are the same grey as the hallway. The floor is the same blonde wood. The ceiling is the same height.

But this room was a bedroom twenty-five years ago. This room was the bedroom where five sleeping bags were laid out in a row on the floor. This room was where the pizza boxes were, where the torch was, where Kiera told ghost stories with the light under her chin and her hair falling around her face and her voice making the walls disappear.

I know this because the room is the same size. The same proportions. The desk is where the sleeping bags were, along the wall under the window. The bookshelf is where the door is, to the left of the entrance. The proportions are precise — twelve feet by ten, ceiling eight feet, window occupying most of the external wall. The room has changed its clothes. The body is the same.

I step inside. The floorboards are new — blonde, polished, modern. But they are wood, and wood is laid over wood in old houses, renovation over original, new over old, surface over bone.

I photograph the room. The desk. The chair. The bookshelf. The window. I photograph the walls — grey, clean, no marks, no scratches, no evidence that anything happened here, that five girls laughed here, that a torch lit a face from below here, that someone told stories about ghosts here. The walls are smooth and grey and they say nothing.

I photograph the ceiling — white, smooth, the same faint discolouration in the corner, the same continental stain. I photograph the light fitting — a single globe, modern, energy-efficient, the colour of weak sunlight.

Then I feel it.

Not a sound. Not a sight. A sensation — the specific, localised sensation of a floorboard that is not like the others. My foot finds it. The ball of my left foot, pressing down, and instead of the solid resistance of timber fixed to joists, there is give. Slight. Millimetres. A fractional softness, as if the board is not nailed down, not secured, resting on its neighbours without being fixed.

I look down.

The floorboard is the third from the window, second from the wall. It is the same colour as the others, the same width, the same finish. But the gap between this board and the one next to it is slightly wider — a millimetre, perhaps two. The edge is not factory-cut. The edge has been trimmed. Modified. By hand.

I kneel.

The floor is cold against my knees. The morning light falls on the board at a low angle, picking out the grain, and I can see the edge is not uniform — one side is slightly bevelled, the work of a blade, careful, deliberate. I press my fingers into the gap. The board lifts. It comes up easily — too easily, as if it has been lifted before, many times, as if the fit has been worn smooth by repetition. The underside of the board is raw wood, unpainted, unpolished, the colour of bone.

I set the board aside.

Underneath: a cavity. The space between the floorboards and the ceiling below, a gap of about eight inches, dark and dusty and smelling of old wood and silence. The dust is thick and undisturbed — except in the centre, where something has been placed. The dust has been pressed down in the shape of an object. A rectangle. The size of a hand.

I reach in. My fingers close around it. I lift it out.

A disposable camera.

The casing is Kodak. Yellow and blue, the kind that was everywhere in the late nineties, the kind you bought at a chemist and took on school excursions and dropped off at the developers and forgot about. The plastic is scratched and dusty. The film advance wheel is stiff but functional. The exposure counter reads: 3.

Twenty-four taken. Three remaining. A disposable camera holds twenty-seven. The counter counts down from twenty-seven — each shot advances the wheel, each click subtracts one from the display. Three remaining means twenty-four photographs were taken. Twenty-four moments she chose to hold still. Twenty-four.

I hold the camera in both hands. It is light — the weight of plastic and film and chemistry, the weight of a moment captured and never developed, the weight of twenty-four photographs that have been sitting in this cavity, in the dark, for twenty-five years. The film is still inside. The emulsion is still on the cellulose. The light that entered the lens is still there, latent, unprocessed, waiting for chemistry to make it visible.

The camera was placed here.

Not dropped — the cavity is too specific, the gap too narrow for a camera to fall into accidentally. Not lost — lost things are found in obvious places, under beds, behind furniture, in the bottom of bags. This was hidden. Lifted floorboard, cavity, camera placed inside, floorboard replaced. Deliberate. Careful. Someone did not want this camera found. Or someone wanted it found only by someone who would look — who would feel the give in a floorboard, who would kneel, who would press fingers into a gap, who would lift.

I turn the camera over in my hands. The plastic is cool and textured and old. The Kodak logo is faded but legible. The flash mechanism is on the front — a small rectangle of plastic that would have popped up when the camera was in use. There is a strap attachment on the side with no strap. The lens is a small circle of glass, smudged with fingerprints — old fingerprints, the oils gone, just the pattern left in the dust.

Kiera's fingerprints.

She held this. Her hands held this camera. Her eye looked through this viewfinder. Her finger pressed this shutter. Twenty-four times she pressed this shutter. Twenty-four moments she chose to capture. And the last photographs she ever took — the ones near the end of the roll, the last clicks of the shutter — those photographs are still inside. Undeveloped. Unseen. The last things her eye selected before — before she went through the window.

I sit back on my heels. The camera is in my lap. The floorboard is beside me. The cavity is open, dark, waiting to be closed again.

The room is quiet. Morning light fills it — soft, diffuse, merciless. The desk. The chair. The bookshelf. The walls, grey and clean. The floor, blonde and polished, with one board removed, a dark rectangle where the wood should be. The window, facing the back garden, the garden Imogen said was the same.

Kiera took photographs that night. I remember — not a story, not a sequence, but a sensation: the flash of a disposable camera, sudden and white, illuminating the sleeping bags and the pizza boxes and the faces of five girls in their pyjamas. Kiera laughing. Click. Click. The sound of moments being saved. Her finger on the button. Her eye at the viewfinder. Kiera who saw everything through a lens before I ever held a camera, Kiera who taught me to look before I knew what looking was.

But this camera was hidden under the floorboard. After the photographs were taken. Someone took the camera from Kiera's things — or Kiera hid it herself, knowing something, fearing something, pressing it into the gap and replacing the board — and placed it here. Before the end of the night, or after. The sequence is wrong but the object is real. The camera is real. It is here, in my hands, light as air and heavy as everything.

I turn the camera over. The exposure counter: 3. I press the advance wheel. It clicks. The counter doesn't move — the film is advanced to the last frame. This is the last photograph. The last thing she framed. Whatever Kiera saw last, whatever she chose to capture in the final moments before — before the window, before the fall, before the air took her.

I think about the flash. A disposable camera flash is bright. Bright enough to illuminate a hallway. Bright enough to capture two shapes at the end of a corridor — one tall, one small. Bright enough to fix in silver halide the last thing she saw before the hand and the push and the window and the dark.

I hold the camera against my chest. The plastic is hard and light against my sternum. I can feel my heartbeat through it — or I can feel the camera's absence of heartbeat, the stillness of a thing that has been waiting in the dark for twenty-five years to be found, to be held, to be seen.

The last photographs Kiera ever took are on this film. The last things she saw through a viewfinder. The last moments she chose to preserve. Twenty-four exposures of light that has never been developed, never been printed, never been looked at by anyone since Kiera's eye was behind the lens.

What did you see? I think. What did you photograph, at the end?

I think about the film inside — the undeveloped film, the latent images, the light trapped in silver crystals since December twenty-five years ago, waiting for someone to add the chemicals that would make the invisible visible. I think about what might be there. The bedroom, the sleeping bags, the girls laughing. The hallway, the window, the moonlight. The shapes at the end of the corridor — one tall, one small. The tall one's arm moving. The small one reaching for the frame. Five fingernails scraping paint.

I think: *If I develop this film, I'll see what she saw. The last things she saw.*

I haven't decided whether to tell the others.

---

# THE FOURTH STEP — Chapter 13: Neve

## The Hallway

The bathroom door is closed.

I stand in the hallway and look at it — the narrow white door with the chrome handle, the gap underneath where light shows, the hum of the extractor fan. Someone is in there. Imogen, maybe. She went that direction ten minutes ago with a toiletry bag and a cheerful "back in a mo" and the door closed and the fan started and it hasn't stopped.

I could wait. I've been waiting all morning — through breakfast, through Cate's questions, through Saskia's watchful silence. Waiting for the house to become a house again, not a container for something I can't name, something pressing outward the way gas fills whatever space it's given.

The pressure in my bladder is real. A simple need with a simple solution: go upstairs, use the bathroom, come back down. An adult woman goes upstairs in a house and uses a bathroom and comes back down. This is not remarkable. This is what bodies do.

I look at the staircase.

It's on the west wall. The same wall. The renovation kept them in place — same position, new materials. They lead to the same place.

The same place.

I have not been upstairs since I arrived. I took the downstairs bedroom. I angle my body away from the staircase when I pass it. I keep my eyes on the floor and the walls and everything except the thing at the top of the stairs that I don't look at. Liam would call this avoidance. I call it practical. The bathroom downstairs was adequate. I didn't need to go up.

The bathroom downstairs is closed. I need to go up.

My hands are at my sides. My fingers are tingling. Peripheral vasoconstriction. The blood moving inward, toward my core. Fight or flight. Sympathetic nervous system activation. The body preparing for a threat that doesn't exist. Because there is no threat. There is a staircase.

I take a breath. Four in, seven hold, eight out. The technique is reliable. I've taught it to a hundred clients.

The extractor fan hums behind the bathroom door.

I turn right.

The first step is easy. A rise, a tread, a foot placed with intention. The wood is solid beneath my shoe. New timber. New finish. Nothing to recognize. The second step is the same — unfamiliar, modern, neutral. The third. My hand finds the bannister — pale wood, smooth, steel fittings, nothing like the bannister that was here before, which was dark and worn and warm to the touch. Which was — I don't know what it was. I don't remember the bannister. I don't remember the stairs.

The fourth step groans.

The new stairs don't creak — I know this, the renovation replaced them — but the sound is there, rising through my feet and into my shins and into my femurs and into my pelvis and into my spine and into the base of my skull and something at the base of my skull — something deep, something pre-verbal, something older than language and older than memory — lights up like a switch being thrown.

Not remembers. Recognizes.

The way a lock recognizes a key. The way a wound recognizes the shape of the instrument that made it. A particular frequency. A particular resonance. A sound my body has catalogued and filed under a heading my mind cannot read, kept in a drawer my mind cannot open.

I freeze.

My foot is on the step. Half my weight on it. My hand grips the bannister. My knuckles are white. The sound is still moving through me — reverberating through bones and joints and the fluid in my inner ear — and I know, with my tissue, not with my cognition, that I have heard this sound before. I have stood on this step before.

The knowledge is not cognitive. It is cellular.

The sound fades. The house settles. I am standing on the fourth step with elevated heart rate and shallow breathing and damp palms — the physiological markers of an acute stress response triggered by an auditory stimulus my body has encoded as significant.

I should go back down.

I keep going.

Step five. Step six. The new stairs are wide and even and well-lit from a skylight at the turn. Step seven, eight, nine. The turn — I grip the newel post and swing around it and the angle is the same, the turn is in the same place, but the materials are wrong — pale wood where there was dark, steel where there was iron — and my body knows the geometry even as my eyes see the new surface. Step ten, eleven, twelve, thirteen, fourteen. The stairs open onto the hallway.

I stop at the top.

The upstairs hallway stretches ahead of me. Five meters. I don't know it's five meters the way Saskia knows it — she measured it with a tape measure, she would, that's what Saskia does with the world: she measures it — but I know the length of this hallway the way I know the reach of my own arms. I know it in steps and breaths and the time it takes to walk from here to there. The time is — the time is too long. And also not long enough.

Grey walls. Pale grey, flat finish, modern and inoffensive. The walls were not grey before. They were cream. Warm cream that held the December light and made it golden. The grey absorbs light. The grey tells me nothing. But underneath — I can feel it. Not see it. Feel it. The cream is under the grey. The cream is under everything.

The floor. New blackbutt — blonde timber, laid in straight lines. The original was carpet. Blue carpet, low pile, synthetic. I walked on that carpet. My feet pressed into that carpet. My feet are pressing into the new blackbutt and feeling the old carpet underneath, the way ears hear an overtone, the way a string vibrates at a frequency no one plucked.

I walk.

The hallway is quiet. The others are downstairs. I am alone up here, walking toward a window I have not looked at since I was twelve years old. My body has not once in twenty-five years allowed me to stand in front of an upstairs window without turning away.

The dimensions are precise. The width — I could extend both arms and touch both walls. The distance to the window — twelve steps. I know because I am counting. Grounding technique. Be in the body. Track the data.

One. Two. Three.

The grey walls are smooth. No photographs. No art. But I can feel the cream underneath.

Four. Five. Six.

Doors on either side. Bedroom. Bedroom. The bathroom — the one I came to use. I should stop here. I should turn in and close the door and go back downstairs.

Seven. Eight. Nine.

My body is responding. Elevated heart rate. Constricted throat. The pressure behind my eyes — building, always building, something my brain has been trying to show me since my hands began to shake on the drive down.

Ten. Eleven.

I stop.

Twelve steps. I'm standing in front of the window. The rectangular weight of it pulls at my attention the way a wound pulls at a hand. My breath is shallow, my hands trembling, my jaw locked tight.

I look at the window.

Smaller than it was. Fixed-pane. Sealed shut. White aluminum frame, double-glazed. It can't open. Someone replaced the original with this smaller, sealed, immovable thing. The glass reflects the hallway behind me — grey walls, blonde floor, a woman standing very still.

I look at the window and something shifts.

It starts at the edges. My vision narrows — not darkness at the periphery, but a softening, a dissolving, as if the world beyond this specific point is losing its coherence. The grey walls lose their edge. The blonde floor loses its colour. There is only the window. Only the glass. Only the rectangle of light that is changing — brightening, warming, shifting from the flat grey of a July morning to the gold of a December night.

The grey walls shimmer.

They are cream. The grey falls away like a curtain dropping and the cream is there — warm, soft, familiar, the colour of the hallway as it was, as it has always been underneath the paint. The cream catches the light. Gold light. Amber light. The light of a summer evening when the sun is low and the sky is orange and the air is thick with warmth.

The floor is carpet. Blue carpet. The blackbutt dissolves and the carpet is there, low pile, synthetic, showing the path of feet, holding the warmth of the day. The carpet my body knows. The carpet my feet remember.

The window is larger. Much larger. It fills the end of the hallway — a tall rectangle of night, open, the casement swung outward, the frame wide, the glass gone, replaced by air. Warm air. December air. The air moves through the hallway the way breath moves through a body — in and out, in and out, the house breathing, the night breathing, and the air carries salt and eucalyptus and underneath both, a third note — metallic. Copper. Iron. The taste of blood on the tongue. The smell of fear, not an abstraction, not a metaphor, but a real chemical signature, adrenaline and cortisol sweating from skin, and I am smelling it, I am tasting it, and it is now, this is now, this is happening now.

The hallway is warm.

I am standing at the top of the stairs.

Two figures at the end of the hallway.

One tall. The tall one stands with her back to me. Dark shape against the open window. The proportions are wrong for a child. The height. The width of the shoulders. The angle of the head. She is tall the way a grown-up is tall. Her arm is at her side. Her hand is at her side.

One small. The small one stands in front of the tall one. Closer to the window. Much closer. The small one is my height. The small one is my size. The small one is a child. Her hair is dark. Her back is to me. She is standing between the tall one and the open window.

The tall one's arm moves.

It comes up. Fast. Not a gesture. Not a wave. Not an embrace. The arm comes up and the hand finds the small one's shoulder. The hand closes. The grip is hard. I can see the fingers press into fabric. I can see the small one flinch.

The tall one pushes.

The small one stumbles backward. Toward the window. The window that is open. The window that is a rectangle of dark air and night sky and nothing between the small one and the edge. The small one's arms go out — reaching, grasping, finding nothing, finding air.

Her hands find the frame.

Timber. Old timber. Painted cream. Paint flaking under fingers that are clawing, desperate, holding on. Her hands grip the frame. I see her fingers. I see her fingernails. Small hands. A child's hands. The hands press into the wood.

The fingernails scrape paint.

A thin, high sound. A scraping. A friction of nail on paint on wood. Five lines. Five fingernails. Downward. The marks score the paint, deep, visible, permanent, and the hands slide and the grip holds and the fingers splay and the small one is holding on, holding on to the frame, holding on to the edge of the window, holding on to the world.

The grip holds.

The small one's fingers lose the frame.

One hand goes first. Then the other. The fingernails leave the wood. Five parallel lines scored in the paint, deep, downward. And then the hands are gone and the small one is gone and the window is empty and the warm air moves through the hallway and the tall one is standing there, at the end of the hallway, in front of the open window, and the night is beyond and the small one is not there anymore and the window is empty and the window is empty and the window is —

The tall one turns.

Her face is —

Her face is —

I'm on the floor.

The hallway is grey. The carpet is gone, the blue carpet, the cream walls, the warm air, the open window — gone. The hallway is grey and the floor is hard and I am on it. My cheek is against the blackbutt. My hands are pressed flat against the boards, fingers splayed. My body is trembling — a deep vibration that starts in my core and radiates outward, teeth chattering, jaw clenched, muscles firing in waves I cannot control.

The grey walls are grey. The sealed window is sealed. The December air is July air and it is cold and it smells of new paint and cleaning products and nothing else.

"Neve. Neve, can you hear me?"

Saskia's voice. Above me. Saskia is kneeling beside me — I can see her knees, dark trousers pressed into the blonde floor, her hands on my shoulders. Her grip is firm. Anchoring. Her face is above mine, dark eyes wide, and underneath the concern there is something else — something sharp, something forensic, something that is cataloguing this event even as it happens.

"Neve. You're on the floor. You're in the hallway. You're safe. Can you hear me?"

I can hear her. The words arrive through a long tunnel. My ears are ringing. My vision is blurred. I blink. Saskia's face sharpens. She's watching me with the intensity I've seen in her eyes since we were twelve — the intensity that misses nothing, records everything.

"You were on the floor," she says. "Unresponsive for thirty seconds. What happened?"

I sit up. The room tilts. Saskia's hand stays on my shoulder. I press my palm flat against the floor. The blackbutt is cool and solid and real. Not carpet. Not blue carpet. Timber. Now. July. I am thirty-seven. I am fine.

"I felt dizzy." My voice sounds far away. "I haven't eaten enough. I skipped — I didn't eat much at breakfast."

Saskia watches me. Her hand doesn't move from my shoulder. Her eyes don't move from my face. She is reading me the way she reads witnesses, the way she reads briefs, the way she reads the distance between what people say and what their bodies tell.

"That was more than dizziness," she says.

"It was dizziness. I stood up too fast. I've been dehydrated — I didn't drink enough water this morning. It's simple. Orthostatic hypotension. Blood pressure drops, brain doesn't get enough oxygen, you faint. It's common. It happens."

I am speaking the way I speak to clients in crisis — providing a framework, a label, a box. This is what I do. I name things. Naming gives control. Dizziness. Dehydration. Orthostatic hypotension. Good, clean, clinical words that keep the thing behind the locked door where it belongs.

Saskia doesn't respond. Her hand lifts from my shoulder. She stands. She holds out her hand.

"Can you walk?"

"Yes."

I take her hand. She pulls me up. My legs are steady — or steady enough. The trembling has moved inward, underground, like a river that has disappeared beneath the surface but is still flowing, still eroding, still shaping the landscape from below.

Saskia walks me downstairs. Her hand on my elbow. The fourth step groans under our combined weight. I don't freeze this time. I keep moving. Down. Down. Into the kitchen.

She sits me in a chair. Puts a glass of water in front of me. I drink it. The water is cold and tasteless and it fills my mouth with something real, something present, something that belongs to this moment and not to any other.

"I'll be fine," I say. "I think I just need — a minute. Maybe some water. I'll be fine."

Saskia nods. She says, "Sit. Finish the water." Her voice is level. Her face is level. Her eyes are not level. Under the composure something is burning and I see it because I am trained to see it and because Saskia has never been able to fully hide what she sees.

She walks out of the kitchen. I hear her footsteps on the stairs — measured, precise, ascending. She is going to the hallway. She is going to look at the place where I fell. She is going to read the floor and the walls and the window the way she reads everything — for evidence, for pattern, for the thing that doesn't fit.

I sit in the kitchen. The glass of water is in my hand. My fingers are wrapped around it — tightly, knuckles whitening, the grip of someone holding on to the only solid thing in a room that won't stop tilting.

The kettle clicks on in the other room. Someone is making tea. The house creaks. The wind moves through the eaves. Normal sounds. Normal house sounds. A normal Saturday in a normal kitchen in a normal house where nothing happened and nothing is happening and everything is fine.

I should call Mum. I should call her and hear her voice and let her tell me what she always tells me — that I was asleep, that I was downstairs, that I didn't see anything. The words would settle me. The words have always settled me. A lullaby of negation, twenty-five years of it, enough to fill a childhood, enough to pave over whatever is cracking underneath.

I don't call her.

I sit in the kitchen. I think: *I saw something. In the hallway. Two people. One of them was — no. No, I was downstairs. I was always downstairs. Mum said I was asleep. I was asleep.* I repeat it like a mantra. But for the first time, the mantra doesn't work.

---

# THE FOURTH STEP — Chapter 14: Saskia

## The Report

I leave Neve in the kitchen and climb the stairs.

The upstairs hallway is empty. Grey walls. Blonde floor. The sealed window at the end. I walk to the spot where I found her — four meters from the top of the stairs, one meter from the window. She was lying on her left side, knees drawn up, hands pressed flat against the floor, fingers splayed. Her eyes were open but unfocused. Her skin was grey-white — not the pallor of a faint but the colour of someone whose blood supply has redistributed to where it's needed in a crisis. Core. Brain. Away from the face.

The floor shows no mark. The hallway is good at holding nothing. At being blank.

I look at the window. The sealed window that cannot be opened. Neve was standing here when it happened. Standing in front of the window. Looking at it.

What did you see, Neve?

I file the question. Fifteenth question, updated from fourteen. The fifteenth: what did Neve experience in front of this window that caused her to collapse?

The answer is not dizziness. I have seen witnesses faint. Fainting is specific — loss of consciousness, loss of muscle tone, the body going slack. Neve did not go slack. Neve went rigid. That is not a vasovagal response. That is a trauma response. The body re-experiencing something the mind has been protecting it from.

Something happened to Neve Cassidy in this hallway twenty-five years ago. Something more than being told a friend fell from a window. Something she has not remembered. Or has remembered and been made to forget — the two possibilities are different in every way except the one that matters: the result is the same. A woman who cannot trust her own mind.

I go back downstairs.

My room is the front bedroom. Ground floor. Door locked — I locked it this morning, key in my trouser pocket. I unlock it. Enter. Close the door.

The desk is by the window. I sit. I open the drawer. Small brass lock. I remove the document sleeve. Rigid A4. Archival quality. Acid-free.

I open it.

The police incident report is on top. Two pages, photocopied from the original at the Sorrento police station in 2006. I have read this document forty-seven times. I read it a forty-eighth.

The header: Victoria Police, Incident Report, Sorrento Station, 14 December 1999. Officer: Senior Constable D. Hargreaves, badge 4417. Retired 2008. Declined to speak to me when I contacted him in 2012. "It was a long time ago, Ms. Brennan. Let it go." The third person to tell me that.

I skip the preamble. I skip the witness list. I know the narrative summary by heart: "At approximately 2230 hours on 14/12/1999, police responded to a report of a juvenile fallen from a second-storey window." I read the injury section.

"Minor bruising noted on the upper back and shoulders, bilaterally. Consistent with impact injuries from fall."

I have read this sentence forty-seven times. I read it a forty-eighth.

Minor bruising. Upper back and shoulders. Bilaterally.

I am a criminal defense barrister. I have spent sixteen years reading injury reports. I know what a fall from a second-storey window looks like on a body — catastrophic injuries to the head, torso, and extremities. The upper back and shoulders are not primary impact sites. Secondary impact bruising is unilateral — one side. The side that hits.

Bilateral bruising on the upper back and shoulders means both sides. Evenly distributed. Symmetrical.

This is not a fall pattern. This is a grip pattern.

Hands placed on the shoulders from behind. Fingers wrapping around the upper arms. The grip of someone standing behind a child, pushing her. Force compressing the capillary beds under the skin. Both sides. Symmetrical. Bilateral.

The report says "consistent with impact injuries from fall." Technically defensible. Bilateral shoulder bruising can occur in a fall if the body lands flat, face-down. But Kiera didn't land face-down. She landed on her left side, primary impact to the head and left shoulder. The bruising pattern is inconsistent with the landing position.

Hargreaves noted it and wrote "consistent" and filed it and no one questioned it because no one was looking for a reason to question it. A twelve-year-old girl fell from a window. Tragic. Accidental. No reason to look closer.

I have been looking closer for twenty-four years.

I place the report on the desk. I take out the autopsy summary. One page. "Cause of death: blunt force trauma to the head consistent with fall from height. Manner of death: accidental."

Accidental. The paramedic run sheet — obtained separately — records the body's position: approximately two meters from the base of the wall. Two meters. A child falling backward from a window would land at most half a meter from the wall. Gravity pulls straight down. Two meters requires horizontal force. A push. Not a nudge — arms extended, force applied, the body accelerated backward with enough velocity to travel two meters before the ground interrupts.

The mathematics are not complex. The physics are not ambiguous. A child does not fall two meters from a building without being pushed.

Until now.

Rowan's photographs — the fingernail scratches on the window frame. Five parallel lines. Downward. A child's hand gripping the frame, sliding, losing purchase. Someone held on. Someone was pulled away or pushed away or let go. The scratches are physical evidence. They corroborate the bruising pattern. They corroborate the landing distance. They corroborate what I have believed since I was fourteen years old: Kiera Doyle was pushed.

And Neve Cassidy just collapsed in the hallway, in front of the window, in the exact position where the scratches are, exhibiting a trauma response consistent with recovered memory.

I place the documents back in the sleeve. I close the sleeve. I place the sleeve on the desk. I look at it — the rigid A4 rectangle that contains twenty-four years of waiting.

Tonight. I'll tell them tonight. At dinner, when they're all together — I'll put the report on the table and read the injury section aloud and explain what it means. I'll show Rowan's photographs. The bruising. The landing distance. The scratches. The evidence that Kiera was pushed and the push was covered up and someone in this house knows who did it.

I haven't decided whether to accuse. Accusation requires a target. I have evidence. I have suspicion. I have twenty-four years of investigation that has led to this house and these women and this weekend. The answer is here. I can feel it the way I feel a case turning in court — the shift in the room, the witness about to break, the silence before the admission.

Tonight.

I stand. I walk to the door. I open it.

And I see it.

A manila envelope on the floor. Brown. Standard A4 size. Sealed. It has been slid under my door — the edge nearest the hallway is slightly buckled from the pressure of being pushed through the gap. The gap is less than a centimeter. Whoever slid it had to press firmly.

I pick it up. The envelope is light. Not empty — something inside, something flat. No writing on the outside. No label. No postmark. No name.

Someone delivered this. Someone in this house, or someone who has access to it.

I close my door. I sit on the bed. I open the envelope.

Inside: a single photograph.

I slide it out. Glossy paper. Standard photographic print, twenty by twenty-five centimeters. The image is a crime scene photograph — I recognize the style immediately: the flat, unflattering flash, the wide angle, the forensic framing that captures everything and aestheticizes nothing. This is not a photograph taken for beauty. This is a photograph taken for record.

The image shows the upstairs hallway. Not as it is now — grey walls, blonde floor, sealed window. As it was. Twenty-five years ago. Cream walls. Blue carpet. And at the end of the hallway, the window — the original window, the timber-framed casement, open, swung outward into the December night. The window that Kiera went through.

The photograph is labelled in the lower right corner with a date and a reference number: 14/12/1999. CR-4471-99. This is an official crime scene photograph from the night of Kiera's death. It is not in the file I obtained through freedom of information. The FOI release contained twelve photographs — exterior shots, the lawn, the position of the body. This is from inside the hallway. It was not released.

Someone has a copy of an unreleased crime scene photograph and they slid it under my door while I was in the kitchen with Neve.

I examine the image. The quality is high — this is not a photocopy of a photocopy. This is either the original print or a first-generation reproduction. The flash has illuminated the hallway evenly: cream walls, blue carpet, two doors visible on the left, one on the right. The window at the end, open, the night visible through the gap. The photograph was taken from the top of the stairs, looking down the length of the hallway toward the window.

The flash has created reflections in the window glass. The open casement has a pane of glass in the upper portion — the bottom pane swings open, the top pane is fixed — and in the glass, the flash has captured a reflection of the space behind the camera. The hallway. The staircase. And in the corner of the glass, at the top of the stairs, a figure.

I lean closer. The light in my room is not ideal — grey July afternoon, the window facing east, most of the morning light already gone. I angle the photograph toward the lamp on the desk.

The reflection is small — compressed by the angle and the curvature of the old glass. But it is clear enough. A figure. Standing at the top of the stairs. Behind the photographer, or to the side, in the position where someone would stand if they had just arrived at the top of the stairs and stopped.

The figure is small. Child-sized. The proportions are wrong for an adult — the head-to-body ratio, the height relative to the bannister. A child. A child standing at the top of the stairs, in the hallway, during the crime scene processing, watching. Very still. The stillness of someone who has been told not to move.

This child is not listed in any witness statement as being in the hallway. The witness statements — I've read them all — describe all four surviving girls as being downstairs, in the living room, when the police arrived. All four. Downstairs. Not one of them was reported as being in the upstairs hallway.

But the photograph shows a child in the hallway.

Someone was there. In the hallway. At the top of the stairs. While the crime scene was being processed. Watching. And that someone was not recorded in any official account.

I turn the photograph over. The back is blank. No annotation. No attribution. No indication of where this came from or who had it or how it arrived under my door.

I turn it back. I look at the reflection again. The child at the top of the stairs. The small figure, half-hidden by the reflection of the bannister, standing very still. Standing the way you stand when you're watching something you're not supposed to see. Standing the way Neve was standing when I found her on the floor of this hallway twenty minutes ago.

The photograph came from someone with access to unreleased crime scene materials. Someone who was in this house while I was in the kitchen with Neve. Someone who wanted me to see this specific image at this specific moment. Someone orchestrating this weekend in ways I do not yet understand.

Cate. Cate Morrow, who asks questions like a journalist and slipped on Kiera's name and knew whose house this was before Imogen told her. Cate, who arrived yesterday morning and has been watching all of us with the focus of someone gathering evidence rather than reuniting with friends.

Or someone else. Someone I haven't identified. Someone pulling strings from a distance.

I file the question — seventeenth — and I look at the photograph again.

The child at the top of the stairs. Watching. Not recorded. Not accounted for. Present in the hallway when every official account says she was downstairs.

I think about Neve. I think about her saying "I was downstairs" and "I felt dizzy" and the way her voice flattened when she said it — the way it always flattens when she says things designed to close a conversation rather than open one. I think about her collapsing in front of the window. Not fainting. Collapsing. Her body rigid, fingers splayed, jaw clenched. She was not in this July. She was somewhere else. In a hallway that was cream, not grey. With carpet that was blue, not blonde. With a window that was open.

I think about the reflected figure in the photograph.

I think about Neve.

I pick up my phone. I photograph the photograph — the crime scene image, the reflection, the figure. Then I slide the photograph back into the manila envelope and place it in the document sleeve with the police report and the autopsy summary. I lock the desk drawer.

I sit on the bed. The time is twelve-fourteen. The house is quiet. Neve is in the kitchen. The others are somewhere. And someone in this house has access to evidence that was never released and is distributing it strategically.

Tonight. I will present what I have. The police report. The injury analysis. Rowan's photographs of the scratches. And now this — the crime scene photograph with the reflected figure, the child who was in the hallway when no one was supposed to be in the hallway, the witness who was never recorded.

Tonight. All of it.

I look at the photograph. In the reflection: a figure at the top of the stairs. Someone was there. Someone watching. Someone who isn't in any official account.

---

# THE FOURTH STEP — Chapter 15: Imogen

## The Game

Saturday afternoon and the house has that particular kind of quiet that happens when five women are all in different rooms and none of them are talking to each other. We all needed a bit of space after the morning, after Neve felt unwell. It's okay. She's okay. She said she hasn't eaten enough, which is perfectly reasonable, and Saskia helped her downstairs and got her water and now Neve is in the garden and I am in the kitchen doing what I always do, which is keeping my hands busy.

I've made a pot of tea. I've sliced the remaining banana bread. I've arranged the Tim Tams on a plate — two rows, alternating chocolate and caramel, because presentation matters, especially when no one is watching, because the alternative is sitting still and sitting still in this house means the house starts to press against you. So I arrange biscuits. That's okay. That's a normal thing to do.

Cate appears in the doorway. She's nice. Perfectly nice. A bit quiet. A bit watchful. The way she listens is the way I imagine a therapist listens, except Neve is the therapist, and Cate is just a nice woman who was a couple of years behind us at school and wanted to reconnect. That's all.

"Hey," she says. "How are we doing? You look like you could use another pair of hands — can I help with something?"

"Oh, I'm okay! Totally okay. Do you want tea? There's banana bread, though it's a bit stale, and Tim Tams, obviously." I laugh. The laugh is too bright. "Sorry. It's been a morning, hasn't it?"

Cate sits at the table and wraps her hands around a mug and looks at me with those steady grey-green eyes and smiles and the smile is kind and it's also searching. The way Saskia searches. Everyone in this house is searching for something and I'm the one providing snacks.

"You know what we need?" I say, because the silence is starting to fill up the room. "A game. Something to break the tension. We used to play games all the time, didn't we? At THE FOURTH STEP. We played —"

I stop. The word is out. THE FOURTH STEP. I said it the way you say a name at a funeral — quietly, accidentally, and then immediately wishing you could take it back.

"I mean not that sleepover. I mean sleepovers in general. Birthday parties and —"

"What kind of game?" Cate asks. Her voice is gentle. She's giving me an exit from the sentence I was drowning in.

"I don't know. Cards?" I open drawers until I find them — a deck of cards in the third drawer.

"Cards!" I hold them up.

Cate takes the cards and turns them over in her hands. Her fingers are precise. Examining, not shuffling.

"We could play something more interesting," she says.

"Like what?"

"Truth or Dare."

The words land in the kitchen like a match dropped in dry grass. Truth or Dare. Kiera's game. Her favourite. She was the one who always suggested it — sitting cross-legged in her sleeping bag with the torch under her chin, grinning like she was about to set the world on fire. She always chose truth. She said dares were for people who were afraid of honesty.

"Oh, God, no," I say. I'm laughing again. The laugh is a door slamming. "That's — okay, no. We're thirty-seven. We're not twelve. We can't just — no."

"Why not?" Cate's smile doesn't change. "It's a reunion. We're getting to know each other again."

The back door opens. Neve comes in from the garden, pink-cheeked from the cold. Behind her, Saskia — where did Saskia come from? Was she in the garden too? I didn't see her go out. I don't always see Saskia. She moves through this house like a camera — recording, observing, never in the frame.

"You two are plotting something," Neve says. Not a question. Her voice is steady. Therapist voice. She's recovered from this morning and she's okay and everything is okay.

"Cate wants to play Truth or Dare," I say. I say it the way you say something ridiculous — with a laugh, with an eye-roll, with the expectation that everyone will agree it's silly and we'll play cards instead.

"Oh," Neve says. Just that. Oh. The word is small and neutral and it contains about seventeen things she's not saying.

"Actually," Saskia says, and her voice is the voice she uses in courtrooms — measured, precise, the voice of someone building a case — "that's not a bad idea."

I look at her. She's leaning against the doorframe with her arms crossed and her eyes are doing the thing they do — the way she's been watching all of us since we were kids, like she's taking notes for a test none of us knew we were sitting.

"Saskia," I say. "You hate games."

"I hate wasted time. This isn't wasted time." She looks at the cards in Cate's hand. "Where's Rowan?"

"Upstairs," Neve says. "She's been upstairs most of the day."

"I'll get her." Saskia goes. The kitchen is quiet. I pour tea. Cate shuffles the cards — her fingers still precise, still examining — and Neve sits at the table and wraps her hands around a mug she hasn't drunk from and we are three women in a kitchen on a Saturday afternoon pretending that this is a normal thing that normal people do.

Rowan appears. Saskia behind her. Rowan is always somewhere else, even when she's here. Her eyes are soft, unfocused. Her hair is loose. The silver ring on her right hand. She sits in the chair nearest the wall and waits.

"Truth or Dare," Saskia announces. She sits at the head of the table. She has positioned herself with a clear line of sight to every person in the room. "Imogen's idea."

"My — no, it was Cate's —"

"It doesn't matter whose idea it was," Saskia says. "We're playing."

She takes the cards from Cate. She fans them face-down on the table. "Highest card goes first."

The cards fall. Cate turns over a jack, Neve a six, me a three — typical — Rowan a queen, and Saskia the ace of spades. Of course she does. Saskia always wins the setup.

"Truth or Dare," Saskia says to Cate. "Since you suggested it."

Cate smiles. "Truth."

"What's the one thing you've never told anyone?"

The question lands. Not the way a question lands in a game — light, jokey. The question lands the way a hand lands on a shoulder. Hard. Deliberate. Staying.

Cate doesn't blink. "I'm terrified of the ocean. Ironic, given I grew up near it. I had a nightmare as a child — I dreamt I was pulled under — and I've never been able to swim since." She says it smoothly. Rehearsed, almost. Like a speech she's prepared.

"I love the ocean," I say, because someone has to say something. "Sophie'd live in the water if I let her."

"My turn," Cate says. She looks at Rowan. "Rowan. Truth or Dare?"

"Truth."

"Where is home for you?"

Rowan blinks. The question seems to travel through her slowly, the way light travels through water. "Home," she says. "I don't have one. I have places. Lisbon was gold. Reykjavik was blue. But home — home is a smell. Eucalyptus and salt. I carry it."

"That's not an answer," Saskia says.

"It's the only one I have," Rowan says.

"My turn," Rowan says. She looks at me. "Imogen. Truth or Dare."

"Truth, obviously. I'm not doing a dare, I'm in a cardigan."

"What do you dream about?"

The question is simple. Four words. Rowan asks it the way she does everything — with the precision of someone who sees surfaces and doesn't understand what's beneath them.

"Dreams? Oh, you know. Normal stuff. Sophie, school, work. I dream about forgetting to mark homework. Very boring." I'm talking too fast. "Greg says I talk in my sleep. He says I say 'it's okay' over and over, which, honestly, that's probably accurate."

No one laughs.

"My turn," I say. I grab the moment and I run with it. I look at Neve. "Neve. Truth or Dare."

"Truth." Neve's voice is careful. Measured. She's sitting with her spine straight and her hands flat on the table and she looks like she's bracing for something.

I should ask her something safe. Liam, Lily, her practice — anything that doesn't —

"How's Lily?" I ask. "Sophie's always asking about her. She thinks Lily sounds so cool."

Neve smiles. A real smile. A mum smile. "She's great. She's — she's so tall now. Twelve going on twenty. She asked me the other day if she could have a sleepover and I —"

She stops. Her hands press flat against the table. Her knuckles whiten.

"A sleepover," Saskia says. Not a question. An observation.

"She can have friends over," Neve says. "That's different. That's our house. That's — that's safe."

The word safe hangs in the air. Neve looks at it and her face does something — a flicker, a micro-movement, the kind of thing I wouldn't notice except I've been watching Neve since we were twelve because Neve is the one who keeps things together.

"My turn," Neve says. She turns to Saskia. "Truth or Dare."

"Truth."

Neve leans forward. "Why are you really here, Saskia?"

The question is a challenge. Even Cate leans forward, her body angled toward the answer.

"I'm here because someone sent me an invitation that appeared to come from me. I traced it. Dead end. But I was already going to come. I've been tracking this property for years." She says this flat, factual, like reading from a document. "I'm here because twenty-five years ago a twelve-year-old girl died in this house and I have never been satisfied with the explanation."

The room goes very still.

"Saskia," I say. My voice is thin. "It was an accident. She fell. The window was open and she —"

"Do you think memories are reliable?" Saskia asks. She's looking at me but the question isn't for me. It's for the room.

"What?" Rowan asks.

"Memories. Do you think they're reliable? Do you think what you remember is what actually happened?"

"That's a therapist question," I say, trying to laugh. "Like when you're absolutely sure you locked the door but then you're not sure, and then you're not sure if you're not sure? Is that — is that what you mean?"

Neve doesn't answer. She's looking at Saskia with an expression I can't read — not anger, not fear, something between the two, something that looks like a door being held shut with both hands.

"My turn," Saskia says. Her eyes move from Cate to me to Rowan to Neve. She's not playing anymore. She's been building toward something and I can feel it rising in my chest, the underwater feeling, the pressure, the thing I hold down every day by keeping my hands busy and my voice bright and my thoughts on Sophie and Greg and lesson plans and anything except —

"Truth," Saskia says. "For all of you. One question. Same question."

The kitchen is quiet. The clock on the wall ticks — each tick a small hammer striking the same spot. Five women around a kitchen table on a Saturday afternoon and the air is thick with something I can feel pressing against my skin, pressing against the inside of my throat, pressing against the words I've spent twenty-five years not saying.

"What do each of you remember about the last time we were all in this house?"

The silence is not empty. It's full of the things five women are not saying, the things we've carried for twenty-five years in different architectures of denial and memory and survival. It fills the kitchen like floodwater, rising inch by inch, pressing against the walls, finding the cracks.

I break it. Of course I break it. That's what I do. I break silences the way I break bread — with both hands, with force, before they can break me.

"I don't — okay, I don't see the point of this." My voice is steady. Reasonable. The voice of a woman who has rehearsed deflection for twenty-five years. "We were kids. It was a long time ago. It was awful and we were kids and there's nothing to — I don't know what you want us to say."

Rowan speaks next. Her voice is the voice of someone describing a photograph — accurate, visual, stripped of narrative.

"I remember the cold."

Three words. They land on the table and they don't move.

"That's all?" Cate asks. Her voice is gentle. The kind of gentle that gets people to say things they didn't mean to.

"The cold," Rowan says. "And the light. Gold light. And the sound — between a gasp and a thud. And two shapes. One tall. One small. And then the window was empty."

"You saw two people?" Cate leans forward. "In the hallway?"

"I saw shapes."

Neve stands. She pushes her chair back — not violently, not dramatically, just stands, the way you stand when your body has decided something before your mind has caught up. Her face is white. Not pale. White. The colour of paper. The colour of walls.

"I don't remember anything," she says.

She says it to the room. She says it to Saskia. She says it the way you say something you need to be true — not with conviction but with desperation, with the urgency of someone repeating a life raft.

"That's convenient," Saskia says. Her voice hasn't changed. Still flat. Still precise. Still reading from a document. But her eyes — her eyes are sharp the way a blade is sharp, and they are fixed on Neve with twenty-five years of focus.

"It's not convenient, it's — I was asleep. I was downstairs. I was on the couch and I was asleep and I woke up when I heard — I woke up and everyone was —"

"That's not what happened." Saskia stands. She and Neve are facing each other across the kitchen table and the table is between them like a border, like a line that is about to be crossed. "I was in the bedroom at the end of the hall. The one by the stairs. I heard the sound — the gasp-thud — and I was the first one out. I was the first one into the hallway."

The room holds its breath. Cate is very still. Rowan is watching with her camera-eyes, recording. I am standing too — I don't remember standing — and my hands are at my sides and my palms are pressed flat against my thighs and I can feel my heart in my throat.

"You were in the hallway, Neve." Saskia's voice is low and precise and every word is a nail. "When I came out of my room, you were already standing in the hallway. Under the window. I was the first one out of the bedroom. But you were already there. You were standing under the window looking at me and —"

"No." Neve's voice is sharp. "No, I was downstairs —"

"You were upstairs. You were in the hallway. You were standing exactly where you stood this morning when you collapsed. Under the window. You were looking at me and I stopped and I looked at you and you looked — your face wasn't surprised, Neve. You weren't surprised. You were already terrified. You were terrified before I got there. Like you'd already seen something. Like you'd been standing there long enough to see it."

The kitchen is silent. The clock ticks. The sea moves. Five women and a table and twenty-five years of silence finally cracking.

"I was on the couch," Neve says. Her voice is controlled. Therapist voice. The voice she uses when she is holding herself together with language. "That's where I was. I was on the couch. I fell asleep during the movie and I woke up when I heard the — I woke up and everyone was running upstairs and I followed. That's what happened. That's what my mother told —"

She stops.

She said it. She said the thing she's not supposed to say. "That's what my mother told." Not "that's what I remember." Not "that's what happened." That's what my mother told. The sentence is a doorway and it's open and the air from the other side is cold.

"I was downstairs," Neve says again. Quieter now. Less controlled. "On the couch. That's where I was."

"I was the first one up the stairs after the sound," Saskia says. "You were already in the hallway, Neve. Under the window. Looking at me. And your face — your face wasn't surprised. It was already terrified."

The silence is long. It stretches across the table and fills the kitchen and pushes against every surface in this house that has been holding its breath for twenty-five years.

"I don't remember that," Neve says.

But her voice cracks on "don't."

---

# THE FOURTH STEP — Chapter 16: Rowan

## The Frame

I leave the kitchen. No one stops me. The conversation continues behind me — or it doesn't. I don't hear it. My ears are full of the sound of my own pulse, rhythmic and warm, the sound a body makes when it is moving toward something it has been moving toward for twenty-five years without knowing it.

The hallway. The stairs. The fourth step does not creak anymore — the renovation replaced it, replaced all of them, new timber, new nails, new silence. But the geometry is the same. Ten steps to the turn. Four more. Then the hallway at the top, and the window at the end, and the cold that rises from the floorboards like something buried.

I climb. One step, two, three. The light changes as I rise — the warm yellow of the ground floor falling away, replaced by the thin grey of the upper hallway, the light that comes through the sealed window and falls on the carpet in a narrow blade. I count steps without counting. My body counts. It has been counting for twenty-five years.

The hallway opens in front of me. Five metres of grey carpet. White walls. The window at the end, smaller than it was, fixed in its frame, sealed against the December air that used to move through it like breathing. The cold is the same. The cold has been waiting.

I walk to the window. Twelve steps. My bare feet — I have taken my shoes off without remembering — press into the carpet and the carpet is thin and beneath it the floorboards are cold and beneath them the bones of the house are old and beneath the bones is the thing I have been circling since I was twelve years old.

I crouch in front of the window frame. The aluminum is white and clean and modern. Rubber seals. Double glazing. The architecture of renovation, of erasure, of painting over what was here before and hoping no one looks too closely. But the frame meets the wall and where it meets the wall there is a gap — narrow, no more than a few millimetres, the space between what is new and what is old, between what was installed and what was covered up.

My fingers find the edge of the aluminum. The metal is cold and smooth. I press my fingernail into the gap. The frame shifts — a fraction, a millimetre, enough to reveal what is beneath.

Wood. Old wood. Dark, stained, rough-grained. The original frame. The bones.

And on the bones: scratches.

Five parallel lines. I see them the way I see everything — as light and form and texture, as a composition, as a frame within a frame. The lines are evenly spaced, the width of a child's hand, the width of fingers spread wide. They run downward, top right to bottom left, gouged into the soft timber with a force that is visible — the depth of each line is consistent, one to two millimetres, the depth of fingernails pressing into wood, the depth of someone holding on.

I lift my camera. The motion is automatic. The camera is already in my hands — I don't remember picking it up, but it is there, an extension of my fingers, a way of seeing that does not require understanding. I frame the scratches. I adjust the aperture. I set the ISO high — the light is poor, the gap is narrow, the flash will overexpose. I shoot.

Click. The shutter fires. The image appears on the screen: the old wood, the scratches, five lines in the dark. I shoot again from a different angle. Click. Again, closer. Click. Each photograph is a moment held still, extracted from time, preserved in light and pixel and data. The camera sees what my brain cannot sequence. The camera holds what my memory cannot narrate.

I sit back on my heels. The floor is cold. The hallway is quiet. The window above me is sealed and dark and the moonlight through it falls on my hands and on the camera and on the silver ring on my right hand — Kiera's ring, the moonstone pale and opaque, the color of condensed fog.

And the hallway changes.

Not the hallway. Not this hallway. The other one. The one that lives behind this one, layered beneath the new carpet and the white walls and the aluminum frame like a photograph beneath a photograph, like a negative beneath a print, the original image that all the renovations in the world cannot develop over.

You are standing in the hallway. You are twelve. The carpet is blue and scratchy under your bare feet and the air is warm — December warm, summer warm, salt and eucalyptus and something else, something metallic, something that tastes like copper on your tongue, like blood on a bitten lip, like the taste your mouth makes when fear enters it.

The window at the end of the hallway is open. The brass hinge creaks. The night air comes through in slow waves and it is warm and alive and it moves your hair. The hallway is filled with gold light — December moonlight, thick and golden, pooling on the blue carpet, rising up the cream walls, touching the ceiling. The light is not light. The light is presence. The light is alive.

You hear a sound. Between a gasp and a thud. The sound enters through your feet and climbs your spine and settles at the base of your skull and you are awake, more awake than you have ever been, your eyes wide, your body still, standing in the blue carpet with the gold light on your skin and the warm air on your face.

You look up.

The hallway is twelve steps long. At the end, in front of the open window, two shapes. One tall. One small. The tall one has its back to you. It is blocking the light. The small one is between the tall one and the window and the small one is — the small one is Kiera.

You know this before you know it. Your body knows. Your eyes know. The shape of her, the angle of her shoulders, the way her hair falls, the particular smallness of her — not the smallness of a child but the smallness of Kiera, who was the smallest of all of you, who was light and quick and laughed with her whole body and dared you to eat Vegemite straight from the jar and told ghost stories with a torch under her chin and was the bravest person you knew.

The tall one moves. An arm extends. A hand — an adult hand, large, firm — presses against Kiera's shoulder. Kiera stumbles backward. Toward the window. Toward the open window with its creaking brass hinge and its view of the dark and the warm air and the drop.

And Kiera's hand reaches out.

You see it. You see it the way the camera sees — clearly, precisely, every detail in focus, every texture rendered, the light on her skin, the shape of her fingers, the smallness of her hand. You know this hand. You have held it a hundred times. You have linked fingers with it walking to school. You have pressed your palm against it playing hand games. You have felt its warmth, its grip, the particular strength of a girl who was small and refused to admit it.

Her fingers splay. Her hand reaches for the window frame. Her fingernails catch the paint — the old cream paint on the wooden frame — and they scrape, and the sound is small and specific, the sound of nails on wood, of someone grabbing, of someone holding on. Five fingers. Five lines of pressure. Her knuckles whiten. Her arm extends. Her body leans out over the dark and her hand is the only thing keeping her and the grip is —

The grip is not enough.

The fingers slide. One by one. The pinky first, then the ring finger, then the middle — slow, deliberate, like watching something fall in slow motion, like a photograph being taken at a shutter speed so low that motion becomes visible as blur, as streak, as the trail of a thing moving from one place to another. The hand slides down the frame. The fingernails leave lines — five parallel lines, downward, gouged into the wood, the last marks of a person holding on.

And then the hand is gone.

The fingers open. The hand disappears through the window. The frame is empty. The air where Kiera was is just air. The space where her fingers gripped is just wood with scratches.

You are standing in the hallway. You are twelve. The gold light is on the carpet and the warm air is on your face and the window is open and the frame is empty and somewhere below you — below the window, below the dark, below the warm December air — there is a sound. Not the gasp-thud. Something after. Something softer. Something the grass makes when it receives something it was never meant to hold.

And then silence. The kind of silence that fills a space so completely that it becomes a presence, a thing in the room, a sixth person in the hallway, standing with you, watching.

I am back.

I am thirty-seven. I am crouching on the carpet in the upstairs hallway of a renovated holiday house in Sorrento in July and my fingers are pressing into the gap between the aluminum frame and the old wood and the scratches are beneath my fingertips, real, physical, present, and my camera is in my other hand and the screen shows the photographs I just took — five lines in the dark, five lines of grip, five lines of someone holding on and losing.

I form the memory into a sentence. For the first time in twenty-five years, I have a sentence. Not a sensation. Not a temperature or a quality of light or a sound between sounds. A sentence with a subject and an object and a verb.

Kiera's hand reached for the frame. Kiera's fingernails scraped the wood. Kiera's fingers slid. Kiera fell.

No. Not fell. The word is wrong. The word has always been wrong. Fell is what happens when you lose your balance. Fell is what happens when the ground beneath you gives way. Kiera did not fall. Kiera was moving toward the window because something moved her. The tall shape moved and then Kiera moved and the direction was not her direction. The direction was the tall shape's direction.
