I have a sentence now. I have more than a sentence. I have a sequence. The fragments have found their order. Not a feeling anymore. A sequence. Before. During. After. The tall shape. The hand. The push. The reach. The grip. The slide. The empty frame.

I stand. My knees ache — the ache of cold pressed into bone and held there. I have been crouching longer than I realized. The hallway is cold. The window is sealed. The light through it is grey and thin and falls on the carpet in a narrow blade that divides the hallway into before and after.

I look at the photographs on my camera. Five parallel lines. The depth and spacing of a child's fingernails. Downward. Someone held on. Someone was pulled away.

I go downstairs.

The kitchen is different now. Neve is not here. Cate is not here. The table where we sat is empty except for the deck of cards, still fanned where Saskia dealt them, and five mugs in various states of emptiness. The overhead light is on — it wasn't before. The afternoon has darkened while I was upstairs. The light through the kitchen window is the colour of iron, winter-grey, the sky pressing low over the coast.

Saskia is standing at the counter with her back to me, pouring water from the kettle into a cup. She hears me and turns. Her face is composed — the measured surface she presents to the world, to courtrooms, to witnesses, to the women she has been observing for two days. But her eyes are sharp and they find me the moment I enter and they read something in my posture, my hands, the camera I am holding the way you hold something that needs to be given away.

"Rowan."

"Look at this." I hold up the camera. "I went back to the frame. I pried it back and I photographed underneath. The original wood. The scratches are there. Clear. I have photographs."

Saskia sets the kettle down. She crosses to the table. She pulls out a chair for me and I sit and I turn the camera toward her and I show her.

She takes the camera. She holds it the way she holds everything — with precision, with intent, her fingers careful on the body, her eyes moving across the screen with the systematic attention of someone reading a document. She scrolls through the photographs. She zooms. She zooms again.

"The spacing," she says. "Consistent with a child's hand. The depth — one to two millimetres. Fingernails. Living wood — the timber was soft enough to hold the marks."

She doesn't ask if I'm sure. She doesn't question the source. She reads the photographs the way she reads everything — as evidence, as data, as a fact that exists independent of the person presenting it. Her jaw is tight. A muscle in her cheek moves once, twice, then is still.

"I saw her hand," I say.

Saskia looks up from the camera.

"Kiera's hand. Reaching for the frame." The sentence is formed now and it will not un-form. It is a permanent structure in my mind, built from twenty-five years of fragments, finally assembled into architecture. "The fingernails scraping. The grip. And then — the fingers slid. One by one. She held on and she couldn't hold."

Saskia sets the camera on the table. Her face is still. The kind of still that is not absence of feeling but containment of it — a dam holding, the water rising, the concrete holding.

"Rowan," she says. "Wait here."

She leaves the kitchen. I hear her footsteps in the hallway. I hear a door open — the downstairs bedroom, Neve's room. A drawer. A pause. Then footsteps returning.

She comes back with a manila envelope.

She sits across from me. She places the envelope on the table between us. She opens it. She takes out documents — folded, worn, handled many times, the creases soft and deep. She takes out a photograph.

Saskia takes out the police report. And the crime scene photograph. She spreads them on the kitchen table next to Rowan's photographs of the scratches. Two sets of evidence, twenty-five years apart, telling the same story.

---

# THE FOURTH STEP — Chapter 17: Neve

## What I Saw

The house is louder when it's empty. The silence has a texture — not absence of sound but the sound of a place listening to itself.

Saskia and Rowan left twenty minutes ago. I watched from the window as they walked down the drive and turned left toward the coastal path, heads close, voices too low to hear, moving with the purpose of two people who have agreed — without saying so — that whatever they're discussing cannot happen inside these walls. Imogen is in her room. Another anxiety pill, she said, her voice thin, her hands trembling. "I just need to rest my brain for a bit, okay?" I said of course. Cate drove to the village. Supplies. She took her jacket and her handbag and her calm, measured smile and the house exhaled when the engine faded.

Four PM on a Saturday in July. Grey light through the windows, the colour of dishwater. The particular stillness of a house that has been full of noise and is full of noise no longer. The heating clicks. The pipes settle. The floorboards groan in places that have nothing to do with weight — the sounds of an empty house contracting around its own silence. Physics. Temperature. Timber expanding and contracting. The sounds of an empty house are not haunted. They are thermodynamic. I have explained this to clients a hundred times.

I'm on the sofa with a book. A literary thriller about a woman who discovers her husband is not who he says he is. Safe. Escapist. Unrelated to anything real.

I can't read.

The same paragraph four times. The words enter my eyes and slide through without sticking. The book could be anything. It could be blank pages. I am not reading. I am listening to Saskia's voice on repeat, a recording I can't turn off, playing the same seven words over and over:

*You were already in the hallway, Neve. You were looking at me. And your face wasn't surprised. It was already terrified.*

Already terrified. Not becoming terrified. Not discovering something terrible. Already. The fear was established. Already means I had seen something before Saskia arrived. Already means I was standing in that hallway for a reason and the reason was already inside me, already known, already processed, already — I was downstairs. I was on the couch. I was asleep. My mother says I slept through the whole holiday. I was twelve. I was a child. Children sleep. Children fall asleep in cars and on couches and I was asleep, I was asleep, I was —

I close the book.

I stand up.

The movement is sudden. Unconsidered. The book slides to the floor. I don't pick it up. I'm standing in the living room looking at the doorway that leads to the hallway that leads to the staircase.

I have been in this house nearly twenty-four hours and I have been upstairs once. Yesterday. The hallway. The window. The flash — not a memory, I told Saskia, dizziness, low blood sugar — and then I was on the floor and she was beside me and I said I was fine because I am always fine because fine is the wall I built between myself and everything I cannot feel.

I am not fine.

The thought arrives without permission. Clear and simple and terrible. I am not fine. I have not been fine since I was twelve years old. The wall is cracking. I can feel the hairline fractures running through it — the same kind of fracture that split my coffee mug in the garden, invisible until the pressure became too much and the ceramic broke in my hands.

I walk toward the hallway.

My feet know the way. They knew the brick under the new concrete. They knew the bluestone step. They knew the pitch of the floorboards. My body has been navigating this house by a map my mind refuses to read, and now my feet are moving and my mind is not stopping them and the staircase is rising ahead of me and I can feel it — the pull, the gravity, the weight of the upper floor drawing me upward.

I put my hand on the newel post. The wood is smooth — renovated, sanded, sealed. But the height is the same. The angle is the same. The way my hand fits around it is the same. My palm knows the curve of this post the way my palm knows the shape of my daughter's hand.

I climb.

First step. Second. Third.

The fourth step groans.

That sound. That specific, weighted, resonant groan. My pulse kicks hard — throat, wrists, behind my eyes. The somatic response is immediate and disproportionate. The body keeps its own records.

I do not stop.

Fifth step. Sixth. Half-landing. Three more. The upper hallway opens ahead.

Grey walls. New carpet. Overhead light off. The only illumination comes from the window at the end — fixed-pane, sealed shut, white aluminum frame. The window that used to be large and open, letting in salt air and moonlight and the warm breath of a December night.

I stand at the top of the stairs.

The hallway stretches ahead. Five meters. The grey walls are not the colour they were. They were cream. Cream with a dark wood dado rail. The carpet was not grey low-pile. It was blue. Deep blue, the blue of summer twilight, the blue of — I know this. I know the colour of the carpet. I know the colour of the walls. I know this hallway and I have known it since I was twelve years old and I have been pretending not to know for twenty-five years.

I walk.

The floorboards groan softly. The ceiling is the same height. The doors are in the same positions — bathroom on the left, two bedrooms on the right. My feet know the exact number of steps to the window. Twelve. I am counting without deciding to count. I reach the window on twelve.

Here.

The window.

Smaller now. Sealed. Fixed-pane. The aluminum frame reflects grey afternoon light. Someone made it smaller. Made it fixed. Made it so it cannot open. As if the window itself was a danger that needed to be contained.

I put my hand on the frame.

The new frame sits on the old woodwork. Aluminum edge, and underneath, the original timber. The wood is still here. Under the paint, under the renovation, the original frame is still here. My fingers trace the edge. And on the wood, under the overhang of the aluminum — scratches. Thin lines. Parallel. I don't need to see them. Rowan showed the photographs. Five lines. Downward. Fingernails.

I press my palm flat against the old wood.

My vision narrows.

The periphery dims. Grey walls darken. The carpet deepens — grey giving way to blue, the low pile growing thick and soft and the blue of summer twilight. The sealed fixed-pane dissolves and the window is large, wide, open, and the air coming through is December-warm and it carries salt and eucalyptus and underneath those, underneath the salt and the green smell of summer, something metallic, something my body knows the way my body knows the pitch of the fourth step.

The hallway is warm.

The hallway is warm and I am twelve.

I am standing at the top of the stairs. Not at the window — at the top of the stairs. I came up. I followed. I was on the couch. I was supposed to be asleep. But I wasn't asleep and I heard something — a voice, sharp, female, from upstairs — and I climbed the stairs and the fourth step groaned under my weight and I am standing at the top of the stairs and the hallway is ahead of me.

Cream walls. Blue carpet. Bathroom door on the left. The window at the end, large and open, warm night air pouring through. The moon is full. The hallway is bright. Silver-blue. Everything visible.

Two figures at the end of the hallway.

One is tall. A woman. Her back is to me. Standing close to the window. Her silhouette is sharp against the moonlight — tall, commanding, the posture of someone used to being the tallest person in any room. Her arm is extended. Her hand is on the shoulder of the smaller figure.

The smaller figure is Kiera.

I know it's Kiera before I know how I know it. The shape of her. The dark hair loose around her shoulders. The small frame — twelve years old, but small for her age, always the shortest, the one who stood on her toes when she wanted to be eye-level. Her face is turned half toward me and half away. Not fear yet. Confusion. The look of a child who has been told to do something by an adult and doesn't understand why.

The woman's arm moves.

One movement. Not a swing. Not a strike. A push. Deliberate. The palm flat against Kiera's shoulder. The force travels from the woman's arm through her hand into Kiera's body and Kiera stumbles backward. One step. Two. Her arms come up. Her hands reach for the window frame. I see her fingers spread. I see her fingernails — small, painted, chipped blue polish from earlier that day when she did all our nails — catch the paint. Five lines. Downward. The nails drag across the wood.

And then Kiera is gone.

Through the window. Into the dark. One moment she is here — fingers on the frame, arms reaching, body leaning into the opening — and the next she is not. The window is empty. The frame is empty. The air where she was is empty. There is a sound from outside, from below, from the garden, and it is the sound of something heavy hitting something soft and the sound is wet and final and it is the last sound Kiera Doyle will ever make.

I do not scream.

I stand at the top of the stairs and my hands are at my sides and my mouth is open and nothing comes out because my body has understood something my mind has not caught up to yet and what my body has understood is that the woman at the window is not a stranger. The woman at the window is someone I know. Someone I have known my whole life.

The woman turns.

She sees me.

Her face — I see her face and my mind shatters. Not breaks. Shatters. Into fragments too small to reassemble. Because the face is known. The face is the first face I ever knew. The face that leaned over my cot. The face that kissed my grazed knees. The face that told me to be brave, to be careful, to be good. The face that taught me to hold a knife and fold a shirt and answer a question without answering it. The face I have looked at every day of my life for twelve years and trusted absolutely and without question because she is my mother and mothers do not — mothers don't — she is —

Mum.

She crosses the hallway in three steps.

She doesn't run. She moves with speed but not panic. Controlled. Purposeful. The movement of a woman who has assessed a situation and decided on a response and is executing that response with the efficiency she brings to everything. She reaches me. She kneels. She is taller than me and she kneels so our faces are level and her hands are on my face. Her palms are warm. Her palms are steady. She holds my face the way she holds it when I have a nightmare, the way she holds it when she wants me to listen, the way she holds it when she is about to tell me something important.

Her eyes are steady.

Her voice is steady.

She says: "You didn't see anything."

She says: "You were asleep."

She says: "You were downstairs."

Her thumbs stroke my cheekbones. Her voice is low and calm and warm and it is the tone I have trusted since the moment I was born. "You didn't see anything, sweetheart. You were asleep on the couch. You were downstairs. You didn't see anything." She says it and says it and the words press into me, fill the space where the memory is forming and crowd it out and replace it with something else — a new shape, a new story, the story of a girl who was asleep, who was downstairs, who saw nothing.

She holds my face.

She holds the story.

She holds both.

I open my eyes.

I am on the floor of the hallway.

Not trembling. Still. Very still. My back is against the wall under the window. My legs are extended. My palms are flat on the carpet — the grey carpet, the carpet that was blue. The floor is solid. The wall is solid. The window is above me and I can feel the frame — the old wood, the original wood, the wood her fingers touched, the wood my mother's hand touched when she pushed —

I am not trembling.

The way you are still after an earthquake. The shaking has stopped but the world has rearranged itself and nothing is where it was and you can't trust the floor to be flat or the walls to be vertical because everything has shifted and the shift is permanent.

I know what I saw.

The thought is the quietest thought I have ever had. Not loud. Not emotional. It sits in the centre of my mind the way a stone sits in the palm — with a weight that does not require announcement. Not "I think." Not "maybe." Not "I'm confused." I know. The way I know my own name. The way I know my daughter's face. The way I know the sound of the fourth step.

My mother pushed Kiera.

My mother's hand on Kiera's shoulder. My mother's arm extending. My mother's palm pushing forward with enough force to send a twelve-year-old girl stumbling backward through an open window. My mother turning and seeing me and crossing the hallway in three steps and kneeling and holding my face and telling me I didn't see anything. My mother. Elaine. Principal. Wife. Mother. The woman who called the ambulance. The woman who held me while I cried. The woman who told the police and the doctors and the psychologists and me, over and over and over for twenty-five years, that I was asleep. That I was downstairs. That I didn't see anything.

She made me forget.

Not made me forget. Made me replace. She didn't erase the memory. She overwrote it. She held my face and spoke the new version into me and spoke it again — every nightmare, every flash of something that felt like remembering, every time I said "I think I saw—" and she said "You couldn't have. You were on the couch. You were fast asleep" — every time was another layer of overwrite, another coat of paint over the original, another brick in the wall at the back of my mind that was never mine, that she built and made me think was mine.

She took me to psychologists. Sat in the sessions. Told them I was anxious. Told them I had an active imagination. Told them I was confused. The word "confused" delivered with such gentleness it felt like love. They treated me for anxiety because I was anxious, of course I was anxious, my nervous system was screaming the truth at me and she was standing between me and the truth, translating the scream into a diagnosis.

She moved us. Sold the house — the house with the fingernail scratches on the window frame. New school. Cut contact with the other families. Isolated me from the only people who might have contradicted her. And then the long, patient work. The daily calls. The concern. "I'm worried about you, Neve. You seem confused." The intermittent warmth — hugs that felt real, pride that felt earned. The warmth that made the gaslighting impossible to identify. Because how could someone who loves you this much be lying? She loves me. She has always loved me. The love is real. The gaslighting is real. Both are true and they always have been and that is what makes it unforgivable.

My body has known for twenty-five years. The chronic neck tension that no physiotherapist could ease — from a muscle that wouldn't unclench against a memory it wasn't allowed to release. The migraines that started at thirteen, arriving like clockwork every time the forgetting threatened to crack. The IBS that flared under stress and the stress was always there, a low hum of wrongness I attributed to genetics and personality the way you attribute a sound in the walls to the house settling because the alternative is something alive inside them. The way I couldn't look at open windows. The way I couldn't sleep with curtains open. The way I called my mother every day and said "I'm fine" and she said "Good" and the conversation was over because the conversation was never about how I was. It was about her checking. Checking that the wall was holding. Checking that the locked room was still locked. Checking that the story she built inside me was intact. Did I choose my career? Did I choose Melbourne? I have never lived more than twenty minutes from her. Every major decision — was it mine? Was any of it mine? I called it closeness. I called it love. And it was love. But it was also — was it also — I can't tell the difference. She taught me not to.

The question isn't what happened anymore. The question is: my mother killed Kiera, and she made me forget.

My phone rings.

Sharp. Sudden. A blade through the quiet of the hallway. I pull it from my pocket and the screen is bright in the dim and my hand knows the shape of this intrusion before my eyes read the name.

Mum.

I look at the name. The phone rings and rings. Seven seconds. Eight. Nine. I don't answer. I watch her name pulse on the screen. Mum. Elaine. The woman who knelt in front of me and rewrote my mind. The woman who told me not to come here. *You don't have to do this, sweetheart. Sometimes it's better to leave things where they are.* Reverse psychology, or a genuine attempt to protect me, or both. She loves me and she did this to me and both of those things are true.

The phone stops.

The screen goes dark.

Then it lights again. A text.

*Call me, sweetheart. We need to talk about this weekend.*

I stare at the text.

The words are ordinary. Maternal. Concerned. But I am reading them with new eyes. With the eyes of the girl who stood at the top of the stairs and watched her mother push her friend through a window. *We need to talk about this weekend.* Not "How is it going?" Not "Are you having a nice time?" We need to manage it. We need to control what happens next, the way we have controlled everything.

She knew about this weekend. The invitation came from Saskia's address but who sent it, really? Who knew about this house, these women, this weekend? Who has been feeding information — a crime scene photograph under a door, an anonymous tip — who has been pulling strings from a bed in a palliative care unit with the same precision she used to build the cover-up?

She knew I would come back. She knew the hallway was the same. She knew the frame was underneath. She knew the scratches were still there. She knew what would happen when I stood in this spot and put my hand on this wood. She knew the memory would come back. She knew I would remember. And she let it happen — or she needed it to happen, or she couldn't stop it anymore, or she wanted to be here when it did, to hold my face one more time and tell me what I saw. The text glows on the screen. *Call me, sweetheart.* The word "sweetheart" — the same word she used in the hallway twenty-five years ago, kneeling, her hands on my face. The word that has always meant love and has always meant control. I understand now that those two meanings were never separate.

I stare at the text. *You knew. You knew about this weekend. You knew I'd remember.* I put the phone face-down on the floor and the screen light leaks around the edges and the dark closes over it like water over a stone dropped into a pond and I sit in the dark with the weight of everything she did and everything she made me not see pressing against my ribs from the inside.

---

# THE FOURTH STEP — Chapter 18: Saskia

## Revelation

The timing is deliberate. Six PM. The light outside is fading — grey July dusk pressing against the windows, the sky the colour of wet concrete, the last of the afternoon draining from the coast like water from a basin. Cate left twenty minutes ago. Phone call, she said. Reception is better at the end of the street. I watched her walk down the drive and turn toward the coastal path, handbag over her shoulder, step measured, unhurried. She did not look back.

I don't know where Cate goes when she leaves this house. I don't know who she calls. I have questions — the seventeenth through the twenty-third, updated hourly — but they will wait. What matters now is the room and the women in it.

I have arranged the living room. Not decorated. Arranged. The low wooden table in the centre has been cleared of Imogen's banana bread and the Tim Tams and the almonds lined up in their precise row. In their place: the document sleeve. Rigid A4. Archival quality. Acid-free. The contents are laid flat on the surface — the police incident report, two pages, photocopied from the original at the Sorrento police station. The autopsy summary, one page. The crime scene photograph, one print, glossy, twenty by twenty-five centimetres. And Rowan's photographs of the window frame, printed from her camera at the highest resolution the machine could manage, showing the scratches in the original timber beneath the aluminum.

The evidence array. I have spent twenty-four years assembling it. It fits on a coffee table. A girl's death reduced to documents that could be slipped into a manila envelope and mailed. The disproportion is obscene.

The light in the room is overhead — a single pendant with a linen shade, casting diffused yellow light that softens edges and flattens surfaces. Not ideal. Courtrooms have better lighting. Fluorescent, harsh, unforgiving — the kind of light that shows you what is actually there. This light is kinder. I don't want kind. I want clear. But this is a living room, not a courtroom, and I will work with what I have.

Rowan sits in the chair with her back to the wall. Her camera is in her lap. She is watching the table the way she watches everything — as light and form and composition, as a frame within a frame. But her jaw is set and her fingers rest on the camera body with a stillness I have not seen in her before. She is waiting. Not in her usual way — adrift in sensation, present but elsewhere. She is present. Fully present. The fragments are assembling behind her eyes.

Neve is on the sofa. She arrived from upstairs ten minutes ago and she is different. The composure she has performed since she walked through the front door on Friday is gone. Not cracked. Gone. Her face is pale and her hands are in her lap and she is looking at the documents on the table and she is not looking away. She knows something. I don't know what. She came down white-faced and silent and when I asked if she was all right she said "Yes" in a voice that contained no information. A one-word answer designed to close rather than open.

But her eyes are different. The flat, guarded gaze she has maintained all weekend has depth now. Something moving behind it. Something held underwater for twenty-five years that has finally broken the surface.

Imogen is in the kitchen. I can hear her — the clink of a mug, the rattle of a teaspoon, the small domestic percussion she uses to fill silence. She has been in the kitchen since I told them I wanted to talk. She heard "talk" and her hands started moving and they have not stopped.

"Imogen. In here."

She appears in the doorway. Her smile is small and uncertain. The wide, warm, everyone's-favourite-teacher smile is not available tonight. She has a mug in both hands — tea, not wine. Both palms wrapped around the ceramic as if it is the only solid thing in the room.

"Sit," I say.

She sits. Next to Neve. The mug between her palms. She is holding it the way you hold something that keeps your hands from doing something else.

The room settles. Four women. One table. Twenty-five years.

Outside, the wind has picked up. I can hear it against the windows — a low pressure sound, the kind that makes houses creak. The coastal wind in July, cold and constant, carrying salt from the darkened sea. Inside, the heating hums. The overhead light is steady. The documents are arranged in a precise row: report, summary, photograph, scratch images. Ordered. Sequential. The way evidence should be presented. Lead the witness from fact to fact until the conclusion becomes inescapable.

I stand. I don't sit for this. Standing gives me control of the room — the physical authority of someone presenting a case. I have done this in courtrooms a hundred times. The document is the witness. I am the voice.

"I've been waiting twenty-four years to have this conversation," I say. "Some of you know parts of what I'm about to show you. Rowan knows about the scratches on the window frame. Neve —" I pause. "Neve knows something happened in this house that was not what we were told."

I look at Neve. She is watching me. Her face is very still. She does not deny it.

"In 2004, I went to the Sorrento police station and asked to see the incident report from December 14, 1999. I was fourteen. They told me to let it go. Two years later, I obtained a copy through a police contact. I have carried it since."

I pick up the first page of the police report. The paper is soft with handling, the photocopy dark with age. The header reads: Victoria Police, Incident Report, Sorrento Station, 14 December 1999. Officer: Senior Constable D. Hargreaves, badge 4417.

I hold it up.

"The report describes the response to a juvenile fallen from a second-storey window. The narrative summary states: 'At approximately 2230 hours on 14/12/1999, police responded to a report of a juvenile fallen from a second-storey window.' The cause was recorded as accidental. The conclusion was that Kiera Doyle leaned out of an open window and fell."

I turn to the second page.

"The injury notation."

I read it aloud. Slowly. Precisely. Each word given the weight it deserves.

"'Minor bruising noted on the upper back and shoulders, bilaterally. Consistent with impact injuries from fall.'"

I set the page on the table.

"I am a criminal defense barrister. I have spent sixteen years reading injury reports. I know what a fall from a second-storey window does to a body. Primary impact sites: head, torso, extremities. Catastrophic injuries. The upper back and shoulders are not primary impact sites. And bruising from a fall is unilateral. One side. The side that hits the ground."

I pause. Imogen's hands have stopped moving on her mug.

"'Bilaterally' means both sides. Evenly distributed. Symmetrical. This is not a fall pattern. This is a grip pattern. Hands placed on the shoulders from behind. Fingers wrapping around the upper arms. The force of a push compressing the capillary beds under the skin. Both sides. Symmetrical. Bilateral."

I let the word sit in the air. Bilateral. Clinical. Precise. A word that conceals what it describes. I have seen this kind of concealment before. The way language can be technically accurate and fundamentally false at the same time. "Consistent with" — the favourite phrase of a lazy report. Everything is consistent with everything until you look closely enough to see what it's consistent with.

"The autopsy recorded the cause of death as blunt force trauma to the head, consistent with a fall from height. Manner of death: accidental." I turn the page. "The paramedic run sheet — obtained separately — records the body's position as approximately two metres from the base of the wall."

I set it down.

"Two metres. A child falling backward from a window lands at most half a metre from the wall. Gravity pulls straight down. Two metres requires horizontal force. A push. Not a nudge. Arms extended, force applied, a child's body accelerated backward with enough velocity to travel two metres before the ground stopped it. That is not a fall. That is a launch."

I pick up the crime scene photograph. The one that arrived under my door in the manila envelope. I hold it facing the room.

"This photograph was taken the night Kiera died. It shows the upstairs hallway as it was. Cream walls. Blue carpet. The original window — timber frame, top-hung casement, open, swung outward into the December night."

I turn the photograph so they can see the image.

"In the upper pane of the window, the flash has captured a reflection. A figure. Standing at the top of the stairs. Child-sized. Watching. Still."

Imogen is shaking. A fine tremor that moves through her hands and into the mug and makes the tea ripple against the ceramic. Her lips are parted. Her eyes are fixed on the photograph.

Rowan is still. Her gaze is on the reflection in the glass and her expression is one I have not seen before — not fragmented, not adrift in sensation, not lost in light and texture. Focused. The focus of someone who has found the missing piece and is watching it slot into place.

Neve is staring at the photograph. And I see it. The moment she registers the reflection. Not recognition of what — recognition of who. The blood leaves her face in a second wave and her pupils dilate and her jaw tightens and for one brief instant her eyes close. Not a blink. A shutter closing. The deliberate suppression of an image she cannot afford to acknowledge.

She knows who the figure is. She is not saying.

I file the observation. Question twenty-four: Why is Neve protecting the identity of the child in the reflection?

I don't press it. Not yet. The evidence first. The questions later.

"The child in this reflection is not listed in any witness statement. All four surviving girls were recorded as being downstairs when police arrived. But this photograph shows a child in the upstairs hallway. At the top of the stairs. During the crime scene processing. Someone was there. Someone who was never accounted for."

I place the photograph on the table. Next to the police report. Next to the autopsy summary. Next to Rowan's scratch photographs. The evidence array is complete: the injury pattern that proves a push, the landing distance that proves force, the scratches that prove Kiera gripped the frame, and the photograph that proves someone was in the hallway who wasn't supposed to be there.

I look at the array. Twenty-four years. The numbers on the pages have not changed. The words have not changed. The only thing that has changed is the room and the women in it and the fact that I am finally, finally saying the words out loud to the people who need to hear them.

"The scratches Rowan found on the original window frame corroborate the bruising pattern. Five parallel lines. Downward. The depth and spacing of a child's fingernails. Someone held on. Someone was pulled away. The scratches are physical evidence that Kiera did not simply fall. She reached for the frame. She gripped it. She lost her grip."

I pick up one of Rowan's photographs. Hold it up next to the injury description from the police report. Two documents. Two sets of evidence. Telling the same story.

"You can see the depth," I say. "These are not surface marks. These are gouges. Fingernails driven into wood with the force of a child trying to stop herself from going backward. The angle is top-right to bottom-left — consistent with a right hand reaching up and gripping. The marks are on the inside of the frame, the side facing into the hallway. Not the outside. She was holding on from inside. She was trying to stay inside."

I set the photograph down.

"The scratches are the last thing Kiera touched. The last physical evidence of her alive. And they prove she did not go through that window willingly."

I look at each of them. Imogen. Rowan. Neve. Three faces, three silences, three reactions. Imogen trembling. Rowan focused. Neve white and still and closed.

"The report says 'consistent with impact injuries from fall.' Hargreaves noted the bruising, wrote 'consistent,' and filed it. No one questioned it. A twelve-year-old girl fell from a window. Tragic. Accidental. Case closed."

I pause. The room is holding its breath.

"Kiera did not fall."

The words land. Quiet and precise. Stones in still water.

"Someone pushed Kiera."

I look at Neve. I look at Imogen. I look at Rowan.

"And someone in this house knows who."

The silence is not the silence of an empty room. It is the silence of a room full of people who have stopped breathing. Imogen's mug trembles. Rowan's fingers curl around her camera. Neve's face is a mask — white, rigid, measured — and behind the mask I can see the architecture of something that has been holding for twenty-five years, and the architecture is failing.

I stand in the silence. I let it hold. I do not fill it. Silence is where truth lives and I have been waiting twenty-four years for this truth and I will not rush it.

I think of Sergeant Bill Tran, who owed my father a favour and handed me these pages across a desk in 2006 and said, "Are you sure about this, love?" I think of the fourteen-year-old girl who walked into the Sorrento police station and was told to let it go. I think of the forty-seven readings, the handwritten timeline updated twenty-three times, the fourteen-month freedom-of-information battle, the photograph of Kiera running at a school sports day. I think of all of it compressed into this moment — four women, a coffee table, and the sound of nobody breathing.

From the hallway, a sound. The front door opening. Footsteps on the floorboards — light, even, unhurried. A pause.

Cate stands in the doorway of the living room. Her handbag is over her shoulder. Her coat is buttoned. Her face is calm and composed and she is looking at the documents spread across the table — the police report, the autopsy summary, the crime scene photograph with the reflected figure, the scratch photographs — and she heard it. The last sentence. I can see it in her eyes, which have moved from calm to alert in the space between one breath and the next, a shift so fast it is almost imperceptible.

She stands in the doorway. She does not enter. She does not leave. She heard me say it: someone in this house knows who.

And the room holds its breath around her.

---

# THE FOURTH STEP — Chapter 19: Imogen

## The Voice

The mug is empty.

I look down at it and it is empty and I don't remember drinking the tea. The tea I made to keep my hands busy, the tea I wrapped both palms around because the ceramic was warm and solid and real and everything else in this room is not real, nothing about this room is real, nothing about this weekend is — I set the mug on the table. Carefully. Both hands. The way you set down something you're afraid of dropping.

The documents are still there. Saskia's documents. The police report. The autopsy summary. The photograph with the reflection — the small figure at the top of the stairs, child-sized, watching, and I don't know who it is but the shape of it is familiar in a way that makes my stomach fold. Rowan's photographs of the scratches. Five lines. Fingernails. Someone holding on.

I keep looking at the mug because the mug is empty and safe and I understand empty mugs. I understand mugs and tea and the way a kitchen smells when you're making something warm for people you care about. I understand the domestic machinery of caring — the stirring, the pouring, the "here you go, love, it's nice and hot." I understand these things because they are the scaffolding I have built my entire life around, the small physical rituals that prove the world is orderly and children are safe and accidents are random and everything is okay.

Everything is not okay.

Saskia said "bilateral" and I didn't know what it meant and then she explained it and I understood and the understanding was like a hand reaching inside my chest and closing around something I had been protecting for twenty-five years. Grip marks. Not fall injuries. Grip marks. Hands on shoulders. Push.

Someone pushed Kiera.

The words are in the room now. Saskia said them and they are in the air and they are not leaving. They have moved in. They are permanent. Someone pushed Kiera and the mug in my hands is empty and I am thirty-seven years old and I am sitting in a renovated holiday house in Sorrento and the world I built — the orderly, warm, well-lit world where I teach Year Six and bake banana bread and pack Sophie's dance bag and say "it was an accident" with the practiced ease of someone who has said it ten thousand times — that world is a lie.

The mug is on the table. Empty. I don't remember setting it down.

Neve is beside me on the sofa. She hasn't spoken since Saskia finished. Her face is white. Not the paleness of someone who is unwell — the whiteness of someone who has seen something they cannot un-see. She is staring at the documents and her hands are in her lap and they are very still. Neve's hands are never still. She grips pen clips and chair arms and her own wrists. But right now her hands are flat on her thighs and they are not moving and the stillness is wrong, it is the stillness of a person who is holding themselves together with so much force that nothing can move, not a finger, not an eyelash, nothing.

Rowan is in the armchair. She is holding her camera but not photographing. Her eyes are on the table — on the evidence — and they are clear. Present. For the first time all weekend she looks like someone who is not lost in fragments. She looks like someone who has found the thread and is pulling.

Cate is in the doorway. She came in from outside and she heard Saskia's last sentence and she sat in the other armchair — the one near the bookshelf, away from the table — and she has not spoken. She is watching. Calm, still, her face arranged in an expression of concern that is perfectly calibrated. It is a good expression. It is the right expression for the moment. And I would believe it except I am a primary school teacher and I have spent sixteen years reading the faces of children who are performing innocence, and Cate's face is a performance.

But that is not my concern right now. My concern is inside me. My concern is the thing I have been carrying for twenty-five years like a stone in my chest, smooth and heavy and impossible to put down, and I have told myself it was a pebble, just a pebble, just a tiny thing, not a stone at all, just a grain of sand, nothing to worry about, it was the TV, it was a dream, it was —

It was not the TV.

The thought arrives with the force of a door being kicked open. Not nudged. Kicked. The frame splinters and the door swings wide and behind it is the room I have kept locked for twenty-five years and the room is not empty. The room has been waiting. The room has a sound in it.

I hear it now. Not as a memory. As a presence. As something that has been in the room with me all along, sitting quietly behind the locked door, patient, waiting for me to stop pretending it wasn't there.

A voice.

A woman's voice. Sharp. From upstairs. Brief — one second, maybe two. Not a scream. Not a cry. A command. The kind of tone an adult uses when a child has done something wrong and the adult needs the behaviour to stop immediately. Authoritative. Commanding. Female.

Then the sound of the fall.

Then the mug shattering in my hands.

I was in the kitchen. The old kitchen, the one with the yellow tiles and the calendar with the lighthouse. I was making hot chocolate. Five mugs lined up on the counter. The pink one with the chipped handle was mine. I was stirring — the chocolate powder into the milk, the spoon going round and round, the way I always stirred, the way I still stir, round and round, the small circular motion that makes powder dissolve into liquid, that makes separate things become one thing, that makes chaos into something warm and drinkable.

And then the voice. From upstairs. Sharp. Female.

And then the sound. Not a voice. A sound. The sound a body makes when it stops being vertical and starts being horizontal and the distance between vertical and horizontal is two storeys.

The mug in my hand shattered.

That's what I remember. The shattering. The hot chocolate on the floor — on my feet, warm, spreading, the brown liquid running between the tiles, the handle still in my hand, the ceramic in pieces around me like something that had been whole and was now not whole and could never be whole again.

I have told myself for twenty-five years that the voice was the television. A movie. Someone left the television on. Or it was a dream — I was half-asleep in the kitchen, stirring the chocolate, dreaming. I have told myself this so many times that the telling has become the memory and the memory has become the telling and I cannot separate them anymore. The TV. A dream. That's what it was. That's what it has always been.

Except it wasn't.

I knew it wasn't. At twelve years old, standing in the kitchen with the broken mug and the hot chocolate on my feet and the sound still echoing through the house — a sound that was not the TV, that was not a movie, that was not a dream — I knew. I knew the way you know the sound of your own mother's voice in a crowd. Not by thinking. By knowing. The voice was real. The voice was upstairs. The voice was female and it was not one of us.

"It wasn't the TV."

The words come out of my mouth and I don't decide to say them. They escape. The way air escapes from a balloon — not a slow leak but a tear, a rupture, the sudden uncontrolled release of something that was under pressure for too long.

Saskia turns. Rowan turns. Neve turns. Three faces and I am looking at them and they are looking at me and I cannot perform anymore. The performance is gone. The warm smile, the busy hands, the banana bread and the Tim Tams and the "everything is okay" — it is gone. What is left is a twelve-year-old girl standing in a kitchen with broken ceramic at her feet and hot chocolate cooling on the floor and a sound in her ears that she will never unhear.

"I heard — I don't know, I heard something," I say. "That night. I was in the kitchen making hot chocolate and I heard —"

My voice cracks. Not a small crack. A break. The kind of break that happens when something held under tension finally gives way. I press my palm against my mouth. Hold it there. Breathe.

"A voice," I say. My hand drops. "I don't — a voice. I heard a voice."

The room is very still. Saskia is watching me with those dark, absorbing eyes. Rowan's fingers have tightened on her camera. Neve — Neve is looking at me with an expression I cannot read, something between recognition and fear.

"From upstairs," I say. "Before — before the fall. Before the sound. There was a voice. A woman's voice. Sharp. Like she was — like she was telling someone to do something. Or stop doing something."

I am crying. I don't know when it started. The tears are not gentle. They are not the tidy crying of a woman in a movie who looks beautiful while she does it. They are hot and messy and my nose is running and my face is contorted and I am making sounds — ugly sounds, gasping sounds, the sounds of a person whose scaffolding has collapsed and who is standing in the rubble.

"I told myself it was the TV," I say. "For twenty-five years. The TV. A movie. A dream. I told myself — I told myself that because if it wasn't the TV, if it was real, if it was a real voice, a real woman's voice from upstairs before Kiera fell —"

I can't finish the sentence. The implications of the sentence are too large. They undo everything. Every lesson plan, every batch of banana bread, every night I lay in bed and told myself the world was safe and children were protected and accidents were random and Kiera fell and it was nobody's fault. Every time I said "it was an accident" to Greg, to my friends, to the mothers at the school gate, to myself — every time was a brick in the wall and the wall is down now and I am standing in the open and the wind is cold and there is nothing between me and the truth.

"The voice," I say. "I heard a voice. A woman's voice. And I knew — even then, I knew it wasn't the TV. I knew."

The words hang in the room. They are out. After twenty-five years of being the TV, a dream, nothing, they are out. The voice was real. The voice was a woman's. The voice was upstairs before Kiera fell. The voice is the thing I have been carrying inside the stone in my chest, and now the stone is cracked open and the voice is in the room with all of us and I cannot put it back.

I am sobbing. Full-body. The kind of crying that comes from the diaphragm, that shakes the shoulders, that makes you gasp for air between each wave. I am not performing grief. I am not being the warm, nurturing Imogen who makes tea and straightens tea towels and arranges everything just so. I am twelve years old, standing in a kitchen with hot chocolate burning my bare feet and broken ceramic everywhere, and I heard a woman's voice and then a girl went through a window and I have been pretending I didn't hear it for my entire adult life because if I heard it then it was real and if it was real then it wasn't an accident and if it wasn't an accident then everything I built to survive it is a house built on a sinkhole and the sinkhole is opening now, under my feet, under my chair, under my whole life.

Sophie. Sophie is ten. Sophie is safe at home with Greg and in two years Sophie will be twelve and twelve is the age when girls have sleepovers and lean out of windows and fall, they fall, they fall and — no. No, Sophie is safe. Sophie is okay. I am a good mother. I protect my children. I protect other people's children. I teach Year Six and I watch them and I make sure they are safe because I couldn't keep Kiera safe, I couldn't, I was twelve and I heard a voice and I didn't say anything and I have never said anything and if I had said something — if I had said something twenty-five years ago — if I had said "I heard a voice, I heard a woman's voice, it wasn't the TV" — if I had said that, would it have mattered? Would someone have looked? Would someone have seen the bruising and the landing distance and the scratches and said "this was not an accident"?

But I didn't. I said it was the TV. I said it was a dream. I built my entire life around not saying it and now Saskia has put a police report on a coffee table and read the word "bilaterally" and the wall is down and I am standing in the rubble of myself and I cannot breathe.

Neve's hand touches my arm. Light. Tentative. The touch of someone who is barely holding themselves together but is reaching out anyway. I take her hand. I hold it. We sit on the sofa and we hold hands and the documents are on the table and the photograph with the reflected figure is on the table and I am crying and crying and the crying won't stop because the voice won't stop, it has been playing inside me for twenty-five years and now it is in the room.

The room is silent. My sobbing has faded to breathing — uneven, ragged breathing, but breathing. The quiet after the collapse. The dust settling. I wipe my face with my sleeve. My eyes are swollen. My nose is running. I am not okay. For the first time in twenty-five years I am not saying I am okay.

The room is silent.

And then Cate, from the corner, says: "Can you remember — the words? Any words at all?"

---

# THE FOURTH STEP — Chapter 20: Rowan

## Two Figures

The room is still ringing with Imogen's voice. The voice — her voice, the real one, not the warm layer she wears over it like a cardigan buttoned to the throat — is still in the air, vibrating, the way a struck bell continues to sound after the hand has pulled away. I have never heard Imogen sound like that. I have never seen her face without its performance. The rawness of her — the stripped, unvarnished, twelve-year-old rawness — has changed the composition of the room. The frame has shifted. We are not who we were an hour ago.

The camera sits in my lap. I have not raised it. Some moments are not meant to be photographed — the lens would flatten them, reduce them to light and composition, and some moments are three-dimensional things that require a body present in the room to hold their weight.

Cate's question — "What did the voice say?" — is hanging in the air between us. Imogen shakes her head. She doesn't know. Or she can't say. The voice was sharp, female, a command — but the words are lost. Twenty-five years of telling herself it was the TV have eroded the specific syllables. What remains is the tone. The register. The authority. The voice of a woman telling a child something that needed to be said immediately, with force, without room for misunderstanding.

I know what Imogen heard. Not because she has described it. Because I heard it too.

Not the voice. I heard something else. I heard the sound between the gasp and the thud, the sound that has lived in my spine since I was twelve, the sound that enters through the soles of my feet and climbs. But I saw something too. I saw — I have always seen — the two shapes. The tall one. The small one. The movement of the arm. The hand reaching for the frame.

For twenty-five years these have been fragments. Sensations without sequence. Impressions without narrative. The cold of the floorboards. The blue quality of December moonlight. A tall silhouette blocking the window. A small hand with fingers spread wide. The scrape of nails on wood. The empty frame.

The brain doesn't make a story from something like that. It breaks the story into pieces and hides them in different rooms. The fear in one room. The images in another. The cold in another. And it locks the doors.

But the shards are assembling. They have been assembling since I found the scratches on the frame. Since I saw Kiera's hand — I know it was Kiera's hand, I know those fingers, I held them, I pressed my palm against them in hand games and walking to school and in the dark of a sleeping bag when we whispered secrets — since I saw the fingers slide and the grip fail and the frame become empty.

And now Imogen's voice has given me another piece. A woman's voice, sharp, from upstairs. Before the fall. The timeline is becoming a timeline. Not a cloud of sensation. A sequence. Before, during, after. The word "before" has weight now. Before the fall, there was something. Before the window was empty, there were two figures. Before the sound that has lived in my spine for twenty-five years, there was a voice that Imogen heard and buried.

I look at the documents still spread on the table — Saskia's police report, the autopsy summary with its clinical language, the crime scene photograph with the reflected figure standing at the top of the stairs, the scratch photographs I took with my own hands from a gap in the aluminum frame no wider than a fingernail. The evidence has been accumulating all weekend, each piece a fragment of a larger image, and the image is almost complete. One more piece and the frame will hold. One more piece and the photograph will develop in the dark room of this living room and we will all see what has been slowly emerging from the chemical bath of twenty-five years.

I set the camera on the chair beside me. I stand. The room adjusts — four women tracking my movement, Saskia from her position of authority near the table, Neve from the sofa, white-faced and still, Imogen beside her, wrecked, eyes swollen, and Cate in the corner, calm, watching, always watching.

"There's something I need to show you. Not with the camera. With words."

The words are formed before I speak them. This is new. Normally the fragments come in fragments — I say "the cold" and "the light" and "the sound" and the words trail into silence because the thread won't hold. But the thread is holding now. For the first time in twenty-five years, the thread is holding and I can pull it and the story comes, not in pieces, but in order.

"I was in the hallway," I say. "I don't know how I got there. I must have woken up. The house was — the house was breathing. You know how a house breathes at night? The air moves through the rooms. The floorboards shift. The sounds are different. Quieter. Deeper. The house was breathing and I was standing in the upstairs hallway and I didn't remember getting out of my sleeping bag or climbing the stairs. I was just there. Standing at the end of the hallway near the bathroom door. The carpet was cold under my feet."

I pause. The room is listening. Four women and the silence between them and the story I am telling — the first story I have ever told in sequence, start to finish, without losing the thread.

"The cold is what I remember most clearly," I say. "The cold of the floorboards coming through the carpet. It was summer, it was December, the window was open and the air was warm — but the floor was cold. The cold came up through my feet the way cold comes up through the ground when you stand on it barefoot. It was the cold of underneath. The cold the house keeps beneath its surfaces."

I look at my own hands. At the silver ring on my right hand. The moonstone is dull in the overhead light. In December moonlight it would have glowed.

I close my eyes. The darkness behind my eyelids is not empty. It is full of December. The blue-gold light of a full moon through an open window. The warmth of summer air moving through the hallway. The smell of salt and eucalyptus and something underneath — copper, metal, the taste of fear before you know you are afraid.

"The hallway was warm," I say. "December warm. The window at the end was open — the old window, the one with the timber frame and the brass hinge. The moonlight was coming through and it was gold — thick, liquid gold, the way December moonlight is, the way it pools on surfaces. The carpet was blue. Deep blue. And the light lay on it like water."

I open my eyes. Saskia is watching me with the focused attention she brings to witnesses. Neve's hands are very still in her lap. Imogen is listening through the aftermath of her own collapse, her breath uneven, her eyes on my face.

"There were two figures at the end of the hallway," I say. "In front of the window. One tall. One small. The tall one was standing between me and the window, so I could see the silhouette — the shape of it against the moonlight. The small one was between the tall one and the window."

I pause. The next sentence is the one I have never been able to form. The sentence that has existed as a sensation — a movement registered by the body and stored without language. The arm. The push. The thing that happened between the tall figure being still and the small figure being gone.

"The tall one moved," I say. "An arm extended. A hand — large, adult — pressed against the small one's shoulder. And the small one moved backward. Toward the window. Not a stumble. A push. The force travelled from the tall one's arm through the hand into the small one's body and the small one — the small one was Kiera."

The name comes out clear. Kiera. Not "the small one." Kiera. The name attaches to the shape and the shape becomes a person and the person becomes my friend and my friend is being pushed toward an open window by a figure that is taller than any of us.

"She reached for the frame," I say. "Her hand — her right hand — came up and her fingers spread and she grabbed the wood. I could see her fingernails. Blue polish. Chipped. She'd painted them earlier that evening — she painted all our nails. Blue. The colour of the carpet. And her nails scraped the paint and I could see the lines — five lines, downward — and her knuckles were white and she was holding on."

I stop. The memory is vivid — not a flashback, not a fragment, but a narrative. I am inside it and outside it simultaneously. The twelve-year-old standing in the blue-gold light of the hallway. The thirty-seven-year-old standing in a living room in July telling the story for the first time.

"And then the grip failed," I say. "Her fingers slid. One by one. The pinky first. Then the ring finger. Then the middle. Slow. The way things fall in slow motion when your eyes are making a record and the record is too important to speed through. The hand came off the frame. The fingers opened. And she went through the window. Not fell. Went. As if the air behind her had reached up and taken her. One moment she was here — fingers white on the wood, body leaning into the opening, the moonlight catching the blue polish on her nails — and the next moment she was not. The window was empty. The frame was empty. The space where she had been was just space."

I stop. The room is holding the image. I can feel it — the weight of four people seeing what I have seen for twenty-five years, the weight of a shared visualization, the moment when a private photograph becomes public evidence.

"Afterward — I don't know how long afterward, seconds, maybe — I heard a sound from below. From the garden. Not loud. The kind of sound grass makes when it receives something heavy. Something it was never meant to hold."

The room is silent. I am aware of sounds — the heating clicking, the wind against the glass, the tick of the kitchen clock Imogen must have straightened at some point. But the silence beneath the sounds is enormous.

"The tall figure — the proportions, the stance, the weight of her — it was a woman," I say. "I could tell by the shape. The proportions. The way she stood — she was standing between me and the window and I couldn't see her face because the moonlight was behind her, silhouetting her, turning her into a shape without features. But she was tall. Taller than any of us. And she stood the way adults stand — planted, certain, the weight of her distributed with authority. Not the stance of a child. The stance of someone who expected the space around her to accommodate her, which is how adults move through the world and not how twelve-year-olds do."

Saskia speaks. Her voice is precise, measured, but there is something beneath it — a current, a frequency, the vibration of a question she has been waiting twenty-four years to ask.

"Tall like an adult?"

"Tall like an adult," I say.

The words land. Tall like an adult. An adult in the hallway. An adult who pushed a child. An adult whose face was hidden by the moonlight and whose voice I did not hear but Imogen did — sharp, female, authoritative. An adult who was in the house that night and who was not one of us.

I look at Neve. She is sitting on the sofa and she has not spoken since Saskia's presentation and her face is white and her hands are flat on her thighs and she is very, very still. The kind of still that is not peace. The kind of still that is a dam. Water behind it. Rising.

She knows. I see it the way I see light on a surface — not through interpretation but through direct perception. She knows who the tall figure was. She has known since before this weekend. Perhaps she has always known, in the way that bodies know things before minds admit them. She is carrying it. The same way I carried the cold and the light and the sound. The same way Imogen carried the voice. The same way Saskia carried the word "bilaterally." We have all been carrying pieces of the same shattered night, each of us holding a shard, none of us with the whole. And the shards are sharp. Twenty-five years of carrying sharp things inside us, pressed against the soft tissue of our lives, and we learned to hold them still so they wouldn't cut. But stillness is not healing. Stillness is just the decision to stop bleeding.

But the shards are on the table now. The scratches. The bruising pattern. The landing distance. The reflected figure in the photograph. Imogen's voice. My two figures. The pieces are coming together and the picture they form is becoming clear and the picture is — the picture is someone tall, someone adult, someone who was in this house that night and pushed a twelve-year-old girl through a window.

Who was in the house? Whose house was it? Whose mother was upstairs? The questions form in the room without anyone speaking them. They hover above the documents on the table like heat above pavement.

Cate speaks from the corner. She has been quiet through all of it — through Saskia's presentation, through Imogen's collapse, through my account. Sitting in the armchair by the bookshelf, her face composed, her hands folded, her eyes moving between each of us with the careful attention of someone collecting information. She has not reacted with surprise to any of it. Not the police report. Not the photograph. Not the voice. Not the two figures.

She asks: "Was there — I mean, were there grown-ups around? That night?"

The question is simple. Five words. Syntax that a child could parse. But the question is not simple. The question is the centre of the maze. The question is the key that turns the lock on the room where the answer has been sitting for twenty-five years.

Everyone turns to Neve.

---

# THE FOURTH STEP — Chapter 21: Saskia

## The Confrontation

The house is quiet after Rowan's revelation. Imogen is in the kitchen, running water, filling silence. Neve is upstairs — alone, I note, which is either courage or compulsion. Rowan is sitting on the back deck with her camera, not photographing, just holding it. And Cate is in the living room with a book she hasn't turned a page of in twenty minutes.

It's nine PM. We have been in this house for twenty-seven hours. In twenty-seven hours I have accumulated more inconsistencies in Cate Morrow's story than I have seen in sworn affidavits from pathological liars. The inconsistencies are small. Microscopic. The kind of detail that wouldn't register in normal conversation, in normal life, between normal people. But I am not normal. I have never been normal. I am a criminal defense barrister who has spent her adult life reading deception, and Cate Morrow is the most rehearsed witness I have ever encountered.

I've been watching her all day. I've been watching her since she walked through the door yesterday morning with her calibrated smile and her rehearsed questions and her eyes that see too much and show too little. I've been building a file in my head — I build files in my head the way other people breathe, continuously, without deciding to — and the file on Cate Morrow has been growing since the moment she said hello.

The file reads like this:

Cate Morrow arrived at nine-fifteen on Saturday morning. She claims to have been two years behind us at Sorrento Primary. She claims to have moved to London at fifteen. She claims to have lost touch. She asks questions about the house, the group, the Doyles, the history of this place. She asks the questions a journalist asks — open-ended, apparently casual, designed to elicit disclosure without the subject noticing. She performs warmth. She performs interest. She performs the role of a woman reconnecting with old acquaintances.

The performance is good. It is very good. I have seen worse performances from witnesses who were coached by senior counsel.

But performances have seams. And I am very good at seams.

I close my laptop. I stand. I walk to the living room doorway.

Cate looks up from her book. The smile arrives before the eye contact — a fractional error. Real smiles synchronize with attention. Performed smiles precede it.

"Saskia. Everything okay?"

"We're going to have a conversation."

"Of course." She closes the book. Sets it on the arm of the chair. Her movements are deliberate, unhurried, the movements of someone who has trained themselves not to flinch. "What about?"

I sit in the chair across from her. I don't take the sofa — the sofa is too soft, too casual, it puts you at a physical disadvantage. The chair is upright, hard-seated, at the same height as hers. Level ground.

"You," I say.

"Me?" The smile again. Warm, open, curious. The right amount of surprise. "What about me?"

"Your backstory. I've been auditing it."

"I'm an open book." She gestures at the closed novel on the chair arm. A small joke. A deflection. I note it.

"Your mother's name," I say.

The question lands. I watch her face. Not her eyes — her eyes will be controlled. I watch the muscles around her mouth, the micro-contractions of the orbicularis oris, the tell-tale tension that precedes a performed answer.

"Margaret," she says. "Margaret Morrow. She passed away four years ago."

A dead mother. Convenient. Dead people don't corroborate. In my experience, witnesses who claim deceased family members as corroboration for their identity are either telling the truth or have constructed their backstory with care. The latter is more common than the former.

"What was her maiden name?"

A pause. Two-tenths of a second. Within normal range but at the upper limit — the cognitive load of retrieving a fabricated detail takes fractionally longer than a real one. I've timed hundreds of these pauses in cross-examination. Two-tenths is the edge. She's at the edge.

"Winters. Margaret Winters. She was from Adelaide."

"Which school did you attend?"

"Sorrento Primary. Years Three through Six. Then we moved, and I went to a school in Rosebud before — before London."

"Which school in Rosebud?"

"Rosebud Secondary College."

"Rosebud Secondary College closed in 2003. It merged with Rosebud High to form Rosebud Secondary College — the current school — in 2004. If you left for London at fifteen, you would have been enrolled there in 2002 or 2003. It was still Rosebud High."

She doesn't blink. But her left hand — the one resting on the arm of the chair — shifts. A small adjustment. A grounding movement. I've seen witnesses do the same thing when they realize opposing counsel has a document they weren't expecting.

"You're right. Rosebud High. I always forget — they changed the name, didn't they? It's confusing."

It is confusing. It's also the kind of error a real former student wouldn't make — the name of your school is embedded in autobiographical memory, not subject to this kind of confusion. But she's recovering well. The self-deprecating laugh, the acknowledgment of error, the redirect. She's been trained for this, or she's trained herself. Either way, the performance is sophisticated.

"It is," I say. "Why did your family move to London?"

"My father's work. He was — he was in finance. Transferred to the London office."

"What firm?"

Another pause. This one is longer — four-tenths of a second. I'm counting. I will always be counting.

"A small firm. It doesn't exist anymore. Merged, actually — there was a lot of consolidation in the early 2000s."

A dead mother. A defunct firm. A maiden name from Adelaide — three hours' flight away, difficult to verify without specific records. Every detail in Cate Morrow's backstory is structured the way a witness statement is structured when the witness has been coached: specific enough to sound real, unverifiable enough to withstand scrutiny. The construction is thorough. But thorough constructions have a weakness. They're too thorough. Real biographies have gaps, approximations, uncertainty. Cate's biography has none. It is complete and verifiable and every detail is precisely positioned to resist challenge. That precision is itself the evidence.

I lean forward. Not much. Two inches. Enough to change the spatial dynamic, to move from conversational distance to something closer to interrogation.

"You said yesterday you were a couple of years behind us at school. You would have been nine or ten when we were twelve. When THE FOURTH STEP happened."

"I was nine. I remember hearing about it. It was all over the — everyone was talking about it."

"What do you remember hearing?"

"That a girl fell. From a window. It was — it was terrible. It was one of those things that — that marks a community."

The sentence is too complete. Too well-constructed. A real memory of a real overheard conversation would be fragmentary — "I remember people talking about it," "something about a girl," "I didn't really understand." Cate's version has narrative shape. It has been composed.

"Who told you about it?"

"I don't remember specifically. It was just — everywhere. You know how small towns are."

"I do. But you were nine. You were at a different school — you said you were at Sorrento Primary, but two years behind us. You would have been in Year Three or Year Four. THE FOURTH STEP happened in December. End of the school year. Would a nine-year-old in Year Three have been talking about a twelve-year-old in Year Six?"

"Kids talk."

"They do. But you said you remembered the five of us being like a little gang. Everyone knew the five girls from Sorrento Primary. Except you said five. There were six of us. I corrected you. You moved past it very quickly."

Something shifts in her face. Not the expression — the expression is still controlled, still open, still warm. But something underneath. Something in the depth of the eyes. A calculation happening behind the performance, a rapid assessment of how much I know and how much she needs to adjust.

"I was nine, Saskia. I'm sure I got some details wrong."

"You did. You've been getting details wrong all weekend. You asked about the Doyles yesterday — specifically, where they went after Sorrento, what Kiera's parents do now, whether anyone has seen the grave. Those are not the questions of someone who overheard a childhood tragedy and vaguely remembers it. Those are the questions of someone conducting an investigation."

The smile doesn't waver. But her breathing has changed. Shallow. Measured. The breathing of someone managing an internal response.

"I ask questions. It's habit. I'm sorry if it feels like —"

"You knew whose house this was before Imogen told you. You said 'oh, of course' when she mentioned it belonged to Neve's family. But the micro-expression before the 'oh, of course' was grief. Not surprise. Grief. You already knew."

Silence.

The house is quiet. Wind around the eaves. The distant sound of the sea. Imogen's water still running in the kitchen. Rowan's footsteps on the deck outside, slow, aimless, the pacing of someone who is somewhere else.

"You asked about the renovation. When it happened. Who owned the house after the holidays. You asked if anyone kept in touch with the Doyles. You asked if anyone has seen the grave. These questions are precise. They are targeted. They are the questions of someone who is building a picture of what happened here, piece by piece, and who already has more pieces than they should."

I pause. Let the catalogue of errors sit in the air between us. In court, I would pause here. Let the jury absorb the pattern. The pattern is the argument. No single discrepancy proves deception. The accumulation proves deception. The weight of small wrongs.

"And there's the knowledge," I say. "You know things you shouldn't know. Yesterday, when Imogen described the kitchen — the yellow tiles, the calendar with the lighthouse — you reacted. Not with surprise. With recognition. A woman who was two years behind us, who never visited this house, who was nine years old, should not have recognized the description of a kitchen she never saw. But you did. I watched you. Your pupils dilated. That's a physiological response to recognition, not novelty."

"Saskia —"

"Your accent slips. Under stress. You've been in London, supposedly, since you were fifteen. Twenty-two years. Your accent should be fully anglicized, or at minimum a genuine hybrid. Instead it's a performance — a London overlay on an Australian foundation. And the foundation comes through. Your vowel sounds sharpen when you're pressed. 'Grave' — you said 'grave' yesterday, and the vowel was Australian. Not London. Not even close."

I am watching her. I have been watching her for two days. I have catalogued every pause, every micro-expression, every question, every over-specific answer. I have built a file and the file says: this woman is not who she says she is. This woman is performing an identity. This woman knows things about Kiera Doyle and this house and this group that a casual acquaintance from two years behind us at school should not know.

"I don't believe you are who you say you are," I say.

The words land between us. Clean. Precise. The way words land in a courtroom when the prosecution says "no further questions" and the witness realizes the cross-examination is over and they have not survived it.

Cate's face does not change. This is itself significant. An innocent person accused of deception would react — shock, indignation, hurt, confusion. The absence of reaction is the reaction. She is managing her face instead of feeling through it.

"I'm not sure I follow," she says.

"I want a name. A real one."

"I've been telling you the truth."

"You've been telling me a story. The truth and a story are different things. I know the difference. It's my job to know the difference."

She looks at me. Really looks at me — not the performed eye contact of the last two days, but something rawer. An assessment. A calculation. Not "is she a threat" but "how much does she know."

I know more than she thinks.

I've spent twenty-five years investigating Kiera's death. I obtained the police report when I was nineteen. I filed freedom-of-information requests for the autopsy summary. I tracked the Doyles — the surviving Doyles — through public records, electoral rolls, social media. I know Kiera had a younger sister. I know the sister's name. Aisling. It means dream, or vision, in Irish. I've seen the name on bylines — A.M. Doyle, investigative journalist, Melbourne. I've read her articles. She writes about institutional failures, cover-ups, cold cases. She writes like someone with a personal stake in truth.

And I know something else. Something I haven't told anyone. Something I've been carrying for twelve months, building in my inbox one encrypted message at a time. A correspondence that started with a single email — brief, professional, citing the bruising pattern from Kiera's autopsy, asking if I had additional information. A correspondence I entered because I am a fool for information, because I have never met a lead I didn't follow, because this person knew things about the case that weren't in any public record and I needed to know how.

Twelve months of emails. Twelve months of sharing what I knew. Twelve months of building a relationship with someone who used the name A.M. Doyle, who never once told me their real name, who I trusted because they had information and I am constitutionally incapable of trusting no one when information is on the table.

I trusted a stranger with twenty-five years of my work. And now I'm sitting across from her.

"I've been corresponding with a journalist," I say.

Cate's face does not move. But her body does. A stillness — not relaxation but rigidity. The stillness of a deer that has heard a branch snap.

"For the past year," I continue. "Someone who's been investigating Kiera's death. Someone who contacted me through an encrypted email service with information about the case. The bruising pattern. The landing distance. Details that aren't in the publicly available records. Details that required access to autopsy files, police reports, forensic analysis."

I pause. I let the silence do what silence does in a courtroom — fill the space, press against the witness, give the truth room to surface.

"This person used the name A.M. Doyle."

I watch her face. I watch every muscle, every micro-movement, every flicker of the eyelids and twitch of the jaw and contraction of the pupils. I watch the way you watch a witness who is about to break.

"A.M. Doyle," I repeat. "Would you like to tell me who that is?"

Cate's face goes completely still. Not the stillness of composure, not the stillness of performance. A deeper stillness. The stillness of something that has been running for a very long time and has just heard the starting pistol it has been dreading.

---

# THE FOURTH STEP — Chapter 22: Aisling

## Unmasked

The silence lasts four seconds. I count them. I've been counting things all weekend — steps, seconds, breaths, lies. The counting is Aisling's habit, not Cate's. Cate doesn't count. Cate flows. Cate smiles and asks questions and moves through rooms like water finding the easiest path downhill, leaving no trace. Aisling counts everything because Aisling doesn't trust anything to be what it seems.

Four seconds. Then I stop.

I stop being Cate.

It's not a decision. It's a release. Like setting down a rucksack I've been carrying for two years, or twenty-five years, or my whole life. The muscles in my face unlock one by one. My shoulders drop two inches. My hands, which have been performing stillness the way a statue performs calm, grip the arms of the chair, and the grip is real, the first real thing my hands have done since I walked through the front door of this house.

"My name," I say, "is not Cate Morrow."
