Saskia doesn't move. She's sitting across from me with her back straight and her hands flat on her knees and her eyes on my face and she is not surprised. She's known — not everything, but enough. She's been building her case against Cate Morrow all weekend the way I've been building my case against all of them for twenty-five years. She's a lawyer. I should have expected this. I did expect this. I just didn't expect it now.

"My name," I say, "is Aisling Doyle."

The name sounds foreign in this room. It sounds like a door opening. It sounds like something that has been locked away for a very long time, finally turning in its frame.

"Aisling Doyle," Saskia repeats. Not a question. A confirmation.

Doyle. The surname lands in the air and I hear it land and I hear what it means. Doyle. Kiera Doyle. The name that has lived inside me like a second heartbeat for twenty-five years, the name I've written on the byline of every investigation I've ever published, the name I've whispered into encrypted email servers at three in the morning when I couldn't sleep because the case was going nowhere and Kiera was still dead and no one cared. Doyle. The name I couldn't say all weekend because saying it would break the mask and the mask was the only thing keeping me in this house.

Footsteps in the hallway. Imogen, drawn by the voices, standing in the doorway with a tea towel in her hands, her face open and confused. Behind her, the back door slides open and Rowan steps inside, camera hanging at her side, her dark eyes finding me across the room. On the stairs — I hear them, the fourth step groaning — Neve. Coming down. Coming to see what's happening.

They gather. Four women in a living room in a house in Sorrento on a Saturday night in July, and I am about to tell them who I am, and my hands are shaking, and I let them shake.

"I'm Kiera's sister."

The word hangs in the air. Sister. The most ordinary word in the language and the heaviest. Sister. The word I haven't said out loud to another human being in ten years, because every time I said it before I watched their faces change — pity, discomfort, the quick pivot to another subject. The sister of the dead girl. The girl whose sister fell out of a window. The family that was destroyed.

"She had a — wait. Kiera had a sister?" Imogen says. Her voice is small. The tea towel is twisted in her hands, wound tight, a tourniquet of fabric.

"She did. She does. I'm sitting in front of you."

I stand. I've been sitting for too long. Sitting is a passive position. Sitting is Cate's position. Cate sat and smiled and asked her careful questions and let other people talk. Aisling stands. Aisling has been standing for twenty-five years.

"I was eight when Kiera died. Eight years old. She was twelve. She was my big sister and she was the centre of my universe and she went to a sleepover at a friend's house and she never came home."

My voice is different. I can hear it. So can they. The Cate voice — smooth, measured, London-touched, grammatically perfect — is gone. What's left is raw. Australian vowels, sharp and flat. Short sentences when I'm angry. Longer ones when I'm not, when I'm grieving, when the words come from the place where Kiera still lives.

I am angry. I am grieving. I have been both for twenty-five years and the mask didn't suppress them — it compressed them. And compression produces pressure and pressure produces rupture and the rupture is happening now, in this living room, in front of these women. I've earned this rupture. I've earned the right to stand here without a disguise and speak in my own voice and feel what I actually feel instead of performing a careful, measured, moderate version of someone else's grief.

"I didn't know what happened," I say. "I was eight. My parents sat me down and told me Kiera had had an accident. A fall. That she'd gone to sleep and didn't wake up. That's what they told an eight-year-old. 'Kiera had an accident.' 'Kiera fell.' 'Kiera's in heaven now.' They used the word 'accident' so many times it lost its meaning. It became a sound, not a word. Accident. Accident. Accident."

Neve has stopped at the foot of the stairs. She hasn't come closer. She's standing very still with her hand on the bannister and her face — her face has been drained of blood, like someone pulled the plug and everything that makes a face a colour has run out of it.

"After she died, everything broke," I say. "My parents divorced within two years. My mother started drinking. Not wine-with-dinner drinking. Vodka-in-the-morning drinking. Fall-down-in-the-kitchen drinking. She couldn't say Kiera's name so she poured it into glasses instead. My father tried to hold it together. He worked and he cooked and he took me to school and he picked me up and he did it all with this face — this grey, empty face that looked like it had been scooped out. He died when I was twenty-five. Heart attack. Fifty-two years old. His heart gave out. The doctor said it was cardiovascular disease. I said it was grief. The medical examiner wrote one thing on the death certificate. I carry another."

I stop. Breathe. The next part is harder.

"My mother is alive. She lives in a unit in Geelong. She drinks. She doesn't talk about Kiera. She has never once, in twenty-five years, said Kiera's name to me. Not once. The name is forbidden. The topic is forbidden. My sister's entire existence has been erased from my family because my mother cannot bear to say her dead daughter's name and my father took the grief with him when he died and I am the only one left who remembers her."

My voice cracks on the last word. Not Cate's controlled, measured vocal break. A real crack. A fracture line running through twenty-five years of compressed grief the way a crack runs through ice when the pressure becomes too much for the structure to hold. I let it crack. I don't fix it. Aisling doesn't fix things. Aisling holds the broken pieces and keeps going.

"I became a journalist because of Kiera. I was twelve — the same age she was when she died — when I decided. I wanted to find things out. I wanted to know what happened to my sister. The official story didn't make sense. A girl falls from a window at a sleepover. An accident. A tragic, terrible accident. That's what everyone said. The police said it. The newspapers said it. The families said it. My parents said it. But accidents leave traces and the traces in Kiera's case didn't match the story."

I reach into the back pocket of my jeans. I pull out my phone. I open the encrypted folder — the folder that has lived on every phone I've owned for the past six years, the folder with two-factor authentication and a twelve-character password that is Kiera's birthday, backwards. I swipe to the photographs.

"These are autopsy photographs."

Imogen makes a sound. A small, sharp intake of breath. Rowan doesn't move. Saskia leans forward.

"I obtained them through journalism contacts. A pathologist. A friend of a friend of someone who owed someone a favour. It took me four years to find someone willing to share them. They're not in any public record. They're not in the police file. They were filed and forgotten and I found them."

I hold the phone out. Saskia takes it. She looks at the screen. Her face doesn't change but her jaw tightens, a small contraction, barely visible. She's reading the photographs the way she reads evidence — clinically, precisely, without flinching.

"The bruising," I say. "Upper back and shoulders. Bilateral. Even spacing. The pathologist I consulted — a retired forensic specialist from the Victorian Institute of Forensic Medicine — described it as 'manual compression marks consistent with hands gripping from behind.' Those aren't impact bruises from a fall. Impact bruises are irregular. Asymmetrical. Concentrated on the point of contact. These are symmetrical. Even. The width of fingers. Ten points of contact. Someone gripped Kiera's shoulders from behind. Someone gripped her hard enough to bruise."

I take the phone back from Saskia. I swipe to the next image. The landing site — the original crime scene photograph, obtained through the same contacts. The lawn. The position of the body. The measurements from the building.

"She landed two metres from the base of the wall. Two metres. A girl who was five foot nothing, who weighed maybe forty kilos. She fell — or she was pushed — from a second-floor window, a height of approximately four metres. A body falling from that height, with no horizontal force, lands directly below the window. Within half a metre. Kiera landed two metres out. That requires horizontal force. That requires a push. Not a stumble, not a slip, not a lean-too-far. A push with enough force to send a child two metres through the air."

Rowan is crying. Not loudly. Not dramatically. Tears running silently down her angular face like rain on a window that hasn't been wiped, her jaw set, her eyes on the phone in my hand. She doesn't wipe them. She lets them fall and each one is a small, quiet verdict.

"I've been receiving anonymous tips for two years," I say. "Emails. No return address. Routed through servers I can't trace. The first one came with a photograph of the original police incident report. The second came with a name: Elaine Cassidy. The third came with a question: 'Who was the adult in the house that night?'"

Neve's hand tightens on the bannister. Her knuckles go white. She's gripping the wood the way Imogen gripped her fork this morning — without knowing, without feeling, the body responding to something the mind hasn't caught up with.

"I don't know who's been feeding them to me," I say. "I've tried to trace the source. I've tried to reply. The emails come from a rotating set of anonymous addresses and they stop as suddenly as they start and then they start again, weeks or months later, with another piece of information. Someone knows what happened to my sister. Someone has known for twenty-five years. And someone has been feeding me information, piece by piece, like they want me to find the truth but they won't come forward and say it themselves. I have my suspicions. A dying woman in a hospice. But I can't prove it."

I put the phone away. I look at each of them.

Imogen is standing in the doorway with the twisted tea towel and her face is the face of someone whose world has just shifted under their feet. The minimizer is minimizing no longer. Her mouth is open. Her eyes are wide. She looks like she's been hit.

Rowan is sitting on the arm of the sofa, tears on her cheeks, her camera in her lap, and she is looking at me — at my face — with an expression I can't immediately read. Then I understand. She sees it. The resemblance. I'm thirty-three, not twelve, and I've changed beyond recognition, but the bone structure is the same. The eyes. Kiera's eyes, looking out of a different face.

Neve is still at the foot of the stairs. Still gripping the bannister. Still white. She hasn't moved since I said my name.

And Saskia. Saskia is sitting in the chair across from where I was sitting, her hands flat on her knees, her face composed, and she is recalculating. I can see it happening in real time — the rapid reassessment of everything she thought she knew. She corresponded with A.M. Doyle for a year. She shared information with A.M. Doyle. She trusted A.M. Doyle with details about the case, about her suspicions, about the police report she obtained when she was nineteen. And A.M. Doyle was sitting across the breakfast table from her yesterday morning, asking about the house, smiling with calibrated warmth, performing the role of Cate Morrow while Aisling Doyle took notes.

"You manipulated me," Saskia says. Not angry. Not yet. Stating a fact.

"Yes."

"You manipulated all of this. The reunion. The emails. You made me think it was my idea."

"Yes."

"Why?"

The question is simple. The answer is not.

"Because I needed you all in this house. I needed you in the place where it happened. I needed you surrounded by the same walls, the same light, the same air. Memory is contextual. You know this — you're a lawyer. Witnesses remember more in the environment where the event occurred. I needed you here so you'd remember. So the walls would do their work. So the house would press against your denial the way it's been pressing against Neve's denial since she arrived."

Neve flinches at her name. A full-body flinch, visible from across the room.

"I've spent ten years investigating," I say. "I created the Cate Morrow identity using a deceased person's records. I reached out to you, Saskia, because you were the one who never let it go. I could see it — your public record, your career, the way you'd pursued the case through official channels. You were the one who would get the others here. You were the one they'd trust."

"You lied to me for a year."

"I told you the truth about everything that mattered. The bruising. The landing distance. The inconsistencies in the report. I just didn't tell you who I was."

Saskia holds my gaze. Ten seconds. Fifteen. The longest silence in the room since I said my name.

The silence stretches. I can hear the wind. I can hear the house breathing. I can hear the weight of what I've done settling over her like snow.

"I trusted you with twenty-five years of my work," Saskia says. Not angry. Not yet. The voice of a woman measuring the distance between trust and betrayal and finding it longer than she expected.

She nods. Once. A single, precise acknowledgment. Not forgiveness. Not acceptance. Recognition. She understands why I did it. She would have done the same.

I turn to face all of them. The room is small and the night is dark and the wind is pressing against the windows and I am standing in a house where my sister died and I am not pretending anymore and the relief is so sharp it cuts.

"I didn't come here to make anyone suffer," I say. "I'm not here to punish anyone. I'm here because my sister was twelve years old and she was happy and she was funny and she told the best ghost stories and she used to close her bedroom door in my face and say 'go away, Aisling, we're doing big girl things.' I used to sit outside her door and listen to her laugh with her friends. I used to press my ear against the wood and wish I was on the other side of it."

My voice is not steady. I let it shake. Aisling's voice shakes. Cate's voice never shook. Cate's voice was a wall. Aisling's voice is a window — broken, open, letting everything through.

"She was my sister. She was my big sister. And she went to a sleepover in this house on the fourteenth of December and she never came home and my family fell apart and I have spent every day since trying to find out why."

I look at Neve. She is looking at me like I am a ghost. Like Kiera has walked into the room wearing a different face.

I look at Imogen. Her hand is over her mouth. The tea towel has fallen to the floor.

I look at Rowan. Rowan, who is still crying, who has been crying since I mentioned the bruising, who is looking at me with recognition that goes beyond the resemblance. Rowan, who has been holding Kiera's ring on her finger for twenty-five years.

I look at Saskia. Saskia, who corresponded with me for a year and never knew she was talking to Kiera's sister. Saskia, who has the police report and the autopsy summary and the photographs and the same obsessive need to know that I have.

"I am Aisling Doyle," I say. "I am Kiera Doyle's younger sister. I am a journalist. I have spent twenty-five years investigating my sister's death, and I know — I know — she did not fall from that window. She did not lean too far. She did not slip. She was pushed."

I pause. The room is silent. The wind is silent. Even the house is silent — no creaking, no settling, no breath. As if the building itself has gone still, the way a room goes still when someone says the thing that everyone has been circling, holding its own air, waiting for what comes next.

"Someone pushed my sister. I know who was in this house that night. And I think one of you saw what happened."

---

# THE FOURTH STEP — Chapter 23: Neve

## Elaine

The headlights come first.

I'm standing in the living room with the others. Saskia has the police report spread across the coffee table. Aisling — not Cate, Aisling, Kiera Doyle's sister — has the autopsy photographs in her hands. Imogen has her face wet and her hands pressed against her mouth. Rowan has her camera around her neck and her eyes fixed on some middle distance where the past and the present overlap. The air in the room is thick with what Aisling has told us. The photographs. The anonymous tips. The revelation that she has spent her entire adult life trying to find out who killed her sister.

And then the headlights.

Two bright beams sweep across the living room windows. They travel left to right, slow and deliberate, the way headlights move when a car is pulling into a driveway rather than passing on the road. The beams cross the ceiling, illuminate the far wall, and stop. Engine off. The lights die.

For a moment, no one moves.

Saskia is the first to speak. She's at the window in three steps, pulling the curtain aside. "Car in the driveway," she says. "Dark sedan. One occupant."

I'm already moving. I don't decide to move. My body is moving and my mind is following, the way it followed me up the stairs this afternoon, the way it followed me into the hallway, the way it has been leading me places all weekend that my conscious mind did not approve. I reach the window beside Saskia and I look out and the world goes very quiet.

It's my mother's car.

The blue Honda she's had for nine years. Parked at an angle in the driveway, not quite straight, the way she always parks when she's tired. The driver's door opens.

Elaine gets out.

She shouldn't be driving. She's in hospice. Pancreatic cancer, stage four, three weeks into palliative care. She drove herself — forty minutes from the hospice in Mornington to this house in Sorrento — in the dark — alone.

She stands behind the open door for a moment. Leaning on it. Her coat is too large — the camel coat I bought her last winter, before the diagnosis. It hangs from her shoulders like fabric draped over a wire frame. Her hair is shorter than it was two days ago. Someone at the hospice must have cut it. She'd never let anyone cut her hair. She always said a woman's hair is the frame of her face and you don't compromise on the frame.

She closes the car door and stands for a moment, one hand on the roof of the car, steadying herself. Her posture — even now, even diminished, even leaning on a car for support — is straight. Her chin is up. She was a school principal for thirty years. She has commanded every room she has entered since before I was born. She is standing in a driveway at ten o'clock at night, gaunt and ill, and she is composing herself the way she composes herself before entering any room: shoulders back, chin level, expression neutral.

She walks toward the front door.

Her gait is slower than it was. She favors her left side — the cancer has spread to her spine, the pain is manageable, which is what she says about everything. Manageable. Contained. Under control. Three words that mean I will not admit what this is doing to me. She takes the path one step at a time. The concrete path that was brick. Her feet know it the way mine do. She walked this path every morning for two weeks, twenty-five years ago. This was her house. Her holiday house. Her jurisdiction.

She reaches the front step. The bluestone step. The step my foot knows without looking.

She knocks.

Three knocks. Even. Spaced. The knock of someone who expects the door to be opened.

I am standing in the hallway. I can see her silhouette through the frosted glass — smaller than it should be, narrower, but the shape is unmistakable. The tall frame. The upright bearing. The way she holds her head. I would know her silhouette in a lineup of a hundred women. I have been watching her silhouette my whole life. Leaning over my cot. Standing in the kitchen doorway. Kneeling in a cream-coloured hallway with her hands on my face.

"Neve." Saskia's voice, low and precise, behind me. "Do you want me to answer it?"

No one else moves. Imogen is frozen on the arm of the sofa. Rowan is frozen in the corner. Aisling is watching me with an expression I can't read — she has been watching me differently since she revealed who she is. Since she learned whose mother was in the house that night.

I open the door.

The cold night air comes in first. Then the salt smell — the sea is close, I can hear it, the low rhythmic push and pull against the shore. Then the light from the hallway falls on my mother's face and I see her clearly.

She is thinner than she was on Tuesday. I visit every day and she is thinner between visits. The cancer is consuming her from the inside. Her cheekbones are sharp. Her collarbones visible above the open collar of the coat I bought her. But her eyes are the same — dark brown, steady, intelligent. The cancer has not touched her eyes.

She looks at me and she smiles.

The smile is small and warm and it reaches her eyes. The smile she has given me every day of my life — the smile that says I'm here, you're safe, everything is as it should be. The smile that held me together through three years of psychologists who asked the wrong questions because she was sitting in the room making sure they didn't ask the right ones.

"Hello, sweetheart," she says. "I think it's time we talked about that night."

Sweetheart. The word lands in me like a stone in still water. Ripples spreading outward, concentric, widening. The word she used in the hallway. Kneeling. Her hands on my face. *You didn't see anything, sweetheart.* The same word. The same voice. The same warmth. She is standing on the threshold of this house — this house where she pushed a twelve-year-old girl through a window, this house where she knelt and rewrote my memory, this house where she held the story for twenty-five years — and she is smiling and calling me sweetheart.

I don't step aside. I don't speak. I stand in the doorway and she stands on the step and we look at each other. The cold air moves between us. The salt air. The air that was here that night, warm instead of cold, summer instead of winter, December instead of July. She was standing in this doorway twenty-five years ago when the ambulance came. She was standing here when the police came. She was standing here when they carried Kiera out and she held my hand and told the officers I was asleep, I was downstairs, I didn't see anything, my daughter was asleep on the couch the whole time.

"Neve, darling." Her voice is gentle. Patient. "Let me in."

I step aside.

She walks past me into the hallway. Her hand brushes my arm — lightly, almost incidental — and the touch sends a current through me. Not warmth. Not comfort. Recognition. My body knows her touch the way it knows the pitch of the fourth step. The hand that pushed Kiera is the hand that held my face and the hand that smoothed my hair and I cannot separate them because she never gave me a world where they were different hands.

Her feet find the floorboards and the floorboards creak and I know she hears the same pitch I hear. She is walking them now with the same measured step, the same deliberate pace, a woman re-entering a space she once controlled absolutely.

She looks at the staircase.

She doesn't look away. She looks at it the way you look at a piece of furniture you used to own — with recognition, with assessment, without sentiment. The staircase. The fourth step. The groan. She knows. She knows the sound it makes. She knew it before I did.

Then she turns left into the living room.

The room goes still.

Saskia has moved away from the window. She's standing by the mantelpiece, arms crossed, the police report still visible on the coffee table. Her face is unreadable — the studied neutrality of a courtroom professional watching a surprise witness enter the box. Aisling is seated on the sofa, the autopsy photographs in her lap, her jaw tight, her eyes on Elaine with the focus of a woman who has been waiting twenty-five years to see this face. Rowan is in the corner, camera raised — photographing, always photographing, recording everything, the instinct of someone who cannot trust memory alone. Imogen is on the arm of the sofa, tear-tracks on her cheeks, hands clasped in her lap, and she looks at Elaine the way a child looks at a headmaster who has entered the playground: frozen, waiting, hoping the attention passes elsewhere.

Elaine surveys the room.

Her eyes move left to right, cataloguing. The police report on the coffee table. I see her register it — the slight narrowing of her eyes, the micro-pause before her gaze moves on. Aisling on the sofa with the autopsy photographs. Another micro-pause. Rowan in the corner with her camera. No pause. Imogen on the arm of the sofa, wet-faced. A pause here — not assessment. Something else. The expression of a woman looking at damage she may have caused. Then she looks at me. Standing in the doorway between the hall and the room. Neither in nor out.

She walks to the armchair by the fireplace.

Not any armchair. The best armchair. The one with the high back and the wide seat and the position facing the door. The power position. She sits down slowly, one hand on the armrest for support. She is frail. The coat pools around her like water around a stone. But when she settles — when her back touches the chair and her hands rest on the arms and her chin comes up — the room shifts. The gravity changes. She is sitting in the chair the way a judge sits on the bench. This was her house. I was her child. These were the girls who slept under her roof. This was the room where she controlled every narrative that passed through it. She is sitting in her chair and the chair remembers her.

"I see you've been busy," she says.

Her voice is even. Mild, almost. The voice she used when I came home late from a party in Year 10. The voice she used when she found out I'd missed a piano recital. The voice that says I am not angry. I am disappointed. And disappointment is a more effective weapon than anger because you cannot argue with it.

She looks at the police report on the table. "The original incident report. From Sorrento station. You've done your homework, Saskia."

Saskia doesn't respond. Her face is a mask of professional neutrality but I can see the tension in her jaw, the way her fingers press into her crossed arms. She is watching Elaine the way she watches a hostile witness — looking for the crack, the inconsistency, the opening.

Elaine's gaze moves to Aisling. "And you," she says. "You're not Cate Morrow."

It's not a question. She knew. Of course she knew. The anonymous tips Aisling described — the photographs, the autopsy details, the name Elaine Cassidy — they came from her. She fed the investigation. She fed Aisling. She has been orchestrating this entire weekend from a bed in a hospice with the same precision she used to build the cover-up.

"You're Aisling Doyle," Elaine says. "Kiera's sister."

Aisling doesn't confirm or deny. She stares at Elaine with a flat, hard intensity I've never seen on Cate's face. This is the real face. The face behind the performance. The face of a woman who was eight years old when her sister didn't come home.

"I knew your mother," Elaine says. "Margaret. She was a kind woman."

The room contracts. Aisling's hands tighten on the photographs. Imogen makes a small sound — not a word, a breath, a sharp intake that might be the beginning of something she can't finish. Rowan lowers her camera. She doesn't photograph this.

Elaine's gaze moves to Imogen. "Imogen. You look well." The way you'd greet a colleague at a conference. The way you'd greet someone whose name you remember but whose relevance you've outgrown. Then: "Rowan. Still taking photographs, I see."

The casualness of it. The way she names each of them, places each of them, assesses each of them in a single glance. She is conducting the room the way she conducted school assemblies — from the front, from the chair, from the position of authority. She is dying and she is gaunt and her coat hangs off her and she is still the most powerful person in this room.

Then she looks at me.

"Neve, sweetheart. Come sit down."

Sweetheart again. The word is a hand on my face. The word is the hallway. The cream walls. The blue carpet. The warm December air. The word is twenty-five years of daily phone calls and every time I said "I think I remember—" and she said "You don't, sweetheart. That's just a dream." The word is the wall at the back of my mind that she built and made me think was mine.

I don't sit.

I stand in the doorway and I look at my mother sitting in the armchair by the fireplace, in the house where she pushed a twelve-year-old girl through a window, and I feel everything. Terror. Rage. Recognition. Love. Not in sequence. All at once. The feelings don't queue. They arrive together, shoving through the same door, and I cannot separate them because she never gave me a world where they were separable. She is the woman who held me when I cried. She is the woman who made me forget why I was crying. My mother sat in the waiting room at the psychologist's and controlled the narrative. My mother held my hand at Kiera's funeral. My mother packed our house in six weeks and moved me to a new school where no one had heard of Sorrento or sleepovers or windows. My mother called me every day for twenty-five years to check that the wall was holding. My mother loves me. My mother used me. Both. Always both.

She is looking at me with such tenderness. Such absolute, genuine tenderness. And it is real. I have felt it my whole life. All of it love. And all of it built on top of — built to contain — built to hold together — a lie that reshaped my mind and stole my memory and made me doubt my own eyes for a quarter of a century.

"Sit down, sweetheart," she says again. "You're shaking. You need to rest."

I sit. On the sofa. Next to Aisling. The autopsy photographs are between us. I don't look at them. I look at my mother.

She looks around the room one more time. She sees everything — the police report, the photographs, Rowan's camera, Imogen's tears, Aisling's rigid jaw, Saskia's crossed arms, my face. Whatever my face is showing. Whatever is leaking through the cracks in the wall I can no longer maintain.

She folds her hands in her lap. The gesture is precise. Composed. A woman settling in for a long conversation she has been preparing for.

"I think," Elaine says, "it's time we all had an honest conversation. Don't you?"

Elaine looks at me, and for a moment — just a moment — I see her the way I saw her in the hallway. Standing over me. Her hands on my face. Telling me what I didn't see.

---

# THE FOURTH STEP — Chapter 24: Saskia

## The Confession

Elaine Cassidy is the most composed person in this room.

I note this the way I note the posture of a witness in the box. The set of the shoulders. The angle of the chin. The placement of the hands — folded in her lap, fingers interlocked, left thumb over right. A grounding gesture. Self-soothing. Also deliberate. The hands are visible. They say: I have nothing to hide.

She is dying. This is evident. The coat, the thinness, the careful way she lowered herself into the chair, the slight tremor in her left hand that she is controlling by pressing her thumb against her index finger. She has driven forty minutes from a hospice bed to sit in a room with five women who have been circling the truth for hours.

Most dying people want comfort. Elaine Cassidy wants control. She is running the last meeting of her life and she intends to chair it.

The room is arranged around her. Imogen on the sofa, tearful, hugging herself. Rowan in the corner, camera lowered, watching with the unblinking attention of someone who registers light and shadow before meaning. Aisling — not Cate, Aisling, Kiera Doyle's sister, the revelation still settling into the room like a rearrangement of furniture — seated with the autopsy photographs in her lap, her face hard and still. Neve on the other end of the sofa, her body angled away from her mother, her hands pressed flat against her thighs. Me, by the mantelpiece, arms crossed, the police report on the coffee table between us like an exhibit entered into evidence.

And Elaine in the armchair. The best armchair. The one facing the door.

She chose that chair. She walked in, assessed the room in one sweep, and selected the position with the best sightline, the highest back, the widest view of every face and every exit. Four seconds. She has been doing this for sixty-eight years.

"I think it's time we were all honest about what happened that night," she says.

Aisling speaks first. Of course she does. She has been waiting for this moment longer than any of us.

"You know what happened that night."

Her voice is harder than Cate's voice. The accent has shifted — less London overlay, more Australian. The vowels are flatter. The journalist's measured cadence has been replaced by something rawer. Grief compressed into fury over two and a half decades, now directed at the woman who was in the room when her sister died.

"I know some of what happened," Elaine says. "I was there."

Three words. I was there. An admission dressed as a statement of fact. She was the adult in the house. We all knew she was there. But hearing her say it, in this room, in this house, is different from inferring it. An admission spoken in a room is a thing that cannot be unspoken. She has just placed herself at the scene. On the record.

She is speaking to the room. Not to any one of us. To all of us. Her voice is clear and measured and unhurried. The voice of a woman who has had time to prepare. The voice of a woman who has rehearsed.

"I had a headache," she says. "I went upstairs to lie down. The girls were downstairs — watching a film, I think. Or playing games. I could hear them from the bedroom. Laughing."

She pauses. The pause is calculated. One point five seconds. A beat for the image to land. The happy girls. The laughing. The innocence. She is setting a scene, and the scene is peace. The scene says: everything was fine until it wasn't. Everything was normal.

"Kiera came upstairs."

Another pause. This one longer. Her gaze moves to Aisling — held for two seconds, no more — then returns to the centre of the room.

"She came into the bedroom. She startled me. I'd been lying down and I got up quickly and — there was a struggle."

Not a fight. A struggle. The word choice is precise. A fight implies two willing participants with intent. A struggle implies bodies in proximity, contact without agency. A struggle is something that happens to you. Something you didn't choose. The passive construction of violence.

"Not a fight," she says, confirming my analysis in real time. "A startled reaction. She came up behind me and I turned and — she stumbled backward. The window was open. It was summer. December. The window was always open at night in summer. She stumbled and she — fell. The window was right there and she — it was an accident. A terrible, terrible accident."

Her voice breaks on "terrible."

The break is slight. A catch in the throat. Contained. Measured. Just enough fracture to signal emotion without losing composure. I have watched defendants manufacture this exact vocal break in the witness box. I have watched lawyers coach it. The technique is straightforward: think of something genuinely sad, let the emotion touch the vocal cords, then recover before it becomes uncontrolled. Elaine breaks and recovers in the space of a single breath. The precision is remarkable.

"It was an accident," she says again.

Repetition. A closing technique. Say it once to introduce. Say it twice to establish. The room hears "accident" the second time and it sounds less like a claim and more like a fact. She is not arguing. She is establishing.

She pauses. She looks at her hands. She looks at them for a long time — four seconds, five — and when she looks up, her eyes are wet.

Not overflowing. Not streaming. Wet. Glistening. The eyes of a woman holding back tears because holding back tears is what strong women do. The performance of restraint. Withholding emotion as a display of emotion. I have seen this in court more times than I can count. The jury always responds to it. The suppressed tear is more persuasive than the flowing one because it suggests depth beneath the surface. It suggests a woman who would cry if she could but is choosing not to, for your sake, for the room's sake.

"I have lived with this every single day," she says. Her voice is lower now. Rougher. The voice of a woman who has been alone with something terrible. "I should have said something sooner. I should have told someone. I was afraid."

She pauses. Her gaze finds Neve.

"I was afraid of what it would do to you."

Neve doesn't move. She is sitting on the sofa and her face is still and her hands are flat on her thighs and she is watching her mother with an expression I cannot categorize. Not anger. Not grief. Not confusion. Something harder to name. The expression of a person watching a performance and recognizing it as a performance but unable to look away because the performer is her mother.

"I told myself you were better off not knowing," Elaine continues. "I told myself you were young and you'd forget and that by the time you were old enough to remember, it wouldn't matter anymore. The police ruled it an accident. The case was closed. I thought — I thought if I said something, it would only hurt you. It would only reopen something that was already healing."

Healing. The word sits in the room like a stone on a table. Healing. As if Neve was healing. As if the panic attacks and the migraines and the chronic neck pain and the daily phone calls and the life arranged within a twenty-minute radius of her mother were healing. As if a gaslit mind can heal from a wound it has been prevented from identifying.

Elaine reaches for Neve's hand.

The movement is slow. Deliberate. A mother reaching for her daughter across the space between the armchair and the sofa. Her fingers extend. Her palm opens. She is asking for contact. Offering comfort. The gesture says: I am here. I am yours. We are together in this. Whatever happened, we face it together.

Neve's hand twitches. Her fingers move — toward her mother, then stop. Suspended in the air. The reach uncompleted. The hand hangs between them like a question neither of them has answered.

"I was trying to protect you, sweetheart," Elaine says.

Sweetheart. The word again. The word she uses the way a surgeon uses a scalpel — with precision, with purpose, in exactly the right place. The diminutive that closes the distance between speaker and spoken-to and then uses that closed distance to direct the emotional trajectory of the room. She is not just speaking to Neve. She is speaking through Neve. She is using Neve's presence, Neve's hand, Neve's response, to make the room see a mother and a daughter, not a killer and a witness.

The room is quiet.

I watch her face. The tears are present but contained — two bright crescents along her lower lashes, not falling. Her hand is still extended toward Neve. Her voice has recovered its steadiness. She has delivered a coherent, emotionally modulated account of a traumatic event in which she is the central figure, and she has done it with the precision of a woman reading from a script she has been writing and rewriting for a quarter of a century.

"Stumbled," she says. Not "was pushed." I note the verb. "Startled reaction," not "I grabbed her." I note the frame. "The window was open." Circumstance, not agency. "Accident," said twice. Framing, not fact. Every word is chosen to minimize. Every word is technically defensible.

This is not a confession. This is a performance.

And a skilled one. If she were in the box, I would dismantle it piece by piece. The startled reaction: how does a startled reaction produce bilateral grip-pattern bruising on the shoulders? The stumble: how does a stumble send a body two metres from the building? The open window: why was a window in an upstairs hallway open wide enough for a child to fall through? The accident: how does an accident produce fingernail scratches on the frame consistent with a child gripping for purchase?

I would take each word apart in cross-examination. I would make the jury see the distance between "stumbled" and "was pushed." I would make them see that "accident" is a conclusion, not evidence. I would hold up the autopsy photographs and point to the bruising and say: show me how a fall produces this pattern. Show me how a child stumbling backward generates enough horizontal force to land two metres from the wall. Show me how an accident leaves fingernail marks in wood.

But I am not in court. I am in a living room in Sorrento at half past ten on a Saturday night in July. And the witness is a dying woman in a too-large coat, reaching for her daughter's hand, her eyes bright with tears that may or may not be genuine.

I note my own response with the detachment I bring to everything. Grudging respect. She is formidable. Even dying. Even diminished. The mind behind those steady brown eyes built a cover-up that survived twenty-five years, a police investigation, a coronial finding, and a family's destruction. She is sitting in a room full of evidence — the police report on the table, the autopsy photographs on Aisling's lap, Rowan's scratch photographs on someone's phone, five women who remember or have reason to suspect — and she is not flinching. She is controlling the room. She is controlling the narrative. She is, in her way, controlling us.

Neve's hand is still suspended between them.

Elaine's fingers wait. Patient. Open. The hand of a woman who has been preparing for this moment — not the confession, but the reaching. The gesture that transforms the narrative from "woman responsible for a child's death" to "mother who made a tragic mistake and spent her life shielding her daughter from it."

The transformation depends entirely on Neve's hand.

If Neve takes it, the narrative holds. Mother and daughter, united in grief, the accident behind them, the love between them intact, the truth managed and contained and rendered bearable by the bond between them. The room would see love, not manipulation. Protection, not control.

If Neve doesn't take it — the narrative fails. The extended hand becomes what it is: a strategic gesture designed to cement a performance.

I watch Neve's face. Her eyes are on her mother. Her jaw is set. Her chest rises and falls with slow, deliberate breaths. She is fighting something. The wall she built. The wall her mother helped her build. The wall that has held for twenty-five years and cracked this afternoon in the upstairs hallway when the memory came back — the full, panoramic memory of what she saw. I saw her face when she came downstairs after that. The colour hadn't returned. Her hands were shaking and she was pressing them flat against her thighs the way she's pressing them now and her eyes had the look of a person who has seen something they cannot unsee.

Her hand moves again. Toward her mother. The fingers extend.

Elaine's face softens. Genuine, I think. Or the best performance of genuine I have witnessed, and I have watched professional actors on the stand sell stories with less conviction. The softening says: yes, sweetheart, I'm here, take my hand, we'll get through this the way we've gotten through everything.

Neve's fingertips touch her mother's.

Then she pulls her hand away.

The movement is sudden. Sharp. Her hand returns to her lap and presses flat against her thigh. Her fingers splay — the same splay I saw in the hallway when she collapsed. Her body is remembering. Her body is refusing.

Elaine's hand stays extended for one second. Two. Then it withdraws. Slowly. She places it back in her lap, interlocked with the other. Her face does not change. But something behind her eyes shifts — a door closing, a calculation recalibrating, a strategy that depended on a single gesture and was just denied.

The room holds its breath.

Neve looks at her mother and says: "That's not what happened."

---

# THE FOURTH STEP — Chapter 25: Neve

## What I Remember

The room doesn't breathe. The air has gone solid — thick and close, pressing against my skin from every direction, like being inside a lung that has stopped mid-inhale.

My mother's hand is still extended. Still open. Still waiting. It hangs in the air between us, the fingers curved slightly inward, the palm upturned, and I am looking at that hand and I am seeing it in two places at once. Here, in the living room, reaching for me across the gap between the armchair and the sofa. And there. In the hallway. Twenty-five years ago. On Kiera's shoulder.

I pull my hand back further. Press it flat against my thigh. Both hands now. Both pressed down, fingers spread, holding me to the sofa the way anchor bolts hold a house to its foundation. Because what is rising inside me is not something I can name with the words I've been given. It is not sadness. It is not anger. It is not grief. It is all of those things and none of them. It is the thing that has been pressing against the wall at the back of my mind for twenty-five years, and the wall is gone, and the thing is loose, and it is enormous.

I open my mouth.

"I remember."

Two words. My voice doesn't sound like mine. It sounds like something dragged from deep water. Low. Broken at the edges. A voice that hasn't been used for the thing it's now doing.

Elaine's hand withdraws. Slowly. She places it in her lap. Her face doesn't change. But I see it — the thing behind her eyes. The door that closed a moment ago opens a crack. Not confusion. Not hurt. Calculation. She is watching me the way she watches everything: assessing, measuring, deciding how to respond.

"I remember what happened," I say.

"Neve —"

"I was not asleep."

The words cut across hers. Sharp. Final. The first time in my life I have interrupted my mother. The first time I have spoken over her. The sound of my own voice doing it is foreign. Startling. Like hearing a door that has never been opened suddenly swing wide.

I stand up. I don't decide to stand. My body does it. The same way it climbed the stairs. The same way it walked the hallway. My body is done waiting for permission.

"I was not asleep," I say again. "I was not downstairs. I was not on the couch. I was not any of the places you told me I was."

Elaine's chin comes up. A millimetre. The movement of a woman bracing. "You were twelve, sweetheart. You were so little. You were —"

"I was standing at the top of the stairs."

The room goes silent. Not quiet. Silent. The kind of silence that has a sound — a high, thin ringing in the ears, the sound of air pressure dropping, the sound that happens in the second after a bomb goes off and before anyone remembers how to scream.

Saskia's arms uncross. Aisling's hands tighten on the photographs. Imogen's breath stops — I hear it stop, a small hitch in her chest. Rowan's camera clicks. Once. The shutter sound very loud in the silence.

"I was standing at the top of the stairs," I say again, and now I am not speaking to my mother. I am speaking to the room. To all of them. To Imogen who was my friend before she was afraid and Rowan who saw things she couldn't name and Saskia who carried the truth like a stone in her pocket and Aisling who lost her sister before she was old enough to understand what losing meant. I am speaking to all of them and I am saying the thing I have never said. The thing that was taken from me and buried and paved over and I have been walking on top of it for twenty-five years without knowing it was there.

"I came upstairs. I heard something — a voice. Sharp. A woman's voice. Not one of us. Not one of the girls. A grown woman's voice from the top of the house. And I got up from the couch and I climbed the stairs. The fourth step groaned. I remember the sound. I have always remembered the sound."

I am shaking. My whole body is shaking and I don't try to stop it. I don't press my hands flat. I don't breathe slowly. I don't do any of the things I have taught my clients to do. I let the shaking happen because the shaking is the truth and the truth has been locked up long enough and the lock has broken and the door is swinging open and there is no going back through it.

"The hallway was cream. The carpet was blue. The window at the end was open. December warm. Salt air. The moon was full. I could see everything."

I close my eyes. Not to hide. To see.

"There were two figures at the end of the hallway. By the window. One was tall. A woman. Her back was to me. Her hand was on Kiera's shoulder."

I open my eyes and look at my mother.

"Your hand. On her shoulder."

Elaine doesn't move. She is very still in the armchair. Her hands are folded in her lap. Her face is composed. But her eyes — her eyes are bright. Not with tears. With something else. Alertness. The look of a woman watching a structure she built begin to crack.

"I saw your arm move," I say. "One movement. A push. Your palm flat against her shoulder. I saw the force go through your arm into her body. I saw her stumble backward. One step. Two. I saw her arms come up and her hands reach for the window frame."

My voice breaks. Not the way Elaine's broke — controlled, measured, a performance of fracture. My voice breaks because it is breaking. Because the words are made of glass and they are cutting me on the way out.

"I saw her fingernails. She'd painted them. Blue polish. Chipped. She'd done all our nails earlier that evening. I saw her fingers spread. I saw her fingernails catch the paint on the frame. Five lines. Downward. She was holding on. She was trying to hold on."

I stop.

The room is waiting. Everyone is waiting. The air itself is waiting, suspended, holding its breath the way your lungs lock when you are standing at the edge of something and you know the fall is coming and you can't stop it.

"And then she was gone."

The words come out as a whisper. I don't choose the softness — the words are too heavy for volume. They fall out of me, weighted and final.

"She went through the window. One second she was there — her fingers on the frame, her nails in the paint — and the next she wasn't. The window was empty. The frame was empty. The air where she had been was empty. And there was a sound from outside. From the garden. A wet sound. A heavy sound. The sound of a body hitting the ground."

I am crying. I don't know when I started. The tears are running down my face and I am not wiping them away. I am not managing them. I am not performing restraint for the benefit of anyone in this room. I am standing in a living room in Sorrento and I am crying for Kiera Doyle, who was twelve years old, who was my friend, who fell from a window and died, and I am crying for myself, who saw it happen and was told it didn't. The tears are hot and they are fast and they taste like salt and they will not stop.

I look at Aisling. Aisling, who was eight when her sister didn't come home. Aisling, who spent her whole life looking for the person who took her sister. I look at her and I say: "I saw her fall. I saw your sister fall. And I am sorry. I am so sorry I didn't say anything. I am so sorry I didn't remember. I am so sorry I let her lie to me."

Aisling's face doesn't change. Her jaw is tight. Her eyes are hard. She doesn't nod. She doesn't forgive me. She watches me with the eyes of a woman who has waited twenty-five years for this and is going to hear every word before she decides what to do with them.

I turn back to my mother.

"And then you turned around."

My voice has changed. The crying is still happening — tears still falling, chest still tight — but there is something else underneath it now. Something harder. Something that has been growing in the space between the memory and the telling. It is fury. Clean and hot, and it is the first rage I have ever let myself feel toward my mother — because every other time the fury started to rise, the wall was there, the wall she built, the wall that said you don't feel this, you don't think this, you are confused, sweetheart, you have such an active imagination.

"You turned around and you saw me. Standing at the top of the stairs. And you crossed the hallway in three steps — you didn't run, you never run, you move fast but you never look panicked — and you knelt in front of me. You knelt down and you put your hands on my face. Your palms were warm. Your palms were steady. You held my face the way you hold it when I have a nightmare. The way you hold it when you want me to listen. The way you hold it when you are about to tell me something that you need me to believe."

I can see it. I can see it as clearly as I can see the room around me. The cream walls. The blue carpet. The empty window. My mother's face, close to mine, her thumbs on my cheekbones, her eyes steady, her voice low and calm and warm.

"And you said: 'You didn't see anything.'"

I am looking at Elaine. She is looking at me. Her face is still. Composed. But the stillness has changed. It is not the stillness of control. It is the stillness of a woman holding very, very still because if she moves, something will crack.

"You said: 'You were asleep.' You said: 'You were downstairs.' You said those words and you said them again and you said them again and you kept saying them until they replaced what I actually saw. Until the real memory was buried and your version was laid on top of it. Until I couldn't tell the difference between what I saw and what you told me I saw."

My hands are fists at my sides. My fingernails are digging into my palms. Four crescents on each hand. My own grip marks. My own evidence.

"You told me every day," I say. "You told me I was asleep. You took me to doctors and sat in the room and told them I was anxious and they believed you because you were the principal and the mother and mothers don't lie about things like that — that's the baseline assumption. That's what every clinician in the room would have assumed. You moved us. Cut me off from everyone who might have asked me what I remembered. And then you called me every day to check — I thought it was love. It was love. But it was also the wall. You were checking the wall was holding."

I stop. I am breathing hard. My chest heaves. My face is wet. My hands are shaking and they are fists and I cannot open them, the fingers locked in place as if the joints have rusted shut. The shaking is the truth and the truth has been in my body for twenty-five years and my body is done being quiet about it.

"Do you know what it's like," I say, and my voice cracks, splinters, "to be a therapist? To sit across from people every day and tell them to trust their perceptions? To tell them their memories are real? To tell them they know what they saw? And to go home every night and not be able to do any of that for myself? Because my mother told me I didn't see what I saw? Because my mother taught me that my own eyes were not to be trusted?"

I look at her. She is sitting in the armchair and her face is grey. Not composed grey. Blood-drained grey. The colour of a woman who is hearing something she did not expect to hear. She prepared for this — she prepared for the confession, for the controlled narrative, for the accident story, for the tears and the hand-reach and the "I was trying to protect you." But she did not prepare for this. She did not prepare for me to remember. She thought the wall would hold. She thought the overwrite was permanent. She thought twenty-five years of daily reinforcement had buried the girl at the top of the stairs so deep she would never climb out.

But I climbed out. This afternoon. In the hallway. I put my hand on the frame and I climbed out.

"You were confused, sweetheart. You were overtired. You'd been dreaming," she says. The words come out slowly. Carefully. The same words. The same tone. The same technique. But the ground has shifted under her and I can hear it in her voice — the slight roughness, the slight breathlessness, the slight crack in the surface of the composure. She is still performing. But the performance is fraying.

"I'm not confused," I say. "I was confused. For twenty-five years, I was confused. Because you confused me. That's what confusion is when it's manufactured — it's someone else's certainty poured into a space where your own certainty should be. You made me confused and then you told me I was confused and then you took me to doctors who confirmed I was confused and the whole time the thing I was confused about was the thing you were hiding."

I step closer. Two feet between us now. I can see the veins on the backs of her hands. I can see the pulse in her throat, thin and fast. I can see the woman who held me when I cried and taught me to fold shirts and told me to be brave and be careful and be good. The woman who was the tallest person in every room. The woman who commanded school assemblies and parent-teacher nights and the narrative of my entire life. The woman who knelt in a cream-coloured hallway and placed her warm palms on my face and spoke a lie into me so deeply I mistook it for my own memory.

"I know what I saw," I say.

My voice is quiet now. The fury is still there — it hasn't gone anywhere, it's still burning — but beneath it, beneath the rage and the grief and the shaking, there is something else. Something clean and hard and immovable. The thing I have been circling all weekend. The thing the wall was built to contain. The thing that came back this afternoon in the hallway when the cream walls and the blue carpet and the warm December air returned and I was twelve and I was standing at the top of the stairs and I saw.

"I saw you push her."

Four words. Four syllables. The simplest sentence I have ever spoken and the hardest. I saw you push her. I. Saw. You. Push. Her. Each word is a door closing. Each word is a wall coming down — not my wall, hers. The wall she built around the truth. The wall she maintained for twenty-five years with daily phone calls and clinical terms and the word "sweetheart" used as a lock.

Elaine doesn't speak. Her mouth opens and closes and no sound comes out. For the first time tonight — for the first time in my entire life — my mother has nothing to say. The woman who has had a word for every occasion. The woman who narrated my childhood so thoroughly I couldn't find my own voice inside it. The woman who told me what I saw and what I didn't see and what I dreamed and what was real and what to fear and what to forget. She is sitting in the armchair and her mouth is open and there is nothing.

The room holds its breath.

---

# THE FOURTH STEP — Chapter 26: Imogen

## The Command

I can't stop shaking.

It's okay. It's going to be okay. That's what I tell myself. That's what I've always told myself. Okay is the word I use the way other people use a life raft — constantly, desperately, because without it I would sink. Everything is okay. Everything has always been okay. THE FOURTH STEP was okay. The years after were okay. My life is okay. My children are okay. My marriage is okay. I am okay. Okay okay okay okay okay.

I am not okay.

Neve is standing in the middle of the room and she has just said — she has just said the words that I think I have been waiting to hear someone say for twenty-five years without knowing I was waiting. I saw you push her. Four words. They hang in the air like something suspended, like something that hasn't landed yet, like the moment between the glass falling off the table and the glass hitting the floor. The moment where everything is still whole and you know it's about to shatter and you can't catch it.

Elaine is sitting in the armchair and her mouth is open and nothing is coming out. I have never seen Elaine Cassidy with nothing coming out of her mouth. She was the kind of adult who always had something to say. The kind who filled every silence with purpose. The kind who entered a room and the room rearranged itself around her. When I was twelve, I thought she was the most impressive person I had ever met. A school principal. So tall. So put together. So sure of everything. I wanted to be like her when I grew up, the way you want to be like someone before you understand what being like them would cost.

I'm sitting on the arm of the sofa and my hands are in my lap and they won't stop shaking and I can't make them stop. I've tried. I've pressed them together. I've sat on them. I've held them under my thighs. They shake anyway. My body is doing something my mind has been refusing to do for twenty-five years and my body is not asking my mind's permission.

Neve sits down. Heavily. On the other end of the sofa. She looks drained. She looks like someone who has just pulled something out of herself that was never meant to come out. Her face is wet and red and her hands are fists and she is staring at the floor between her feet. Aisling hasn't moved. She is sitting next to me — next to me, Kiera's sister, Kiera's little sister, the eight-year-old at the funeral who I don't remember because I don't remember the funeral, I don't remember any of it, I only remember before and after and the after is a blank space I filled with the word "accident" the way you fill a hole with sand — and her face is hard and still and she is watching Elaine with the eyes of someone who has been waiting a very long time.

Saskia is by the mantelpiece. Arms crossed. Her face is doing that thing it does — the neutral, measured thing, the lawyer thing, the thing where she looks like she's taking notes inside her head. But her jaw is tight. Her jaw is always tight but tonight it's tighter.

Rowan is in the corner. Camera down. She's not photographing anymore. She's just watching.

The room is waiting for something. For Elaine to speak. For someone to speak. For the next thing to happen. The silence is enormous. It fills every corner. It presses against the windows. It sits on my chest like a weight and I can feel it pushing down and the pushing down is opening something in me, a door I closed a very long time ago, a door I sealed shut with the word "accident" and the word "okay" and all the other words I've used to build the wall between me and the night Kiera died.

I hear my own voice before I know I'm going to speak.

"There was — I heard a voice. I don't know how to explain it, there was just — a voice."

The room turns. Every face. Every pair of eyes. They turn toward me and I want to shrink. I want to disappear. I want to go back to my room and take another pill and close my eyes and tell myself everything is okay until I believe it the way I've always believed it.

But I don't. I sit on the arm of the sofa and I say it again, because it needs to be said, because Neve just said her piece and Saskia said hers and Aisling said hers and Rowan showed the photographs and the door is open and the truth is coming through whether I'm ready or not.

"A woman's voice," I say. "From upstairs. Sharp. Quick. Not screaming. Not panicked. Sharp the way a voice is sharp when you're telling someone to do something. When you're giving an instruction. When you're not asking — you're telling."

My hands are shaking harder. I press them between my knees. The shaking doesn't stop. It moves up my arms. Into my shoulders. My whole body is vibrating at a frequency I can't control and I think: this is what it feels like to stop lying. This is the physical sensation of a lie leaving your body. It's not relief. It's not freedom. It's a kind of violent trembling, like something being dislodged, like something stuck for a very long time finally coming unstuck.

"I was in the living room," I say. "We were all in the living room. Neve was — I thought Neve was asleep. On the couch. That's what I thought. That's what I was told. But I was awake. I was holding a mug of hot chocolate. The pink one with the chipped handle. I was holding it with both hands because the mug was warm and the night was warm too but I liked holding warm things. I always liked holding warm things."

The detail is stupid. Irrelevant. The pink mug. The chipped handle. Who cares about the mug? But I can't stop talking about it because the mug is the thing I remember most clearly — the warmth of it, the weight of it, the way my fingers fit around the curve — and the mug is the safe part of the memory and everything after the mug is not safe and I am trying to stay with the mug as long as I can.

"I heard the voice," I say. "And I knew — I knew right away — that it wasn't the television. I knew it wasn't a film. I knew it wasn't one of us. I knew it was a grown woman's voice from upstairs and I knew it wasn't supposed to be there. Because the grown woman was supposed to be asleep in her room. Because the grown woman had said goodnight and turned out the light and we were supposed to be the only ones awake. That was the deal. That was THE FOURTH STEP. The grown-ups went to bed and the girls stayed up and that was the fun of it. That was the whole point."

I'm crying now. I don't know when it started. The tears are quiet, the way my tears always are — I'm a quiet crier, I've always been a quiet crier, Greg says he never knows when I'm upset because I cry the way other people breathe, quietly, without making a show of it. The tears run down my face and I don't wipe them because my hands are still between my knees and I can't move them.

"I told myself it was the TV," I say. "For twenty-five years, I told myself it was the TV. I told myself I imagined it. I told myself it was a dream. I told myself every explanation except the one I knew was true. Because the true explanation meant it wasn't an accident. And if it wasn't an accident then everything I built my whole life on — every 'okay' and every time I told myself that the world was safe and children were protected and terrible things only happened by chance — all of that was a story I told myself to survive."

I look at Elaine. She is still in the armchair. Still still. Her face is grey and her hands are folded and she is watching me with those steady brown eyes and I think: you were in the kitchen that evening. You made us hot chocolate. You used the pink mug with the chipped handle for me because you knew it was my favourite. You heated the milk on the stove and you stirred the chocolate powder in and you handed it to me with a napkin and you said "don't stay up too late, girls" and you smiled and you went upstairs and you said goodnight and we were safe. We were supposed to be safe.

"I think — I don't know, but I think it was a command," I say.

The words come out small. Barely a whisper. But the room hears them. The room hears everything tonight.

"I think she told Kiera to do something. I think Kiera was upstairs — I don't know why, I don't know how, maybe she went to get something, maybe she heard something, maybe she just went — and Elaine was up there and something happened and Elaine told her to do something. Go back downstairs. Be quiet. Don't say anything. Something. And Kiera —"

My voice breaks.

"Kiera wouldn't. She wouldn't just do what she was told. Not if she thought something was wrong. Not if she thought someone was doing something they shouldn't be doing. She was brave. She was so brave. She was the kind of kid who would look an adult in the eye and say 'that's not fair' and mean it. She was the kind of kid who would refuse."

I'm shaking so hard now that my teeth are chattering. It's not cold. The heating is on. It's not cold at all. But my body is cold, cold from the inside, cold from the thing I've been carrying for twenty-five years that has finally cracked open and is spilling out of me.

"I think they argued," I say. "I think Elaine told her to be quiet and Kiera refused and it escalated and — and that's when — I heard the voice. Sharp. Once. Like a command. And then nothing. And then the sound."

The sound. The wet sound. The heavy sound. The sound I have been telling myself was a movie, was a dream, was the TV, was anything other than what it was: a twelve-year-old girl hitting the ground from a second-floor window.

"I heard the voice and I told myself it was nothing. I told myself it was okay. And then there was the sound and I dropped the mug — I dropped the pink mug and it broke and the hot chocolate went everywhere and I thought: that's not okay. That sound is not okay. Something is very, very wrong."

I look at Aisling. She is sitting next to me with her sister's autopsy photographs in her lap and she is watching me with a face I can't read — hard and still and something underneath the hardness, something that might be grief but might also be something sharper.

"I'm sorry," I say to her. "I should have — I know I should have. But I told myself it was the TV for twenty-five years and that's not okay. I know that's not okay."

Aisling doesn't respond. She looks at me for a long moment. Then she looks at Elaine. And when she speaks, her voice is quiet. Very quiet. The quiet of a blade being drawn slowly from its sheath.

"My sister was not the kind of person who backed down from a confrontation," Aisling says. "If someone told her to be quiet, she'd do the opposite."

---

# THE FOURTH STEP — Chapter 27: Aisling

## The Evidence

The living room of this house is eleven feet by fourteen.

I know this because I measured it. Two years ago, when I first tracked the property after it changed hands, I obtained the council renovation plans from the Mornington Peninsula Shire archives. I have the original floor plan from 1998 — the year Elaine Cassidy purchased the holiday house — and I have the renovation plan from 2020, when the new owners gutted the interior and rebuilt it. The dimensions are unchanged. The hallway is unchanged. The window position is unchanged.

I have spent ten years learning every measurable fact about this house. I have spent ten years learning every measurable fact about my sister's death. I am not here to share feelings. I am not here to share memories. I am here to present the case.

I stand up from the sofa.

The autopsy photographs are in my hands. Eight of them. I have looked at them so many times I can see them when I close my eyes — the bruising, the angles, the stillness of her small body on a metal table. I was eight when she died. I didn't see her after. My mother wouldn't let me. She said Kiera looked like she was sleeping, which is what people say when someone does not look like they are sleeping, when someone looks like what they are, which is gone. I didn't see her. But I have seen the photographs. Every week for three years. I have studied them the way I study everything — systematically, thoroughly, until the details are so familiar they feel like my own memories.

"Twenty-five years ago," I say, "the Sorrento police responded to a call at this address. A child had fallen from a second-floor window. The responding officers filed an incident report. The coroner conducted a post-mortem examination. The cause of death was recorded as 'head injuries sustained in a fall from height.' The finding was accidental death. The case was closed within seventy-two hours."

I look at Elaine. She is sitting in the armchair. Grey-faced. Mouth closed. Watching me the way a person watches a wave approaching — knowing it's going to hit, unable to stop it.

"The incident report noted minor bruising on the victim's upper back and shoulders, bilaterally. The examining officer recorded this as consistent with impact injuries from the fall. That notation — that single sentence, that throwaway assessment — is the reason I am standing in this room tonight."

I hold up the first photograph. The bruising on Kiera's back. Purple and green. Symmetrical. Two patches, one on each shoulder blade, with smaller marks above them where the fingers would have gripped if someone grabbed her from behind.

"You've seen this bruising," I say. "Saskia showed it to you. I'm not going to explain it again. I'm going to tell you what the experts said."

I spent eighteen months finding the right people. Dr. Raymond Cho, a forensic pathologist in Melbourne — twenty years with the Victorian Institute of Forensic Medicine. He looked at the bruising for forty-five seconds and said: "That's grip marking. Someone held her from behind." Dr. Nadia Sharif at the University of Adelaide, a specialist in paediatric injury biomechanics. She measured the distance between the bruise clusters and said: "The spacing is consistent with a tall adult female hand. The grip was applied from behind and above." A retired detective sergeant in Perth who looked at everything — the bruising, the distance, the scratches — and said: "This was no accident, love."

Three experts. Three states. One conclusion.

I hold up the second photograph. A wide shot. The body's position relative to the building.

"Kiera Doyle's body was found two metres from the exterior wall of this house."

I let the number sit. Two metres. I see Saskia register it — her eyes narrow, the lawyer in her cataloguing the distance. I see Imogen's face crumple. I see Rowan's camera rise and lower and rise again.

"Two metres. Not directly below the window. Not thirty centimetres from the wall, which is where a body lands when someone slips and falls straight down. Two metres. The distance requires horizontal force. The distance requires something other than gravity."

I walk to the centre of the room. I have the floor now. The room has given it to me — Neve's testimony opened the door, Imogen's account of the voice pushed it wider, and now I am walking through it with everything I have.

"I consulted with Dr. Helen Park, a biomechanical engineer at the University of New South Wales. I gave her the incident report, the autopsy findings, the photographs, the building dimensions, and the window height. I asked her to model the fall. She ran the reconstruction three times, varying the parameters — different body weights, different positions, different initial velocities."

I look at Elaine.

"A slip — a stumble backward from a standing position near the window — produces a landing distance of thirty to fifty centimetres from the wall. Gravity pulls the body straight down. The horizontal velocity is near zero because there is no horizontal force. The body falls and the body lands close to the building."

I hold up the autopsy photograph again.

"Kiera landed two metres from the building. Dr. Park's reconstruction found that to achieve a two-metre landing distance from a second-floor window, the horizontal force applied to the body must be significant. Not a bump. Not a brush. Not a startled reaction that sent a small child stumbling. A push. A deliberate, forceful push with enough energy to propel a forty-two-kilo body outward as well as downward."

My sister weighed forty-two kilos. I know this because it was in the autopsy report. She was small for her age. She was the shortest in her class. She stood on her toes when she wanted to look me in the eye and she was always on her toes, always reaching up, always trying to be taller than she was. Forty-two kilos. The weight of a loaded suitcase. The weight of a year's worth of textbooks. The weight of my sister's entire body going through a window and landing two metres from the wall.

"The force required," I say, "is roughly equivalent to a firm two-handed shove. The kind of force an adult uses when they want to move someone out of the way. Not a tap. Not a reflex. A shove. Applied to the shoulders or upper back of a child who weighed forty-two kilos and stood one hundred and forty-two centimetres tall. A child who had no chance of resisting that force. A child who had no time to brace or counterbalance. One push and she was gone."

I look at Neve. She is sitting on the sofa with her hands in fists and her face raw from crying and I can see the recognition in her eyes. She saw it. She saw the push. The force she described — the arm extending, the palm flat, the deliberate forward motion — matches the physics exactly. Her twelve-year-old eyes recorded what the biomechanical model confirmed twenty-five years later.

"My sister didn't fall," I say. "My sister was pushed with enough force to send her body two metres from the building. That is not a startled reaction. That is not an accidental stumble. That is a deliberate push."

I put the photographs down on the coffee table. Next to Saskia's police report. The evidence is accumulating. The police report with the bruising notation. The autopsy photographs with the grip marks. Rowan's photographs of the fingernail scratches on the frame. Neve's testimony of what she saw. Imogen's account of what she heard. And now the biomechanical reconstruction showing that the physics don't support anything except a push.

Five independent threads. The bruising. The distance. the scratches. The eyewitness. The auditory witness. Every thread leads to the same place. Every thread leads to the armchair where Elaine Cassidy sits with her grey face and her folded hands and her silence that is louder than any confession.

"The scratches on the window frame," I continue. "The ones Rowan photographed. Five parallel lines. Downward motion. The depth and spacing of a child's fingernails. Forensic analysis of those marks — I sent the photographs to the same expert who examined them — confirms they are consistent with someone gripping the frame with significant force. The kind of force a child would use when she was trying to hold on. When she was being pulled away. When she was fighting not to go through."

I pause. I let the evidence breathe. I have presented this case in my head a thousand times — in my apartment in Melbourne, in hotel rooms across three states, in the car on the way here, in the shower this morning. I have rehearsed every word, every gesture, every pause. But the rehearsal did not prepare me for what it feels like to say these words in this room, in this house, with my sister's body lying in the ground for twenty-five years and the woman who put her there sitting three feet away from me.

"The scratches confirm the sequence," I say. "Kiera was near the window. She was pushed. She stumbled backward. Her hands came up and she grabbed the frame. Her fingernails dug into the paint. She held on. But the force of the push was too great. Her grip failed. Her fingers slid down the wood, leaving five parallel lines. And then she was through the window and she was gone."

I look at Elaine Cassidy. She is sitting in the armchair and her face is grey and her hands are folded and she has not spoken since Neve said the words — I saw you push her — and the silence from her is louder than any words she could say.

"You said Kiera came upstairs and startled you," I say to her. "You said there was a startled reaction. You said she stumbled backward and the window was open and it was an accident. So I'm going to ask you, directly, in front of every person in this room: how does a startled reaction produce bilateral grip-pattern bruising on the shoulders? How does a stumble generate enough horizontal force to send a body two metres from a building? How does an accident leave fingernail marks in a window frame?"

Elaine's jaw tightens. I can see the muscles working beneath her skin. Her throat moves — a swallow. Her fingers press together in her lap. The left hand over the right. The grounding gesture Saskia noticed. But it's not working anymore. The grounding is failing. The composure is failing.
