For the first time tonight, the formidable woman in the armchair looks like what she is: a person who is cornered.

"These are estimates, not evidence," Elaine says. "You can't convict someone on a computer model."

Her voice is different. I hear it immediately and I know everyone in the room hears it. The steadiness is gone. The measured, steady, principal's voice that narrated the confession and reached for Neve's hand and said "sweetheart" like it was a password — that voice is gone. In its place is something thinner. Something less certain. The voice of a woman who has been controlling every room she has entered for sixty-eight years and is now in a room she cannot control.

"It was dark," she says. "She was small. These reconstructions — they're models. They're not reliable. You can make a model say anything if you adjust the parameters. They don't account for — for wind conditions, for the exact position, for —"

"The wind that night was two kilometres per hour," I say. "I checked the Bureau of Meteorology records. December fourteenth. The overnight wind speed was negligible. The parameters were not adjusted to produce a result. The parameters were taken from the incident report and the autopsy findings. Dr. Park ran the model three times with conservative estimates and all three runs produced the same conclusion: a fall from that window cannot produce a two-metre landing distance without significant horizontal force."

I lean forward. Not much. A few inches. Enough to close the distance between us.

"Someone pushed my sister. The bruising tells you how. The distance tells you how hard. The scratches tell you she fought. And every person in this room who was here that night has now testified — under their own volition, in their own words — that they saw or heard something inconsistent with an accident."

I straighten up.

"I have spent twenty-five years trying to find out who killed my sister. I followed the evidence. The bruising. The distance. The scratches. The voices. The testimonies. And every piece of evidence — every single piece — points in one direction."

I look at Elaine Cassidy.

"To the adult in the house. To the woman who was upstairs. To the woman whose hands were on my sister's shoulders."

Elaine's mouth opens. Closes. Opens again.

"The data is incomplete. You weren't there. You don't know what it was like," she says. But the words come out thin. Unconvincing. The words of a woman repeating a defence she no longer believes.

Elaine's voice wavers. For the first time tonight, she sounds like what she is: a woman who is running out of lies.

---

# THE FOURTH STEP — Chapter 28: Neve

## The Truth

The wind has picked up.

I can hear it pressing against the walls, finding every gap in the sealant, every place where the renovated house doesn't quite meet the frame of the old one. The windows rattle in their fittings like teeth in a cold jaw. The kitchen door, which doesn't latch properly, taps against the jamb — once, twice, a rhythm like a finger tapping a table, a rhythm that says I'm still here. The house groans. A long, low sound from somewhere deep in the walls, the sound of timber contracting in the cold, the sound my rational mind assigns to every noise this house makes because the alternative is that the house is responding to what is happening inside it and that alternative is a door I will not open.

Elaine is sitting in the armchair.

I have started thinking of her as Elaine. Not Mum. Not Mother. The name surfaced sometime during Aisling's forensic presentation — somewhere between "bilateral grip-pattern bruising" and "horizontal force" — and it stuck. Elaine. A name at a distance. A name that creates space. The space I need to look at the woman in the chair and see what is actually there instead of what I've been trained to see.

She is gripping the arms of the chair.

Both hands. Her knuckles are pale against the dark fabric, the blood pulled away from the surface as if her body is rationing. The grip is tight — I can see the tendons standing out on the backs of her hands, the thin skin stretched over bone like fabric over a wire frame. She is holding the chair the way a person holds a rail in rough seas — not for comfort but because letting go means going under. The poise she walked in with — the principal's posture, the measured voice, the measured tears from her version of events — is still there, but it is thinner now. A glaze over something less controlled underneath. I watched it crack when I said "That's not what happened." I watched it crack further when I told the room what I remembered — the hallway, her back, her hand on Kiera's shoulder, the push. I watched Aisling lay out the physics and the bruising and the two-metre landing distance. I watched Elaine's voice lose its steadiness.

But she hasn't broken. Not fully. Not yet.

She is still Elaine. Still composed. Still sitting in the best chair in the room with her chin up and her hands on the armrests and her eyes steady. Diminished, yes. Frail, yes. Gaunt in her too-large coat, the cancer eating through her the way termites eat through a beam — invisibly, structurally, from the inside out. The hospice forty minutes behind her. But the authority is still there, the way a building's architecture remains even after the paint peels and the windows crack. She commands this room. She commanded every room she entered since before I was born. She is sitting in a room full of evidence that dismantles everything she has built and she has not left. She has not broken.

I am standing in front of her.

I didn't plan this. I was sitting on the sofa, next to Aisling, the autopsy photographs between us. Then I was standing. The way I stood in the hallway this afternoon. The way my body leads and my mind follows and my feet know the way even when I don't. I am standing in front of Elaine Cassidy and the room is behind me and the wind is outside and the house is groaning and I have a question. My jaw is clenched so tight my molars ache. I unclench them. I breathe. The therapist trained into my bones: breathe before you speak, regulate before you engage, the calm voice is the strong voice. I am not calm. But my voice is. The voice is a tool. The voice is the one thing I control in a room where I have never controlled anything.

"What were you doing upstairs?"

The words come out steady. Clearer than I expected. The therapist's voice — the calm, measured, controlled tone I use with clients who are avoiding something. The tone that says: I am not angry. I am asking. And I will wait as long as it takes.

Elaine's eyes meet mine.

For a moment — one beat, two — she doesn't answer. The silence has weight. The room behind me is utterly still. No one breathes loudly. No one shifts. I can feel five pairs of eyes on the back of my head, on my shoulders, on my mother's face. The air is thick with anticipation and dread and something else — something like the feeling in a theatre just before the climax of a play, when the audience knows what's coming and cannot look away.

"I had a headache," Elaine says. "I went to lie down."

The same answer she gave before. The rehearsed answer. The practiced version. The narrative she built twenty-five years ago and has been maintaining ever since.

"You were on the phone," I say. "Who were you talking to?"

The room contracts. I feel it — a tightening, a drawing-in. The controlled confession didn't include a phone call. The headache version didn't include a phone call. I am asking about something she didn't account for, something she left out of the script, and the script is the only thing holding her together.

Elaine's grip on the chair arms tightens. I can see her knuckles go from pale to white.

"I wasn't on the phone."

"You were."

"I was in bed. I had a headache, Neve. I told you —"

"I heard you."

The words are out before I've examined them. They come from somewhere below language, somewhere in the body, somewhere in the twelve-year-old who stood at the top of the stairs and heard her mother's voice before she saw her mother's face. I heard you. The sentence doesn't come from the memory I recovered this afternoon — the visual memory, the panoramic memory of the push. It comes from something older. Something deeper. A sensory fragment I didn't know I carried. The sound of her voice, low and intimate and not meant for anyone else, the tone she used when she was talking to someone she cared about in a way she didn't want the world to know about.

"You were talking to someone," I say. "A man. Upstairs. In a house full of twelve-year-old girls at eleven o'clock at night. Who was he?"

Elaine's composure cracks.

It doesn't shatter. It cracks. A single fracture line running from the surface to the core. I see it in her eyes first — the steadiness wavers, not for long, a fraction of a second, but enough. Then in her jaw — a small tightening, the muscles bunching beneath thin skin. Then in her hands — the grip on the chair arms loosens, just slightly, as if the effort of holding on has become too much.

She closes her eyes. The first time she has broken eye contact with me since she sat down. She closes her eyes and her chin drops — just slightly — and for a moment she looks what she is: a dying woman who is tired.

"A colleague," she says. The performance is gone. What's left is lower. Rougher. The voice of a woman choosing to speak rather than delivering a script.

"His name was David. David Harmon. He was married. A teacher at a neighbouring school. We met at a conference two years before. It wasn't planned. It wasn't something I went looking for. It happened."

The room is silent. Not performance silence. Real silence. Five women hearing something they did not expect. Aisling, behind me, is very still. Saskia hasn't moved from the mantelpiece. Imogen's breath has gone shallow. Rowan's camera hangs at her side, forgotten.

"You were having an affair," I say.

"Yes."

The word is simple. Unadorned. No justification. No framing. Just: yes.

The wind outside surges — a gust that rattles every window, that makes the kitchen door bang against its frame, that fills the walls with the sound of air under pressure. And in the silence after the gust, I ask the question.

"What was so important that a twelve-year-old girl had to die?"

The word hangs in the air. Die. The word I have not said out loud until this moment. The word that turns the managed confession into something else entirely.

Elaine's eyes open. She looks at me. The crack in her facade has widened. I can see the structure buckling behind her eyes — twenty-five years of denial and control, splitting under a single word.

"I didn't — that's not what happened. I didn't kill anyone."

Her voice wavers.

Not controlled-uncertain. Not performed. Genuinely uncertain. The uncertainty of a woman who is no longer sure which version of the story she is telling.

"You didn't kill anyone," I say. Not a question. A platform. A place for her to stand while I pull the ground out from under her.

"I didn't. She startled me. She came upstairs and I was on the phone and she heard — she heard me talking. She was a child. Twelve years old and she heard something she shouldn't have heard and she was upset. She was angry. She said —"

Elaine stops. As if she's hit a wall. Her hands are shaking now. Visible tremors. She presses them flat against the chair arms the way I press mine flat against my thighs. The gesture is mine. I learned it from her.

From behind me, Aisling moves.

She reaches into her bag and pulls out the autopsy photographs. She crosses the room and sets them on the arm of Elaine's chair. One by one. The shoulders. The back. The grip marks — ten points of contact, bilateral, even, the width of fingers compressed into bruised skin. The landing site, measured from the building. Two metres.

"Look at them," Aisling says.

Her voice is flat. Steady. The voice of a woman who has spent twenty-five years wanting to put these photographs in front of the person responsible.

Elaine looks.

Her eyes go to the first photograph. The bruising on Kiera's back. Purple-black, twenty-five years frozen in emulsion. Her jaw tightens. She looks at the second. The shoulder marks — ten points of contact, the width of fingers, bilateral, even, grip-pattern bruising that no amount of narrative management can explain away. Her lips press together. The third. The measurement. The distance from the wall. Two metres. The physics of a push, not a fall. The evidence that turns "she stumbled" into a lie and "it was an accident" into something else entirely.

I watch her face as she looks. The composure — what's left of it — drains away. Not gradually. Like colour leaving a photograph, like saturation sliding to zero. The skin goes slack around the jaw. The careful architecture of control — the raised chin, the steady gaze, the measured breaths — collapses in on itself like a tent in a windstorm. She is looking at the photographs of the damage her hands did. The bruises her fingers left. The evidence that has been sitting in a file in a forensic pathologist's office for twenty-five years, waiting for the right person to find it.

She stares at the photographs for a long time. Five seconds. Six. The room waits. The wind presses against the glass. The house groans. And Elaine Cassidy sits in her armchair and looks at what she did, and the looking is the thing that breaks her.

"What did she say?" I ask.

Elaine's voice, when it comes, is barely above a whisper.

"She said she was going to tell."

Four words. The words that changed everything. A twelve-year-old girl standing in a hallway at eleven o'clock at night, hearing something she shouldn't have heard, and doing what brave, funny twelve-year-old girls do — refusing to keep a secret for an adult who was doing something wrong. Kiera Doyle was going to tell.

"She was going to tell everyone," Elaine says, her voice the slow-motion description of a car accident — the way witnesses describe it, the detached clinical narration of a mind that has replayed the event so many times the emotion has worn grooves into the words. "I panicked. I reached for her. I grabbed her arm. She pulled away. She was heading for the stairs and I grabbed her again. Her shoulders. I was trying to stop her. I needed a minute. I needed to think. Your father couldn't know. His wife. My career. A school principal having an affair — it would have destroyed everything. Everything I'd spent twenty years building. And she looked at me like I was — like I'd done something terrible. And she was right. I had. But I couldn't let her —"

"What happened?"

The words are quiet. Not demanding. Not angry. The quiet of a woman who already knows the answer and is giving her mother one last chance to say it.

"I pushed her."

Two words. The words she has never said. Not "she stumbled." Not "she fell." Not "there was a struggle." I pushed her. Active voice. The agent. The truth.

"I was holding her shoulders. She was pulling away. She was strong — stronger than she looked. She was fighting me. Trying to get to the stairs. And I pushed. Not toward the window. I wasn't trying to — I pushed her away from the door. Away from the stairs. I needed her to stop moving. I needed her to stay still so I could think. And the window was right there. It was open. It was summer and it was always open at night in summer. I pushed and she went backward and her hands came up and —"

Elaine's voice breaks.

Not the controlled break from before. Not the performed fracture designed to elicit sympathy. A real break. A collapse. Her face crumples — the word is exact, it crumples, the way paper crumples, the way a structure gives way when the load exceeds capacity, the way a face gives way when the face can no longer hold the shape it has been holding. Her mouth opens and closes and no sound comes out. Her eyes overflow. The tears she has been managing all night — managing for twenty-five years — are no longer manageable.

"Her hands reached for the frame," she says, her voice a wreck of itself. "I saw her fingers. I saw her fingernails catch the paint and I thought she had it. I thought she'd hold on. I thought there was time. I thought I could grab her."

"There wasn't time."

"No."

Nearly inaudible. A breath with a consonant attached.

"It was two seconds. I pushed. She went back. Her hands caught the frame. Her hands slipped. She was gone. Two seconds and everything changed. And I couldn't take it back."

She pauses. Her eyes are on the photographs still sitting on the arm of her chair. When she speaks again, her voice is different — quieter, directed not at me but at the room.

"The emails. The anonymous tips. That was me. I was dying. I wanted the truth to come out. But I wanted to control how it came out."

Her hands have left the chair arms. They are in her lap. Shaking. Not the controlled tremor of a woman managing her presentation. The shaking of a woman who has held something for twenty-five years and can no longer hold it.

The wind outside drops. For one moment, the house is quiet. No rattling. No tapping. No groaning. Just the sound of breathing. Six women in a room that has been waiting for this truth.

"And then you made me forget."

The words come out flat. Not angry. Not sad. The flatness of a statement so fundamental it doesn't need inflection. My mother killed a girl and then made me forget I saw it.

Elaine looks at me. Her face is wet. Her eyes are red. The tears run freely down the hollows of her cheeks, into the corners of her mouth. She doesn't wipe them.

"I was protecting you, sweetheart. I was always protecting you."

The words are so familiar they could be an echo. I have heard them my whole life. In the psychologists' offices. In the car on the way to the new school. In the kitchen of every house we lived in after Sorrento. The phrase that turned control into love and cover-up into care and twenty-five years of gaslighting into motherly devotion.

"You were protecting yourself."

The words land between us. No cruelty. No venom. A statement of fact. You were protecting yourself. Your reputation. Your career. Your marriage. Your freedom.

Elaine doesn't flinch. She holds my gaze. Even now. Even diminished and broken and exposed. She holds my gaze because Elaine Cassidy has always held my gaze, has never been the first to look away.

"Both."

One word. The truest word she has spoken tonight.

Both. She was protecting herself and she was protecting me. The love and the self-interest. The maternal instinct and the survival instinct. The woman who held my face in the hallway and whispered the lie into me loved me. She loved me. And she lied to me. And the lying was done in the name of love and in the name of fear and in the name of a life she was not willing to lose. Both. The word is not a defence. It is not an excuse. It is an admission of the thing I have been circling all night. She is not a monster who pretended to be a mother. She is not a mother who became a monster. She is both. She has always been both.

Silence.

The room is utterly still. The wind outside has stopped. The kitchen door has stopped tapping. The house is holding its breath. The only sound is breathing — six women breathing in a room where the air has been rearranged by a single word. Both. And I see her, for the first time, not the monster who pushed a child, but the frightened woman who made one catastrophic choice in two seconds of panic and then spent twenty-five years compounding it with every phone call and every "you were asleep, sweetheart" and every psychologist's appointment and every time she checked that the wall was still holding, like a builder returning daily to inspect a dam, knowing the water is rising, knowing the concrete is failing, and patching instead of admitting the structure was wrong from the start. The monster and the mother. The same person. Sitting in an armchair in a house she used to own, crying tears that are real, diminished by cancer and truth in equal measure.

---

# THE FOURTH STEP — Chapter 29: Neve

## Aftermath

The kitchen is the only room that doesn't carry the weight of what just happened.

I don't know why I came in here. The pull of habit, maybe. The therapist's instinct to create a neutral space. Or the simpler truth: I needed to move. I needed to put my body in motion and let the motion be purposeful and the kitchen is a room where motion has purpose. Boil water. Find mugs. Open cupboards. The rituals of hosting that I learned from Imogen, who learned them from her mother, who learned them from the same cultural script that tells women to make tea when something unbearable happens. The script is a lie — tea doesn't help. But the hands need a task while the mind catches up to the wreckage.

I fill the kettle. Set it on the base. Press the switch. The blue light comes on. The water begins to heat. Mechanical actions. They require no thought. I am grateful for them.

Through the doorway, I can see the living room. The scene is frozen. No one has moved. Elaine is in the armchair and her eyes are closed and her hands are in her lap and she is very still — the stillness of a woman who has said everything she is going to say and now has nothing left. Aisling is on the sofa, sitting exactly where she was during the forensic presentation, her face unreadable, her hands flat on her knees. The autopsy photographs are still on the arm of Elaine's chair. Saskia is by the mantelpiece, arms crossed, her jaw working — not tension, calculation. She is thinking. Saskia is always thinking. Imogen is on the far end of the sofa, her face still buried in her hands, her shoulders shaking with silent crying that has moved beyond the point of sound. Rowan is in the corner, camera in her lap, staring at the sealed window with an expression I would call emptiness if I had to name it — the kind of blankness that comes when a sensor has been overloaded and shut down.

The kettle clicks off.

I pour water into six mugs. I don't check whose mug is whose. It doesn't matter. Tea is tea. The act of carrying it matters more than the drinking. I set the mugs on the counter. I don't carry them in yet. I'm not ready to go back into that room.

My phone is on the counter. Face up. The screen dark. I pick it up and hold it the way you hold something you're about to use, and then I stand there, holding it, not using it. The screen reflects the overhead light. My face looks back at me, ghostly, transparent. I don't look like myself. I look like someone who has been through something. Which I suppose I have.

I press the contact. Liam.

It rings twice. Three times. I picture him in our kitchen in Melbourne, Lily upstairs in bed, the house quiet, his phone on the counter where he always leaves it. He picks up on the fourth ring.

"Neve? It's late. Is everything okay?"

His voice. The steady, slightly bewildered voice I married. The voice of a man who loves me and knows there are rooms in my mind he has never been allowed to enter and has stopped asking about. He stopped asking about my childhood before age thirteen years ago. He accepted that there were things I didn't discuss. He thought it was shyness. He thought it was privacy. He didn't know it was a locked room built by someone else inside my mind.

"Liam."

My voice sounds wrong. Thin. Stretched. He hears it immediately — he always hears it, the small shifts, the way I say his name when something is different.

"What's wrong? Is it your mum?"

Is it your mum. The question lands in the centre of my chest and I almost laugh. Almost. Is it my mum. Yes. It is entirely my mum. My mum who pushed a twelve-year-old girl through a window and then spent twenty-five years making me forget I saw it. My mum who drove forty minutes from a hospice to sit in an armchair and try to control the narrative one last time. My mum who just admitted everything. My mum who is crying in the next room and whose tears are real.

"I need you to — can you tell Lily that I love her?"

A pause. The pause of a man hearing something in his wife's voice that he has never heard before. Not fear. Not sadness. Something more fundamental. A shift in the ground beneath the words.

"Neve, what's going on?"

"I need you to tell her that I love her. I need you to tell her that her grandmother —"

I stop.

The sentence won't finish. It sits on my tongue, half-formed. Her grandmother. The woman who will hold Lily and kiss Lily's cheek and buy her birthday presents and tell her she's smart and beautiful and brave. The woman who will do all of those things genuinely and with love because that is who Elaine is — who she also is. The woman who will love my daughter the way she loved me. The woman who held my face in a hallway and whispered a lie into me and then held me when I had nightmares about the lie she whispered.

I cannot tell Liam about his mother-in-law tonight. I cannot form the sentence. The words exist but the passage between knowing and saying is blocked.

"I need to tell her I'll be home soon," I say. "That's all. I'll be home soon."

"Do you want me to come? I can get a sitter. I can drive down tonight."

"No."

"Neve —"

"I'll explain — when I'm home. I just need you to tell Lily I love her. Can you do that?"

"Of course."

"Thank you."

"Neve?"

"Yeah?"

"I love you."

The words are so simple. Three words. No architecture behind them. No manipulation. No twenty-five-year strategy. Just a man in a kitchen in Melbourne telling his wife he loves her because it's late and she sounds wrong and he doesn't know what else to do.

"I love you too," I say, and I mean it the way I have never meant anything, and I hang up before my voice breaks, because if it breaks now it won't stop and there are things that need to happen first.

I set the phone down. Pick up two mugs. Carry them into the living room.

Aisling takes one without looking up. Her hands close around it automatically. She has spent twenty-five years investigating her sister's death, ten years as a journalist, two years posing as someone else, and now she has the confirmation she came for. The truth is out. The woman in the chair did it. The evidence aligns. The confession is complete. I don't know what Aisling is feeling. I'm not sure Aisling knows. She structured her entire identity around the investigation. The investigation is over. What's left when the thing that defined you is finished? The grief underneath. The grief she never processed because she turned it into a case file instead.

I set a mug beside Saskia. She nods — a small, precise acknowledgment — and wraps her hands around it. Her eyes are moving. I can see the calculation: evidence, witnesses, confession, admissibility, statute of limitations, coronial reinvestigation. She is building a case. The structure is how she processes. She became a lawyer for the same reason Aisling became a journalist. The same origin. Different paths. Both arriving here.

I bring a mug to Imogen. She takes it and her hands are shaking so badly the tea sloshes over the rim and burns her fingers and she doesn't react. She is somewhere else. Not dissociating — I would know dissociation. She is too close. The minimization has collapsed and underneath it is twenty-five years of grief she was never allowed to feel.

"I'm sorry," she whispers.

I don't know who she's talking to. Me. Aisling. Kiera. Herself.

"I'm sorry I didn't say anything. I knew. I think I always knew. I heard the voice and I knew it wasn't the TV and I was twelve and scared and I told myself it was the TV because that was easier. And then it was too late to unsay it."

"That wasn't yours to carry."

"I should have —"

"You were twelve. The adults told you what to think and you thought it. That's being twelve."

She nods. But the nod is automatic. The grief is still there, pressing against the walls of the moment. She will need to feel it later. When the shock wears off and the guilt rises and the twenty-five years of "it was an accident" come crashing down. But not now. Now the rule is: stay upright. Keep breathing. Hold the mug.

Rowan hasn't moved.

She is in the corner, camera in her lap, staring at the sealed window. Her face is expressionless. Not composed — empty. The way a face looks when the thing behind it has been used up. She has been crying on and off all evening. Now the tears have stopped and what's left is a stillness that is worse than tears. I set a mug beside her. She doesn't reach for it. She stares at the window and I think: she is seeing what she saw. The hand. Kiera's hand. The fingernails scraping paint. The fingers splaying, grasping, losing grip. The fragments she's carried for twenty-five years — cold, light, sound, silhouette — now assembled into a complete picture by the truth Elaine just spoke.

Elaine is still in the armchair.

Her eyes are closed. Her breathing is slow and audible — a slight rattle in the chest, the kind cancer makes when it reaches the lungs. She has been sitting there for — I don't know how long. Time has become unreliable. What matters is: she hasn't left. She hasn't asked to leave. The truth is in the room with all of us now, shared, known, and it cannot be taken back.

I stand apart from the group.

My body has taken me to a place by the wall, near the doorway, between the living room and the kitchen. A liminal space. I lean against the wall. The plaster is cool through my shirt. I press my shoulder blades into it.

Two truths.

My mother is a killer.

My mother is my mother.

I hold them simultaneously. The way you hold two heavy things in both hands. Not choosing between them. Not dropping either. Just holding. The weight of each. The weight of both. The woman who pushed a child through a window is the woman who taught me to ride a bicycle. The woman who gaslit me for twenty-five years is the woman who sat up with me all night when I had my first migraine at thirteen, pressing a cold cloth to my forehead, stroking my hair, saying "it's all right, sweetheart, I've got you." The woman who just admitted to killing Kiera Doyle is the woman who drove forty minutes from a hospice because she knew the truth was coming and wanted to be here when it arrived. She wanted to control it. She wanted to manage it. She wanted to hold my face one more time and tell me what I saw.

And she couldn't.

The wall held for twenty-five years. Through a police investigation, a coronial finding, a funeral, a house sale, a relocation, three psychologists, two decades of my life. She held it together from a hospice bed, sending anonymous emails, orchestrating a reunion, arranging the evidence so it would surface in this room on this night. The architecture was magnificent in its way. A masterwork of maternal manipulation built on the simple insight that a child will believe whatever a loving parent insists is true. She held it together when she walked through the front door. She held it together through the managed confession — the headache, the startled reaction, the accident. She held it together until I asked about the phone call.

Until the script failed.

Until both.

The room is quiet. The wind has not returned. The house is still. The clock on the mantelpiece ticks. The kitchen tap drips.

And then, from the next room, a sound.

A crash.

Not the wind. Not the house settling. Not a door or a window or a pipe. A different sound. The sound of something heavy hitting the floor. The sound of wood striking wood — a chair tipping, an arm hitting a side table. The sound of a body falling.

Elaine.

I am moving before I know I'm moving. Through the doorway. Into the living room. The armchair is empty. The coat is draped over the arm like a shed skin. And on the floor, between the armchair and the coffee table, Elaine is lying on her side. Her eyes are closed. Her face is grey — not the grey of composure, the grey of blood leaving the surface. Her lips are slightly parted. Her left arm is bent underneath her at an angle that doesn't look right.

"Elaine."

I am on my knees. I don't remember kneeling. My hand is on her shoulder — the thin shoulder, the bone sharp under the fabric. Her skin is cold. Not cool. Cold.

"Mum."

The word comes from somewhere deeper than language. From the place where the twelve-year-old still lives, the girl who watched her mother push her friend through a window and then believed her mother when she said it didn't happen. From the place where "sweetheart" still means love even though it also means control. From the place where the monster and the mother are the same person and that person is on the floor and she is not moving.

"Mum."

She doesn't respond.

The room erupts behind me. Saskia is already on her phone — 000, ambulance, Sorrento, the address spoken with the precision of a woman who gives addresses for a living. Imogen standing with her hands over her mouth. Rowan risen from her corner, camera forgotten, dark eyes fixed on Elaine's still form. Aisling beside me — when did she get there? — her hand reaching for Elaine's wrist, two fingers pressing below the thumb, checking for a pulse.

"She's breathing," Aisling says. "Faint. She's breathing."

I hold my mother's shoulder. The bone under my palm. The cold skin. The too-large coat pooled on the chair behind her, empty, the shape of her still pressed into the fabric.

The woman who held my face in a hallway. The woman who told me I was asleep. The woman who loved me and controlled me and lied to me and protected me and pushed a twelve-year-old girl through a window and couldn't take it back.

The monster and the mother. The same person. On the floor. Not moving.

And I am on my knees beside her, holding on.

---

# THE FOURTH STEP — Chapter 30: Saskia

## The Decision

The ambulance is on its way. We moved her to the dining room. Imogen and I, half-carrying, half-dragging. Elaine's legs gave out in the living room after the confession — the body finally surrendering what the mind had been holding together. Neve is in the dining room with her mother now, who is conscious but grey, her head against the back of the chair, her breathing shallow and wet. Imogen is in the kitchen, running water for no reason, filling and emptying the same glass. Rowan is sitting on the stairs with her camera in her lap. Aisling is standing at the window, watching for headlights.

I am at the kitchen table with the evidence spread in front of me.

The police report. The autopsy photographs. Rowan's photographs of the scratches on the window frame. Aisling's forensic analysis — the biomechanical report, the landing-distance calculations, the bruising-pattern comparison. Four witness statements, given tonight, in this house. Imogen's account of the voice. Rowan's account of the two figures. Neve's account of what she saw. And Elaine's confession — the controlled version and the uncontrolled one.

I arrange it in my mind the way I arrange a brief.

The standard for a coronial reinvestigation is low. The coroner needs only to be satisfied that there is new evidence not available at the time of the original finding. The autopsy photographs were never in evidence. The forensic biomechanical analysis did not exist twenty-five years ago. The witness statements were never properly taken. The scratches on the window frame were never examined.

Any one of these would be enough. All of them together constitute a compelling case. The crime scene photograph with the reflected figure at the top of the stairs — Neve, standing in the hallway, watching — was never followed up. The original attending officer noted "minor bruising" and wrote it off as impact injury from the fall. No forensic pathologist was consulted. No biomechanical analysis was performed. The investigation was perfunctory, the finding was swift, and the case was closed within seventy-two hours of a twelve-year-old girl hitting the ground.

I have spent twenty-four years trying to get someone to reopen it. And now, at one in the morning on a Sunday in July, sitting at a kitchen table in the house where it happened, I am laying out the options to a room full of women who have earned the right to hear them.

If the coroner finds homicide — and I believe the coroner will — the matter is referred to the Director of Public Prosecutions. The standard for prosecution is reasonable prospect of conviction. The evidence is strong. Elaine's own admission, given in a room full of witnesses, would be admissible. The physical evidence supports a finding of force, not accident. The witness accounts corroborate each other on the central point: Kiera was pushed.

But Elaine is dying.

This is the fact that sits beneath every legal analysis I construct. A committal hearing. A Supreme Court trial. Appeals. The fastest path from charge to verdict in a homicide matter in Victoria is eighteen months. Elaine will not be alive in eighteen months. She may not be alive in eighteen weeks.

The DPP knows this. Charging a dying woman with a twenty-five-year-old homicide — even with strong evidence — would be extraordinary. The defence would argue abuse of process. The trial would be a media spectacle. The victim's family would be dragged through cross-examination on memories a quarter of a century old. And the defendant might die before the jury returned, rendering the entire exercise moot.

Neve comes into the kitchen. Her face is drawn. She sits across from me and looks at the papers and doesn't touch them.

"Status?"

"Conscious. Asking for water. The ambulance is eight minutes out."

"Is she going to be alright?"

She means: is she going to die tonight? She means something she cannot say because saying it would make it real and she has spent her whole life managing reality by not saying the thing that would make it real. I recognize the pattern. I have seen it in witnesses. The careful avoidance of the sentence that transforms possibility into fact.

"She's stable for now," I say. "The collapse is consistent with the progression. Pain, exhaustion, dehydration. She drove forty minutes from a hospice bed to confront five women with evidence of a homicide she committed. Her body has limits."

Neve flinches at the bluntness. I don't soften it.

"What are — what are the options? I need to understand what happens now." she says.

"Option one. We take everything to the coroner. The photographs, the report, the biomechanical analysis, the witness statements, Elaine's admission. The coroner opens a reinvestigation. If the evidence supports it — and it does — the cause of death is changed from accidental to homicide. The matter is referred to the DPP."

"And then?"

"The DPP decides whether to prosecute. Given Elaine's condition, charges are unlikely. A dying defendant. A twenty-five-year-old case. Memories that defence counsel would tear apart on cross-examination. The DPP may conclude there is no reasonable prospect of conviction. But the record changes. Kiera Doyle's death certificate would read homicide, not accident. The official history is corrected. The truth is established in law, even if no one goes to prison."

"Option two."

"We get a full, recorded statement from Elaine. Not the controlled version. The real one. The phone call, the affair, the push, the gaslighting. Recorded, witnessed, preserved. If the DPP ever needs it, it exists. If Aisling publishes, it backs the reporting."

"Option three."

"We do nothing. Elaine dies. The evidence exists but is never formally presented. Aisling publishes what she has. The story is told. But the coronial finding stands. Kiera Doyle's death stays classified as accident."

The kitchen is quiet. The tap runs. Imogen turns it off. She has been listening from the doorway and I let her listen because this decision does not belong to Neve alone.

Aisling appears behind Imogen. She moves differently now — not the calibrated glide of Cate Morrow but the direct walk of a woman who no longer needs to perform ease.

"What do you want, Aisling?" I ask.

"I want the truth on the record. I want the coroner to change the finding. I want my sister's death certificate to say what actually happened."

"Even if no one is charged?"

Aisling's jaw tightens. "That's not my call."

"It's the DPP's call. And the DPP's most likely decision is not to charge."

"Then the truth is in the record and the person who did it dies without facing a jury."

"Is that enough?"

"No. But it's more than I had yesterday."

Imogen speaks from the doorway. Her voice is rough, scraped raw by hours of crying. "But what about — I mean, what about Neve? What does this do to her?"

"This isn't my decision," Neve says.

"It's partly your decision," I say. "You're the primary witness. Your testimony would be central. And Elaine is your mother. The law doesn't require your consent, but practically, ethically — you're in the room."

Neve nods. Her fingers press against the table. Her knuckles whiten.

"The evidence is sufficient," I say, summarizing because that is what I do when the room becomes too heavy for analysis. "Autopsy photographs showing grip-pattern bruising inconsistent with a fall. A police report noting bilateral shoulder bruising documented and dismissed. Biomechanical analysis demonstrating that the landing distance required horizontal force beyond what a stumble could generate. Fingernail scratches on the window frame consistent with a child gripping for purchase. Four witness statements from women who were in the house that night. Two confessions from the person responsible. If this were any other case, with any other defendant, the path would be straightforward."

"But it's not any other case," Imogen says.

"No."

The headlights sweep across the kitchen window. The ambulance. Aisling steps back. Imogen presses herself against the doorframe. Neve stands.

I stay at the table with the evidence. Someone needs to hold the structure while the emotion moves through the room like weather.

The front door opens. Two paramedics, young, efficient, with kits and a stretcher and the calm professional neutrality of people who arrive at crises and leave with bodies. They move past us into the dining room. I hear Elaine's voice — thin, directional, still commanding — giving them information. She is managing her own emergency. Of course she is.

Neve goes to the doorway. She stands watching, her back to me, her shoulders rigid, her spine straight — her mother's spine. The posture of a woman who holds herself together by holding still.

She comes back. "They want to take her to Mornington. She's stable. She's asking for me."

"Go," I say.

She hesitates. The hesitation is the whole evening compressed into two seconds — a choice between a mother who killed and a mother who loved her, between the woman who gaslit her and the woman who raised her.

"Go," I say. "We'll be here when you get back."

She nods. She goes.

The ambulance leaves. Aisling sits in the chair Neve vacated. Imogen sits nearest the door. Rowan appears in the hallway and leans against the frame. Four women in a kitchen at one in the morning.

"The coronial inquest," Aisling says. "That's the path."

"It's the most likely path to a corrected finding."

"And charges?"

"Unlikely."

Aisling nods. Her hands move on the table — opening and closing, the fingers of a woman who has been building a case for twenty-five years and is about to discover whether the case produces the thing she was chasing.

I have seen this before. The moment when the pursuit ends and the thing pursued is either delivered or it isn't. The discovery, almost universally, is that the thing pursued does not fill the space the pursuit was filling. The pursuit was the structure. The pursuit was the purpose. When the pursuit ends, what remains is grief, and grief is heavier than purpose because grief has no edges and no end.

"Justice and truth are not always the same thing," I say.

The room is quiet. Imogen is looking at her hands. Rowan is looking at the ceiling. Aisling is looking at me.

"What do you mean?" Aisling asks.

"I mean that a coronial finding of homicide is truth. It is the official, legal, documented truth that your sister was killed. It is the correction of a record that has been wrong for twenty-five years. It matters. It matters enormously. But it is not justice in the way most people mean justice. Justice means consequences. Justice means a courtroom and a verdict and a sentence. Justice means the person who did it faces the weight of what they did. You may not get that. Not because the evidence isn't strong enough, but because time and illness have moved faster than the legal system can follow."

Aisling looks at the evidence on the table. The photographs. The report. The scratch images. The biomechanical analysis. Twenty-five years of her life, reduced to papers on a kitchen surface in a rented house in Sorrento at one in the morning.

She places her hands flat on the table — palms down, fingers spread, the mirror of Neve's posture. The gesture of a woman pressing herself into the present moment because the alternative is drowning.

"I spent twenty-five years wanting to know who killed my sister," Aisling says. "Now I know. And I don't feel anything. I thought I'd feel something — a release, a surge, a gate opening. I don't feel anything at all. Just the echo of all that wanting, reverberating in an empty space where the wanting used to live."

---

# THE FOURTH STEP — Chapter 31: Neve

## The Last Conversation

The ambulance is white and bright and it smells of disinfectant and something underneath the disinfectant — the smell of a place where bodies are failing, faint but unmistakable, like the smell of rain before it arrives. My mother is on the stretcher. An oxygen mask covers her nose and mouth, fogging rhythmically with each breath. The paramedic — a woman with short blonde hair and steady hands — is taking her blood pressure on the other side. The siren is off. She's stable, they said. Non-urgent transport. My mother killed a twelve-year-old girl and covered it up for twenty-five years and the ambulance did not use its siren.

I am sitting on the bench beside the stretcher, holding her hand because there is nothing else to hold. The red glow of the equipment panel. The green pulse of the heart monitor. The white overhead light that makes my mother's skin look like paper.

She is awake. Her eyes are dark brown and steady and they are watching me the way they have always watched me — with attention, with precision, with the particular focus of a woman who tracks the people in her orbit the way a lighthouse tracks ships.

I should not be here. The therapist in me knows this. The clinical term is enmeshment. The clinical term is trauma bond. The clinical term is a lot of things that describe exactly what I am doing and none of them explain why.

I am doing it because she is my mother. And she is dying. And whatever she did, whatever she took from me — she was the first person to hold me when I was born and she will be the last person I watch die and the distance between those two facts is the distance of my entire life and it will never make sense.

She lifts her free hand. Slowly. The IV line tugs. She pulls the oxygen mask down to her chin.

"You don't have to stay, sweetheart. You should rest." she says.

Her voice is different. Not the guarded, measured voice of the armchair. Not the commanding voice of the school principal. Not even the sweetheart voice, the one that finds the crack in everything and widens it. This voice is thin. Tired. The voice of a woman who is lying on a stretcher in an ambulance at two in the morning with her organs failing and nothing left to manage.

"I want to stay," I say.

I don't know if this is true. Maybe saying it makes it true. She would know about that. She constructed an entire truth for me out of repetition and authority and a mother's face telling a child what she did and didn't see.

"Neve."

"Mum. You should save your strength."

"I need to talk." She pulls the mask further down. It hangs around her neck. Her breathing is labored without it — each breath a small effort, a slight hitch at the top, the sound of a body working hard to do something it used to do without thinking. "I need to say this while I can."

The paramedic glances over. She hears us. She hears the intensity in my mother's voice — the urgency of a woman who is running out of time and knows it. She doesn't intervene. She monitors the equipment and lets us talk.

"I loved you."

Past tense. I have heard these words thousands of times. I love you. Present tense. Past tense is different. Past tense suggests a thing that is ending, or a thing that needs to be stated in its complete form before the opportunity passes.

"I loved you," she says again. "Everything I did — every lie, every phone call, every time I told you to forget — I thought I was keeping you safe."

The ambulance hits a bump. The stretcher jolts. Her hand tightens in mine. The grip is weaker than it was. Six months ago, her grip was firm. Three months ago, it was fading. Now it is the grip of someone who is holding on because holding on is the only thing left.

"You kept me blind," I say.

The words come out before I can stop them. Not clinical. Not measured. Raw. The words of a thirty-seven-year-old woman who just discovered that her mother stole her memory and made her doubt her own eyes for a quarter of a century.

She doesn't flinch. She is beyond flinching. She is lying on a stretcher with her organs shutting down and her daughter's hand in hers and she has no performances left.

"Is that worse?" she asks.

"Yes."

The word is small and absolute and it sits between us in the ambulance the way the truth has sat between us for twenty-five years — present, unavoidable, shaping everything around it without being acknowledged.

She closes her eyes. For a moment I think she's going to stop. I think we will ride the rest of the way in silence, two women in an ambulance, one dying and one holding her hand.

She opens her eyes.

"I'm sorry."

Two words. Not the rehearsed apology from the armchair. Not the calibrated tears. This is not performed. This is a woman with nothing left to lose, saying the thing she should have said twenty-five years ago, in a voice that cracks on the first syllable and doesn't recover.

"I'm sorry I took that night from you."

The words land. Not in my chest — in my spine. In the place where the neck pain has lived for two decades. In the place where the migraines start. In the place where the wall was built, brick by brick, by a woman kneeling in a cream-coloured hallway with her hands on her twelve-year-old daughter's face, saying you didn't see anything, you were asleep, you were downstairs.

"I'm sorry I made you doubt your own eyes."

The ambulance moves through the dark. The monitor beeps. The road hums. And my mother is crying.

Not the managed tears of the armchair. These tears are messy. They run down the sides of her face, into her ears, into her hair. Her chin trembles. Her mouth opens and no sound comes out for several seconds and then the sound that comes is a thing I have never heard from her — a raw, broken, animal sound, the sound of a woman who is no longer managing anything.

I have never seen my mother cry like this. I have seen her cry at the right moments with the right amount of emotion. I have never seen her cry like someone who has lost control of the crying. The woman who controlled every room she entered is lying on a stretcher and she has lost control of her tears and the loss of control is the most honest thing I have seen from her in my entire life.

"You were right," she says. "You saw it. You were standing at the top of the stairs and you saw it. And I told you that you didn't. I told you every day. I told the doctors. I told the police. I told you so many times that you stopped believing yourself and started believing me and I knew what I was doing. I knew I was replacing your memory. I told myself it was love. But it wasn't love. Or it was love and something else at the same time. It was love and fear and control and it was the only thing I knew how to do."

I don't speak. I hold her hand and I listen and the ambulance moves through the dark and the monitor beeps and the road hums and my mother tells me the truth for the first time in twenty-five years.

"I was on the phone," she says. "Upstairs. In the hallway. I was talking to — it doesn't matter who. Someone I shouldn't have been talking to. Kiera came up the stairs. She heard me. She was twelve years old and she was bold and she was brave and she looked at me with those eyes — those dark, certain eyes — and she said she was going to tell. She said it was wrong and she was going to tell the others and I grabbed her arm. I grabbed her arm to stop her from going downstairs. I wasn't thinking. I was panicked. I grabbed her and she pulled away and the window was open and I pushed — I pushed her away from the stairs, away from the door, toward the window. I didn't mean — I didn't intend — the window was — I didn't know it was that close. I didn't know she would — it was two seconds. Two seconds. My hand on her arm and then she was — she was gone."

Her voice breaks on "gone." Not a controlled break. A fracture. A fault line running through a voice that has been smooth for sixty-eight years.

"And then you were there. Standing at the top of the stairs. In your pyjamas. The blue ones with the stars. And you looked at me and I could see that you'd seen. You were so small. You were looking at me like the world had changed shape and you didn't recognize it anymore."

I remember the pyjamas. Blue with silver stars. I haven't thought about them in twenty-five years. But she's right. I was wearing them when I followed Kiera upstairs. I was wearing them when I stood at the top of the stairs and watched my mother's hand move and a girl went through a window.

"And I told you that you didn't see it," she says. "I knelt down and I held you and I told you that you were asleep. And I kept telling you. Every nightmare. Every time you looked at me with that face — the face that knew something was wrong — I told you it was a dream. I told you your mind was playing tricks. And I took you to doctors who treated you for anxiety because I told them you were anxious. And I moved us away so you wouldn't be near anyone who might contradict what I'd told you. And I called you every day for twenty-five years to make sure the wall was holding."

Her hand is shaking in mine. Or my hand is shaking in hers. I can't tell anymore. The tremor passes between us like a current, like we're two conductors touching the same live wire.

"I thought if I controlled it — if I controlled the story, if I controlled what you remembered — I could keep you. I could keep you from knowing that your mother killed someone. I could keep you from carrying that. I could keep you."

"You kept me from carrying it. You carried it instead."

"I carried it and it crushed me. And I let it crush you anyway."

The ambulance slows. A red light, maybe. Or a turn. She closes her eyes. Her breathing is labored. I reach over and pull the mask back up over her nose and mouth and she lets me. She doesn't resist. She lets her daughter put the mask on her face the way she must have let me do things when I was a baby, before the controlling started, before the lie, before the hallway.

"I don't forgive you," I say.

The words come out and they are true and I didn't know they were true until I said them. Every version I'd composed was careful and measured and therapeutic. And then the words came out raw and the truth was: I don't forgive you. Not yet. Maybe not ever. Because twenty-five years of making me doubt my own mind is not a mistake. It is an architecture. It is a wall she built inside me and forgiveness is a word that belongs to the person on the other side of the wall, not the person who built it.

She nods. Behind the mask, her eyes are open and wet and she nods.

"I'm not asking you to," she says. The mask muffles the words. I hear them anyway.

I hold her hand. I hold it the way I've been holding it since the ambulance left the house — loosely enough that she could pull away if she wanted to, tightly enough that she knows I'm here. I don't say I forgive you. I don't say I don't. I hold her hand and the ambulance moves through the dark and the monitor beeps and the road hums and my mother breathes in and out through a mask that fogs and clears and fogs and clears.

She turns her head toward me. The mask is on her face and her eyes are above it and she looks at me with an expression I have never seen on her face before. Not composed. Not controlling. Not managing. The expression of a woman who has run out of things to manage. The expression of a woman who is looking at her daughter and seeing — not the child she controlled, not the adult she gaslit, not the therapist she trained by example to manage other people's pain while ignoring her own — but a person. A person she wronged. A person she loves. A person she cannot fix.

"I'm sorry," she says again. The mask swallows the words. I hear them through the fogging and the clearing and the beeping and the hum of the road.

I hold her hand.

The ambulance slows. The driver says something into a radio. The paramedic beside me straightens, checks the monitor, makes a note. We are here. The hospital. The lights outside the window change — streetlights, then the bright white fluorescence of a hospital loading bay, then the overhead doors of the emergency entrance rolling open.

The ambulance stops.

The back doors open.

Light floods in — harsh, white, institutional, the light of a place where people go when their bodies are failing and the people who love them follow because there is nowhere else to go. The light fills the ambulance and I squint and my mother's hand tightens in mine and the paramedic reaches for the stretcher and I am still holding Elaine Cassidy's hand.

I am still holding my mother's hand.

---

# THE FOURTH STEP — Chapter 32: Neve

## Morning

I come back to the house at seven.

The hospital stabilized her. That is the word the doctor used — stabilized. Not improved. Not recovering. Stabilized. The way you stabilize a building that is still standing but should not be. Her vitals are within acceptable range. The pain is managed. She is resting. She asked me to go home and get some sleep and I said I would and I drove back to Sorrento instead because this house is where I need to be right now and I don't know why and I have stopped requiring myself to know why.

The drive from Mornington takes twenty minutes. I did it in the grey pre-dawn, the sea a darker shade of dark on my right. The radio was off. My hands were on the wheel at ten and two, the way she taught me. I drove past the war memorial and past the newsagent and through the roundabout and I know this road the way I know the sound of the fourth step.

The house is quiet when I pull in.

Saskia's car is gone — she left for the hospital an hour ago, she texted, to review Elaine's statement with the DPP liaison. Imogen is asleep in the downstairs bedroom — I can see the shape of her through the curtain, curled on her side, her hand under her cheek, her face slack with the kind of sleep that only comes after total emotional exhaustion. Rowan left sometime in the night. A note on the kitchen counter, in her angular handwriting: "Gone to walk. Need air. Back later." Aisling is gone too — no note, no text, just absence. She does not owe this house her presence. She got what she came for. The truth. A name. The knowledge that her sister didn't fall.

I stand in the kitchen for a long time. The evidence is still on the table — the police report, the photographs, the printouts of Aisling's forensic analysis. Saskia organized them into neat stacks before she left. Saskia organizes everything. It's how she copes. The same way Imogen cooks and Rowan photographs and I analyze. We all have our architectures. The things we build to hold ourselves up when the ground shifts.

I make coffee. I stand at the counter and drink it. The kitchen window faces east and the light is changing — grey to pink to gold, the slow colour wheel of a winter dawn. I can see the garden through the window. The stone wall. The path. The lawn, winter-pale, flat, still.

I go outside.

The air is cold. Sea air. Salt and grass and the dampness of a garden that has been dark all night. I stand on the back step and breathe it in and the cold fills my lungs and the smell fills my head and for a moment — just a moment — I am twelve and it is December and the air is warm and the garden is green and everything is about to happen and hasn't happened yet.

I shake it off. The flashback is gentler now than it was yesterday. Not a flood. A ripple. The memories are coming back in pieces — not the violent, immersive panorama of the hallway, but smaller fragments, quieter. The sound of the brick under my shoes. The smell of the pizza in the living room. The way Kiera laughed — with her whole body, her head thrown back, her dark hair swinging. She had a scar on her left knee from a bicycle accident. She showed it to me that night, pulling up her pyjama leg, and she said "battle wound" and I laughed and she laughed and we were twelve and it was a sleepover and nothing bad had happened yet.

I sit on the back step. The concrete is cold. I don't go back inside. I sit and I hold my coffee and I look up.

The window.

It's smaller than it was. The renovation made it smaller — a fixed pane, aluminum frame, sealed shut. You can't open it. The window that exists now is not the window that existed then. But the position is the same. Second floor. End of the hallway. The same rectangle of glass in the same wall of the same house.

It looks different now. Not smaller — I already knew it was smaller. Different in the way a thing looks different after you know what happened behind it. It was a window. A rectangle of glass. Now it is the place where a twelve-year-old girl's fingernails scraped paint while she held on. Now it is the threshold between the hallway and the dark and the grass below.

It looks harmless. Closed. Still. A window in a wall in a house in a coastal town on a Sunday morning in July. But I know what happened behind that glass. I know what those scratches mean. I know because my mother told me, in an ambulance, at two in the morning, while I held her hand and she held mine and the monitor beeped and the truth came out of her in pieces, like something that had been locked inside a body for too long and had finally found the crack.

The door behind me opens.

I turn. Aisling is standing in the doorway. She came back. She's wearing the same clothes — jeans, dark jumper — and her face is pale and tight and her eyes are red-rimmed and she is holding something in her hands.

Kiera's camera.

The disposable camera Rowan found under the floorboard in the upstairs room. White plastic, yellow trim, the kind of camera you bought at a chemist in the late nineties for five dollars and used once and dropped off at the photo counter and forgot about. The film is still inside. Twenty-five years of undeveloped film in a plastic shell. The last photographs Kiera ever took are on that roll. The last things she saw through a viewfinder.

Aisling sits beside me on the step. She holds the camera in both hands, resting on her knees. Her thumbs trace the edges — the plastic seam, the winder, the tiny viewfinder window. She touches it the way you touch a thing that was touched by someone you loved, as if warmth could pass from plastic to skin, as if the object carries a ghost of the hand that last held it.

"I found it on the kitchen counter," she says. "Rowan must have left it out."

"The film's still inside."

"I know."

She turns it over. The counter window on the top shows a number. Eight. Eight photographs. Eight frames of film that have been waiting in the dark for a quarter of a century.

"I don't know if I should develop it," she says.

Her voice is different this morning. Softer. The hard, flat, Australian vowels of last night's unmasking have settled into something quieter. The voice of a woman who is no longer performing and no longer pursuing. The voice of a woman who has arrived at the end of a twenty-five-year investigation and is standing in the aftermath, looking at the last piece of evidence, and the evidence is not a document or a photograph or a forensic analysis. The evidence is her sister's camera. The last thing Kiera held. The last thing Kiera saw.

"I think you should develop it," I say.

She looks at me.

"You should," I say. "She took those photographs. They're the last things she saw. They're hers."

Aisling nods. She doesn't cry. She has cried enough this weekend. Or maybe she hasn't. Maybe there isn't enough crying in the world for what she's lost. A sister. A family. Twenty-five years of not knowing. But she nods and she holds the camera and we sit on the back step together — a woman whose mother killed a girl, and a woman whose sister was killed — and the morning comes up over the garden and the light touches the upstairs window and the window doesn't glow or change or transform. It's just a window. It's always been just a window. The meaning is what happened around it.

We sit for a long time. We don't talk. There is nothing left to say that hasn't been said in the last twelve hours. The evidence is on the kitchen table. The truth is known. The confession is given. The ambulance has come and gone and my mother is in a hospital bed in Mornington, and Aisling is holding her dead sister's camera, and the garden is the same garden it was twenty-five years ago, and the grass is the same grass, and the stone wall is the same stone wall, and the window is smaller but the frame is the same.

Two women connected by a dead girl. That's what we are. Kiera Doyle is the space between us. She was twelve. She was brave. She told the best ghost stories. She went upstairs because she heard something that didn't sound right and she was the kind of person who went toward the thing that didn't sound right, and the thing was my mother on the phone with someone she shouldn't have been talking to, and Kiera was brave enough to say so, and my mother was frightened enough to push her, and the window was open, and everything changed in two seconds.

Aisling stands. She puts the camera in the pocket of her jumper. "I'm going to go," she says. "I need to sit with this. Properly. Alone."

"Okay."

She looks at me. Her face is still. What remains is something quieter — the face of a woman who has spent her entire adult life in pursuit of an answer and has finally received it.

"Thank you," she says.

I don't know what she's thanking me for. For the truth. For the confirmation. For holding my mother's hand in the ambulance while she said the things Aisling needed someone to hear. For being the one who was there, the one who saw, the one who was made to forget. I don't ask. I nod.

She goes. The front door opens and closes. A car engine starts.

I sit for a while longer. Then I go inside.

The house is quiet. The kitchen is empty. Imogen is still asleep. The hallway is dim — the morning light hasn't reached it yet. I stand in the hallway and I look at the staircase.

I go up.

Fourth step. The groan. Low and resonant, the same pitch, the same vibration through my feet. My body knows the sound. My body has always known the sound. I don't stop. I keep going. Step five. Step six. The turn. Step eleven. Step twelve. Step thirteen. Step fourteen. The top.

The hallway stretches ahead. Grey walls. Grey carpet. The doors to the bedrooms, all closed. The window at the end, small and sealed, the aluminum frame catching the morning light.

I walk.

Twelve steps. The same twelve steps Rowan counted, the same twelve steps my twelve-year-old feet counted on a warm December night. The dimensions are identical. The walls are different and the carpet is different and the light is different but the space is the same space and the space knows me and I know it.

I reach the window.

I stand in front of it. The glass is cold. The aluminum frame is smooth and new. Behind the aluminum, behind the rubber seal, behind the plaster and the paint — the original frame. The wood. The scratches. Five parallel lines. Downward. The marks of a twelve-year-old girl holding on.

I put my hand on the frame.

My palm flat against the aluminum. My fingers spread. The same spread as the scratches beneath — the echo of a hand that reached for this frame twenty-five years ago and held on and lost its grip. I press my hand against the new surface and I feel the old surface beneath it. The bones of the house. The thing that doesn't change when everything else does.

I stand there for a long time. I don't cry. I don't shake. I stand with my hand on the frame and I look through the glass at the garden below and I think about Kiera Doyle, who was twelve and brave and who told the best ghost stories, and who stood where I am standing and looked through this window at the dark and heard the brass hinge creak and felt the warm December air on her face and then felt hands on her shoulders and then felt the window take her.

I take my hand away.

I walk back down the hallway. Twelve steps. The staircase. Fourth step. The groan. The ground floor. The living room, empty, the armchair where my mother sat and delivered her managed confession. The coffee table where the evidence was spread. The kitchen where I made coffee at five in the morning because making coffee is a thing you do when you don't know what else to do.

I pick up my phone from the kitchen counter. I scroll to Liam's name. I press call.

It rings twice.

"Neve? Are you okay? What time is it?"

"It's early. I'm okay."

A pause. He is sitting up in bed. I can hear the shift of the mattress, the click of the lamp. "Where are you?"

"I'm still at the house. I'm coming home. I'll be there in a couple of hours."

"Okay." Another pause. He is choosing his words the way he chooses them when he knows something is wrong but doesn't know what. "Do you want to talk about it?"

"Not yet. But I will. When I get home. I need to tell you something."

"Okay."

"I need to talk to you about my mother."

Silence. Liam has been married to me for fourteen years. He has met Elaine hundreds of times. He has watched me call her every day and arrange my life within twenty minutes of her and refuse to discuss my childhood before thirteen. He has accepted all of it with the steady, bewildered patience of a man who loves a woman he cannot fully know. He has never pushed. He has never asked why I can't talk about it. He has just waited.

"And I need to talk to you about what happened to me when I was twelve."

The silence is longer. I hear him breathe. In and out. The breathing of a man who has just been told that his wife — the woman he married, the mother of his child, the therapist who helps other people face their fears — has been carrying something for twenty-five years that she has never spoken about.

"Okay," he says. "I'll be here."

"I know."

"I love you, Neve."

"I know. I love you too."

I hang up.

I stand in the kitchen and I look through the window at the garden. The morning light is full now — gold, soft, the light of a winter day. The house is behind me. The staircase. The hallway. The window at the end. The scratches under the frame.

I walk to the car. I start the engine. I drive away.

---

# THE FOURTH STEP — Epilogue: Neve

## Three Months Later

Elaine died on a Tuesday.

Six weeks and four days after the weekend at Sorrento. She was in the hospice — the same hospice she drove herself away from on the Saturday night she arrived at the house, gaunt in her camel coat, commanding the room from the best armchair, holding the narrative together with the last of her strength. The nurses said she was lucid until the final forty-eight hours. She organized her affairs. She updated her will. She wrote letters — to colleagues, to old friends, to me. The letter to me is nine pages long, handwritten, in the precise script she used for school reports and official correspondence. The handwriting gets smaller toward the end. I have read it twice. I have not decided if I will read it again.

In the final days, she told the full, uncontrolled truth. I was there. I sat beside her bed and I held my phone below the edge of the mattress and I pressed record and she talked. Three hours and forty-seven minutes of audio. The phone call with the married colleague. Kiera coming upstairs, overhearing, confronting. The grab. The push. The trajectory. The sound she said she heard every night for twenty-five years — a thud followed by nothing. Then the gaslighting in detail. The night of the fall, kneeling in the hallway, repeating the story until my twelve-year-old face stopped showing terror and started showing confusion. The doctors. The psychologists. The move. The isolation. The systematic, methodical construction of a wall inside her daughter's mind.

She told me she loved me. She told me she was sorry. She told me these were not contradictions, even though they sounded like contradictions, even though they sat in the same sentence like two people trying to share a single chair. I held her hand — the hand that pushed, the hand that soothed, the same hand, I held it. I didn't say I forgive you. I didn't say I don't. Some things do not need to be said. Some things are still forming and will continue to form for years and the formation is the thing, not the conclusion.

The coronial inquest opened last month. Saskia presented the case — the autopsy photographs, the biomechanical analysis, the fingernail scratches, the witness statements, and Elaine's recorded statement. The finding is expected within weeks. Saskia is confident it will change the cause of death from accidental to homicide. The DPP is reviewing. Given Elaine's death, criminal charges are unlikely. There is no defendant to prosecute. But the record will be corrected. Kiera Anne Doyle's death will no longer be listed as an accident.

This is what Aisling wanted. The record. The truth, in writing, in the permanent documentation of a state. She got it. Whether it fills the space that twenty-five years of investigation carved inside her, I don't know. I don't know if anything fills a space like that. I don't know if spaces like that are meant to be filled or if they just become part of the architecture of the person who carries them.

Aisling published her investigation. A long-form article, twelve thousand words, in a national publication. She used pseudonyms for the women but asked me if she could use her own real name and mine. I said yes. The article was widely read. She told the truth with the precision and the fury and the controlled grief of a woman who spent her entire adult life learning how to tell this specific story. She got the byline she wanted. A.M. Doyle. Her sister's name next to her own in print.

David Harmon died in 2015. He never knew what his phone call cost.

I am in therapy. Real therapy — not the therapy I perform for others. EMDR. Eye Movement Desensitization and Reprocessing. The memories come back in fragments. Not the violent panorama of the hallway — that came back all at once, in the worst possible way. The other fragments. Smaller. Quieter. The sound of Kiera laughing. The smell of the pizza. The texture of the sleeping bag under my fingers. The ordinary fragments of a night that started as a sleepover and ended as a crime scene. They are coming back. Piece by piece. The wall is coming down.

I am learning to trust my own perception. Twenty-five years of being told that what I saw was not what I saw — that my mind was unreliable and my perceptions were distorted and my memories were dreams — does not repair itself in a few months. I second-guess everything. I check my perceptions against external evidence. I ask Liam if I heard what I think I heard, saw what I think I saw. He is patient. He is patient in the way he has always been patient, but now there is less bewilderment in the patience and more understanding. He knows what happened. I told him that first morning, sitting at our kitchen table while Lily slept upstairs, and he listened with his hands wrapped around a coffee mug and his face very still and he said "What do you need from me?" and I said "I don't know yet" and he said "That's fine. We'll work it out."

Lily is in therapy too. She doesn't know the details. She knows something happened. She knows her grandmother is dead. She knows her mother cries at unexpected times and that the crying is not something to be frightened of. She accepted this with the matter-of-factness of a twelve-year-old who has not yet learned what adults are capable of.

I moved. Not far — still in Melbourne. But the house that was nineteen minutes from my mother's, the house I lived in my entire married life inside the invisible fence she maintained for twenty-five years by making me believe it was my choice to live inside it — we left it. Liam found a townhouse in Collingwood. Narrow, bright, close enough to Lily's school that she can walk. The first morning, I stood in the kitchen and looked out at the small courtyard garden — brick walls, a potted lemon tree, a cracked birdbath — and felt something I could not immediately name. Space. The absence of a wall. The feeling of standing in a place that was not chosen for me or managed for me by someone who used love as a construction material, but that was simply mine.

Saskia calls once a week. She updates me on the inquest. She speaks in the precise, short sentences she has always used, and when she is done with the legal update she pauses and says "Where are you today, Neve?" and the question is not performed. It is the question of a woman who has been watching me for twenty-five years and is still watching now. I tell her I'm okay. For the first time in my life, I might mean it.

Imogen went back to Perth. She sent an email — warm, full of domestic detail. Sophie had a school concert. Ethan lost a tooth. Greg is thinking about a vegetable garden. At the end, one line: "I'm seeing someone. A therapist. I'm talking about that night. It's hard. It's the right thing."

Rowan sent a photograph. An envelope, no return address, postmarked from somewhere cold. A window in a wall, golden light, the kind of light that makes everything it touches look like it is remembering something. No note. The photograph says what Rowan has always said: light, shadow, frame, glass.

Aisling developed the film.

She called on a Thursday evening. Her voice was steady but there was something underneath — the sound of a woman who has just seen something that rearranged the inside of her chest.

Eight photographs. Kiera's photographs. The last eight things she saw through a viewfinder. THE FOURTH STEP. The girls. A pizza box. A sleeping bag. Rowan with her camera. Imogen with her busy hands. Saskia at the edge of the frame, watching. Me — twelve years old, brown hair, eyes that watched everything — mid-laugh, my head thrown back, caught in the moment before.

The last photograph is a self-portrait. Kiera's face, arm extended, camera pointed at herself. Twelve years old. Dark hair. Dark eyes. A scar on her left knee. She is smiling. She is happy. She is in a house where she is safe, with friends, on a night that hasn't ended yet, in the moment before everything changes.

Aisling said she was going to frame it. Two photographs on the wall beside her desk — Kiera running at the sports day, and Kiera smiling into her own camera. A whole life in two frames.

I think about frames sometimes. The frames we live inside. The frames people build for us. The frame of a window in Sorrento with scratches beneath the surface. The frame of a narrative that held for twenty-five years and then cracked, and through the crack, the truth came flooding in.

The house in Sorrento is still a rental. Someone else will stay there next weekend. They will not know what happened in the hallway upstairs. They will see a window — sealed, modern, harmless — and they will not think about it at all.

But I know. I carry the knowledge the way I carry the grief — not cleanly, not neatly, not in a way that resolves, but honestly. For the first time in my life, honestly. The knowledge sits in my chest with a weight I have stopped trying to redistribute. It is part of me now. The wall is gone and in the space where it stood there is room to breathe, and the breathing is uneven and sometimes it catches and sometimes it shakes, but it is mine.

The window is closed. The scratches are still there. And underneath them, the wood remembers the grip of a girl who held on with everything she had, and the frame still carries the shape of her fingers, and twenty-five years could not sand it smooth.

I am.
